THE RED PAGE.
The machinery of Words.
TUCKED unobtrusively away at the side of
the brain—leftside in most people—just
under the temple, there is a small patch of
cells and fibres upon whose activity has
depended the evolution of the human intellect
and all its works. By the mechanism of these
few square inches of tissue we hold communica-
tion with the minds of our fellows. Disorganise
or destroy this patch, and tongue and. pen
are silent, language becomes but a hissing,
clicking gibberish; the carefully-stored lean-
ing of centuries is locked up under a for-
gotten combination. A rap against a mantel-
corner, or a pin-head clot of blood swept
into its blood-supply, can reduce a Darwin to the
productive level of a piccaninny of the Cave Age.
What about the'other brain hemisphere, you ask ?
In right-handed people only the left speech area
(for the present we will include under this term
the entire mechanism) is active, for a reason to be
understood later. The corresponding area on the
other side may ultimately be educated, but it is a
lengthy process. The machinery is in duplicate
indeed, but only a single engineer is provided.
In the easy simplicity of trained brain-action,
we do not realise the extreme delicacy of the
mechanism. Speaking broadly, our power of
communication with our fellows depends on two
word-memory storehouses—one for sounds, the
other for pictures. These are connected with the
ear and the eye, and again with two sorting and
delivering agencies, where the memories of as-
sociated movements for mouth and hand, on
receiving the order, sort out and deliver what is
required in speech dr writing. Put into scientific
terms we have then (1) an auditory word memory
centre, and (2) a visual one ; (3) a glosso-kinaes-
thetic centre,, and (4) a cheiro-kinsesthetic one;
with intercommunicating nerve-tracts, connecting
all up in circuit. So simple an affair does speech
“’seem, that an occasional block in any of the in-
tricate lines or centres appears an unwarrantable
personal injury; and the apparatus works so
smoothly in health that some have prophesied
that it will become automatic, the infant speaking
as soon as it is born, like the philosopher’s babe in
the Bab Ballads.
Of the two word memory centres the auditory
is inconceivably older than it's brother of the pic-
tures ; and correspondingly better educated.
We—-the speaking, writing we—are essentially
auditive. The visual centre may be highly trained,
and educated deaf-mutes using lip and finger
language depend on it altogether ; but the audi-
tory rules all who have acquired labial speech.
It is simple enough to prove. Call up, without
definite thought, a few of the derelict Words
floating at large in your mental Atlantic—the
first words which “come into your mind.” You,
listen for them, do you not ? You cock your ear
(physical reflection of mental action); your lips
and tongue automatically endeavour to perform
the associated muscular movements necessary for
their pronunciation ; and, in the action, feqns-long
of listening, speaking ancestors outweigh the
-few poor thousand years of visual writers.
Watch the working of the machine in ordinary
speech. An impression registered through the
ettr calls the auditory centre for a reply. The
selection is made, and a word message goes to the
associated movement centre for the mouth. The
machinery of lips, tongue, and larynx is set going
to the pattern furnished by memories of previous
experience—and we speak.
* * • •
Switch on another circuit, a rather more com-
plicated one. We wklnto take a note of a striking
quotation. The eye telephones the visual centre,
which calls up the auditory for the recording of
the desired words. We pick up the pen, and the
visual centre acts further, calling up the associated
movement memories for the hand in the act of
writing. The pen scratches obediently, the audi-
tory centre dictating the while and rousing up the
associated tongue movements to check the result
if necessary. (Note how often we correct;'a slip
of the pen with an admonitory whisper). The
visual centre brings „o:it the memory-picture of
written words for the guidance of the hand, and
behold ! our quotation is duly recorded.
Writing and reading have been grafted on to
speaking and hearing. The mechanism is seen
plainly enough in the agonies of certain unskilled
penmen, so nakedly auditive, who must speak
words or letters aloud before the visual centres
can be sufficiently roused up. Under excitement
the readiest writers “throw back” like this —wit-
ness Alexandre Dumas, in the days when he wrote
his own books, yelling the defiances of his supple-
wristed heroes as he set them down. I believe the
most hardened bookfellow is not above repeating,
as it forms, any phrase which stirs him—no doubt
to the scarce-veiled perturbation of the office-boy,
who sees the weekly wage evanishing into a
padded cell.
The process, then, is sufficiently simple in
health. Suppose we try the effect of breaking the
circuit here and there, just as a clot or a passing
molecular disturbance will do in their degrees.
Cut out the mouth movement memory centre—our
man is struck speechless. Lips, tongue and
larnyx are as good as eVer for other purposes ; he
can read and write ; and he expresses very visible
annoyance at the meaningless noises which have
replaced his speech. He is suffering from motor
aphasia, and were he the whple Academia Della
Crusca under his own hat, he could not ask for a
fill of tobacco.
Another experiment. Break up the visual word,
memory centre. Now your man can talk to the
top of his pitch, but he cannot read. He has
alexia ; all written or printed words have lost their
associated meanings. He can write spontaneously
in an uncertain hand, but he cannot read what he
writes. Yesterday, perhaps, he could read San-
scrit inscriptions; to-day he doesn’t know his
own signature.
Again. Destroy the auditory word centre,
where sound memories are stored. Your, man is
one of those who fled from Babel, on the plains of
Shinar. He can hear sounds as well,-as ever—as
sounds; but all spoken tongues have turned to
meaningless jargon. He is word deaf; he has lost
his mentaldictionary and phrase-book. Since the
auditory centre is the real controlling centre of
the whole area, there are other disturbances. He
can read well, but speech and writing are very de-
fective. Wrong words and syllables slip in with-
out his knowledge. The visual centres help him
somewhat through the communication, but the
governing power is gone.
• * * *
Now for the scribes. Cut out the hand memory
movement centre and our victim is straightway
in a condition of agraphia. Hand and arm are as
snpple and strong as ever ; he can read and speak
thoroughly ; nothing is wrong except—except that
when he handles a pen only a meaningless scrawl
results in place of the clean-cut sentences which
erstwhile were the delight of editors. Complicate
this with the destruction of the auditory centre
and its connections, and had he the collective
genius of the Elizabethans he must needs be mute
of tongue and pen. A spicule of dislodged bone,
the plugging or rupture of an artery little bigger
than a hair, can reduce the productive level of a
Stephen Crane belo.w that of a Solomon Islander.
Just a stumble against a mantel-corner—think of
that as you drink tobacco ! There is hope, though,
in uncomplicated agraphia, for the left hand can
be readily-enough educated, with its governing
power.
Other disturbances incidentally crop up in
injuries about the speech area. Arnusia, an abso-
lute incapacity for distinguishing musical tone Or
rhythm, may result in similar fashion from injury
or disease. It depends on the destruction of cer-
tain storage cells closely related to the auditory
word centre. Again, a man may have his visual
centres mysteriously reversed so that he writes
backwards and can only read from the reflection
in a mirror. He may become mind-blind, so that
objects have for him no associated meaning;
he can neither recollect the use of a fork nor of
the food it accompanies. The painter forgets the
meaning and use of a brush ; the writer that of a
pen. Mind-deafness may smite him so that sounds
lose their purport, -and a cry of murder becomes as
destitute of meaning as the tinkle of a dinner-
bell, though he be both humane and hungry. It
is well thus to remind ourselves, in this Tin Grod
Age of mah, that our ideas and impressions are
poor, uncertain things depending on the accurate
working of a very intricate mechanism, and that
a small disturbance may make a large difference
in the picture presented to the Ego.
There are points of interest in the dry physio-
logy of the speech-centre. We naturally seek a
reason for its location on the left side in the great
majority of brains, and find it in the associated
use of the, right hand. The nerve-fibres of ope
hemisphere always supply the opposite side of
the body. Right-headness depends primarily upon
the freer blood-flow obtained by the straighten
course of the left carotid artery as compared with
the right. In the left-handed the position of the
speech centre is reversed, depending probably
(although the question has never been definitely
settled) upon some arterial peculiarity. Anropos,
I once knew a man of strange ideas who insisted
that his children should be so trained as to become
ambidextrous. His aim was that they should de-
velope, not greater dexterity, but increased facul-
ties of speech. Reasoning from anatomical pre-
mises, he argued that the increased blood-flow to
the left-hand centres of the right hemisphere
would tend to stimulate tire adjacent speech-
centres to an equal activity with those of the op-
posite-side, thereby cultivating a double fluency
of tongue. The experiment was not a success, and
as yet the double-barrelled orator cannot be grown
at will. The specific gravity of the portion of
brain corresponding to the active speech centre
is appreciably higher than that of the opposite
latent one, and this specialised area has a greater
complexity of convolutions than its uneducated
brother. The reason for the one-sided develop-
ment lies in the fact that the extreme concentra-
tion of nerve-force necessary for such complicated
processes as speech and writing is rendered pos-
sible only by the limitation of the area of activity
to the smallest possible space. Nature is no
spendthrift.
* * » *
Regarding the theory of the existence of an in-
dependent centre for ideational concepts, from
which thoughts spring fully armed like Minerva
from the brain of Jove, no definite proof is forth-
coming either way. Non-believers argue that all
thought arises in the auditory centre as a result of
external stimulation —that all thought requires a
physical foundation for its inception. The other
side premises the existence of an independent
centre capable of actually originating ideas. The
weight of anatomical argument is in the affirma-
tive scale. There are brain areas close by which
do not belong to any other body function, and
which may well be assumed as having definite
association with the speech mechanism. More-
over, these areas are undeveloped in any animal
except man, and in the infant do not take on the
characteristics of active brain-matter until a con-
siderable time after birth.
I have explained the machinery of words suffi-
ciently, perhaps, to show dimly something of its
method. Actual cases are seldom so clear as the
experimental instances we have called up, since
the destruction does not limit itself to this centre
or to that, and the phenomena must be picked
out of a jumble of symptoms by patient observa-
tion. It is a fine piece of mechanism, this brain
of man; and the Doctrine of Small Causes
dominates its working. Given but a dislodged
bone-speck entangled in the wheels, and we
become as those other sages of whom Omar sang
that—
“. . . their words to scorn
Are scattered and their Mouths are stopt with
Hust.”

POYYAKA.
Sydney Art and Artists.
Another artist talks on the burning question of
Art in Sydney— -
Of course the triple subsidy should be abolished. I
agree that the results of the Technical College classes
show a ridiculous waste of time and money. Why, my
second-month pupils show better work than was hung in
the exhibition solemnly blessed by Minister Berry. But
are the results of the Societies’ classes so very much
better? One Society gives us a wooden uniformity of
pupils’ work—the master’s heavy hand runs all through,
crushing Out individuality with his particular formula.
It isn’t education—leading out; it’s lessons driven in. My
idea of a good teacher is a man who lets his students
develop themselves —suggests and stimulates, but leaves
them free to work (heir own salvation. If they can’t
become artists of themselves, a teacher can’t pound art
into them. .
The work of the other Society’s students seems to me
plebeian, mechanical. .
And if N.S.W. Gallery trustees were entrusted with a
single Govt, subsidy for tuition, it would he the greatest
mistake in the world to put one artist, however good an
artist or efficient a teacher, in charge of all the
classes. Divide the work up among the artists, and
you’ll help the artists and profit the students. In-
stead of giving £5OO a year to one man, give £lOO a year
to five, and let the trustees or a sub-committee of them
act as honorary directors, examine the students’ work
every six months, and judge the results so as- to
pass the students from class to class. Suppose, for ex-
ample, Vander Velden were put in charge of a composition
class, Long-staff in the life-class, ahd Ashton and others
were given the nude, the antique, the draped model,
geometry and perspective; and the rest, while another
man took a preparatory class,—l know and affirm that
you would get better results than if any artist taught
everything single-handed. No local artist excels in every
branch. Why not take specialists in each, and let the
students benefit ?
I would n’t even appoint any teachers permanently—not
until after six months, anyway. Let them come as pro.
bationers, and let their teaching powers be judged by
their students’work. A committee of trustees could sit
in judgment, or four of the artists might pass the other’s
students from class to class.
The Govt, money will be wasted if the change merely
means a lucrative billet and a man to fill it—you mark
my words!
Another idea.
Here the. Gallery trustees go round the Societies’ shows
with so much to spend ; and if you don’t sell your big
picture, you’re done. ..Nobody else Js likely tp buy it.
Result : . Our artists are more and more pressed to con-
fine themselves to little sketches which do not fully
show their talents, give no scope for their best energies.
I suggest that, if the Trustees have £5OO to spend, they
spend say £4OO on pictures, and £lOO in giving medals
and small money prizes for work not purchased, but
worthy. ' That would cost little, and greatly encourage
the artists. The smallest distinction, the smallest
reward, are better than none. Besides, a picture so dis-
tinguished has a cachet of honour, and is more likely to
find a purchaser. I recommend this idea to the Gallery
trustees. f
Another idea.
When a work comes back from exhibition, no matter
how much time or pains he has spent upon it, it comes
back to face thejwall of the artist’s studio. He can send
it to a picture-dealer if he likes; but as a rule it isn’t
worthwhile. Why can’t the Gallery trustees, or Govt'.,
or the Societies, arrange a permanent exhibition, where
an artist can hang his work all the year round on the
chance of getting a. purchaser. The rent Wouldn’t be
very much —not as much as the Societies spend in keep-
ing two salons empty for the best part of the year ; and
the exhibition would be attractive to the public,
even if they only came to disparage. That could
be done in one of the Gallery annexes, or in a
building hired in town. And there are lots of returned
pictures which our country cousins and visitors, who
have n’t had the chance of seeing them - on exhibition,
would like to have a look at—maybe to bu^.
Now, these are practical ideas—good for the" public and
good for the artist, who wants all the good he can get.
And when these ideas are digested I’ll give you some
more.
Amen!

Not long ago died R.W. Bro. Henderson, a
prominent Freemason of Bend go (Vic.). In his
life R. W. Bro. Henderson’s portrait was painted
by Lon^staff; after his death it was painted by
Wor. Bro. Harry Hall, P.G.D. Follows : tha
commentary of the Freemasons’ organ ( Masonry,
Sept. 27) upon the second portrait,
The painting of the picture was entrusted to Wor. Bro.
Harry Hall, P.G.D., Eyre-street, Ballarat, and he had for
his guidance only a portrait of the deceased taken some
little tiifie before his demise. In the Art Gallery of the
city of Bendigo hangs a portrait by Longstaff on a larger
canvas, which cost the citizens some £l5O, and although
it presents, perhaps, some intrinsic values commensurate
with the greater cost, as a likeness of the deceased, and
for a natural and easy position, it offers no advantages
over that painted so successfully by Wor. Bro. Hall. . . .
Charming, isn’t it?

Mrs. Craigie’s (“John Oliver Hobbes”) new
novel, “ Robert Orange,” is heavier than “The
School for Saints,” which was 80 per cent, pure
lead. Instead of buying it, read her early books
over again, and profit both ways.
London’s spring publishing season is reported
the worst for twenty years. Pfebple read about War
in the newspapers, and didn’t buy books —nor did
publishers. The current autumn season promised
a little better, they say; but opened badly
enough.

Swinburne’s revised and collected poems, in six

Wm. E. Smythe, in “The Conquest of Arid
America,” recently published by Harper, finds
that U.S. soil and climate away from the seaboard
are producing the best crops and the best men.
The theory is that chemical plant-foods and
formed in the “weathering ” of rocks
to make soil, are washed away from the sea-
board states which lack “the inestimable blessing
of aridity.” Maybe our Australian desert, then,
will yet.prove our best heirloom from the ages.
Dr. .Ralph Worrall, of Sydney, in Austra-
lasian Medical Gazette for September, reports
the-case'of a. two-months’ ovum retained m utcro
for nine months —the patient’s health remaining
excellent all the time. At the end of nine months
she was delivered of a two-months’ child ; and Dr.
Worrall comments—
Had this woman’s husband been absent for eight dr
nine months prior to the expulsion of the ovum, there are
few medical men who, Seeing the patient then for the
first time, would not have felt convinced that illicit inter-'
course must have taken place about two mouths pre-
viously.
From a medico-legal point of view this case is thus
of great importance.
Alex. M.—
In making Boaliieea belong to “ the dark-haired Celtic
race,” “ Willewa (6/10/0), is misled by the erroneous
popular application of the designation ” to the
non-Aryan race which occupied Britain before the arrival
of the people, which subsequently opposed the Roman
invasion. The former people, the Silures (now more
commonly called by ethnologists—on account of demon-
strated affinities With certain Continental races—the
Iberians) were small, dark, dolichocephalic, buried their
dead under what are known as “ long burrows,” and are
how represented-chiefly in Wales, the Highlands, and the
West of Ireland. In Ireland and in the Highlands, in-
deed, very potable is the existence, of this type side by
side with the big, raw-boned, red-headed descendant of
the race that invaded Britain about the beginning of the
Bronze-Age—the race to which Boadicea belonged, and
to which, as spea,ldng an Aryan tongue of the group now
known as Celtic, the name of Celts most properly belongs.
And this race—which buried under “ round burrows”—
was, upon undeniable- exhumatory evidence, tall, strong,
and brachyeephalie ; while, upon equally indisputable
contemporary historical testimony (Lucan, Vitruvius,
Tacitus, &e.), it was florid and light-haired. Dion Caseius,
indeed, describes Boadicea herself, in so manj- words, as
having “ great proportions, a fierce aspect, and an abun-
darjeeof tawny hair.”

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO WOOLGROWERS
“ New South Wales and Queensland.
The Dissolution of the Sydney Wool-selling Brokers’ Association by effluxion of time,
and our Refusal to join the Wool-selling Institute, enables this Company to Revise its
Charges on the Sale of Wool by Reducing same, and also Abandoning the CONSOLI-
DATED COMMISSION CHARGE, which, we have always contended, unnecessarily
Penalises the Woolgrower, as the BETTER the character of the Wool Grown and the
PRICE REALISED the HIGHER the CHARGE.
The following will be our Charges to the Grower :
COMMISSION
2 per Cent.
WAREHOUSING (including Receiving into Store, Fire Insur-
ance, Weighing, Lotting, Repacking Sample Bales, Adver-
tising, Cataloguing, etc.), on Wool realising over Gd. per lb.,
the charge will be ... ... ... ... ... 3s. 9d. per Bale.
On Wools realising 6d. and over 4d. per lb. ... ... ... 2s. 6d. per Bale.
On Wools realising 41b. per lb. and under ... ... ... Is. Gd. per Bale.
ON PRODUCE (other than Wool), viz., Skins, Hides, Tallow, etc., SPECIAL RATES will
be quoted on application.

Chills
Ever become
chilled through and
through ?
The acute sen-
sation of cold is
followed by fever-
ishness and aches in
all parts of the body.
Finally the whole
trouble settles in the
throat and lungs in
the shape of a cough.
There is nothing
so bad for a cough
as coughing.
i^rm
cherry.
Pectoral
will cure these chills and coughs. A
few doses are sufficient at first. More
time is required later on.
Stop coughing and you will get well.
Keep coughing and you invite bronchi-
tis, pueumonia, or even consumption
itself. Better stop your cough at once
and thus end the whole matter.
Prepared by Dr. J. C. Ayer &• Co., Lowell, Mass., U. S. A.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It now turns out that the tombstone (B.
l/8/’OO) found in Little River, Canterbury (M.L.),
inscritied “ sacred to the memory of B. Townsend ;
died A.D. 1774,” was a little joke on the part of a
Chinaman employed by surveyor B. Townsend in
1874. After surveying the Kaituna estate this
genius erected a rude stone and put the date back
a hundred years, making it appear that Towns-
end had “pegged out’’tour years after Captain
Cook visited Australia.
Navvying.
O ! it’s trench it at the edges, and it’s undermine
below,
A bar or two behind it —heave away, and “Let
her go! ”
And the shouting of the gangers can be heard
above the din,
From the “bank” below the township where
the “ lads ” are “ linking in,”
And the shovels are a-grating in the gravel and
the clay,
As We load the old muck-waggons on the tem-
porary way.
“ Fill ’em up, ye sons of —horses, there ! and
hook-on blanky quick ! ”
And you’re never kept a-waiting with the shovel
and the pick.
Then it’s keep the shovels going though you’re
failing in the back,
And you haven’t quite recovered from the
starving On the track ;
And it’s keep the waggons rolling till the “ sprag-
ger ” makes a slip,
And the foremost goes a-crashing into splinters
off the “ tip.”
“Hi! you upon the slope, there, have you got
an eye at all ?
Cut down them bloomin’ bumps a bit and regu-
late the fall.
No pointin’ there, Maloney, or ! Look out!
it’s falling, lads ! . . .
Here, nipper, to the blacksmith like the devil
with those gads ! . . .
Stand by and let the waggons in—once more, ye
cripples, go ! ”
And anxious ears are waiting for the dinner-time
“YoHo!”
And for building up an appetite there’s nothing
known can lick
A morning’s heavy grafting with the shovel and
the pick.
Then it’s keep the picks a-swingmg till the sweat
is all a-drip,
And the waggon-wheels are ringing as they’re
rolling to the “tip,”
For the Co. is in a hurry and the contract’s got to
pay,
And it wants a lot of muscle for the “ six-an-eight
a day.”
So, it’s grafting in the daytime, and it’s drinking
beer at night,
And it’s “ headin’ ’em ” on Sundays, or it’s looking
at a fight.
Though it isn’t elevating you will follow for the
fun,
And get yourself a “ plugging ” p’rhaps before the
day is done.
Ah! well, the brute’s but hidden by the regal
raiment bright,
And the grime upon the navvy often hides a man
that’s white.
His mind it may be rusted, but bis sympathy is
quick,
And of sin he’s fully shriven by the shovel and
the pick.
v.
BEN SUN.
A White Man.
There was a man on the Western Side
With a heart as big as his lands were wide;
For his squatter-friends he had open door,
And a helping hand for the weak and poor ;
He was the heir of an ancient race,
With a firm hand-grip and a laughing face,
And a courtly style and a record fair,
And they called him the White Man everywhere.
And the swagmen passing at any hour
Had their pound of meat and their pint of flour ;
And the drivers bogged on the black-soil plain
Never asked for the White Man’s help in vain,
For the famous team with the Spade-Bar brand
Would slew them out to the nearest sand ;
And the teamsters swore, as they took the track :
“ He is the Whitest Man out-back ! ”
But the droughts made havoc of grass and sheep,
And lined his forehead and wrecked his sleep ;
And troubles came to him one by one,
Till the Bank took over his river-run,
And left him one paddock that men might say
They had not taken his all away ;
And he toiled at the plough behind the hill
But the bushmen knew him a White Man still.
And there came a day when the White Man died,
And the neighbors gathered from far and wide,
From far and wide and the fenceless west
To follow their friend to his last long rest;
And the bushmen crowded from far and near,
Bronze cheeks wet with a silent tear,
For out in the land of the sunsets red
They know how to honor a White Man dead.
And they buried him down where the wattles
grow,
By the road where the wool-teams come and go,
Where the night is a splendid silence, save
For the curlews calling across his grave ;
And, stumbling under their world-old load,
The swagmen turn from the dusty road
To the truth of the head-stone’s carven scroll:
“He was a White Man : rest his soul! ”
N.S.W.
WILL OGILVIE.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing With Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 5000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 20, 1900.
A Policy for the Commonwealth —
Federal Old Age Pensions.
The Parliament shall, subject to this Constitution, have
power to make laws for the peace, order and good govern-
ment of the Commonwealth with respect to
XXIII. Invalid and Old Age Pensions.— Commonwealth
Constitution clause 51.
So far none of the States of the coming
Commonwealth has really established an Old
Age Pension system, though the matter is
being talked about with the same deadly and
abstract persistence that has made N.S.W.’s
Local Government Bill and Sydney’s city
railway cheerful but unproductive subjects
of conversation for 35 years. Maoriland,
where a virile race does things, instead of
being only on the verge of doing them, has
led the way, and proved that the Old Age
Pension idea is practicable, and perfectly
within the range of everyday politics. Vic-
toria has proved the same thing accidentally
and without being aware of the fact. Under
a disastrous Civil Service Act, passed by
certain wildly irresponsible parties in the
very early days, Victoria is paying about
£312,000 a year in pensions, not to the poor
and aged, but to a rather affluent class—
many of them not at all aged. The pension
law was repealed nearly 20 years ago so far
as concerned new-comers into the Civil Ser-
vice, but its consequences remain, and will,
remain, though in a constantly diminishing
degree, for many years. According to Maori-
land experience, and according to the respec-
tive populations, £312,000 a year would be
about sufficient to provide for every indigent
male and female Victorian of 60 or 65 and
upwards. The State pays this sum t j its ex-
civil servants with so little effort that
probably not one voter in twenty is aware
that the responsibility exists ; and while the
Tories of the land denounce Old Age Pensions
as an impossible burden Victoria proves
every day, without being really conscious of
ihe fact, that there is no real difficulty in the
matter. The State Governments of New
South Wales, Victoria, and S.A. are all
understood to be “ considering the matter ”
with great energy, and the other three States
are supposed to be keeping it in view—with
rather less energy, perhaps, but very steadily.
In fact, some kind of Old Age Pension
system is inevitable in several of the Aus-
tralias within a very few years, either as a
State or as a Federal responsibility.
* m * m
The reason why it should be made a
Federal matter is that the Central Govern-
ment can carry the system out more
thoroughly than the individual States. As
a matter of necessity there must be a
residence as well as an age stipulation.
The applicant must qualify by living in the
State for a certain number of years—enough
to make it reasonably certain that he gave
the State some value in labour and taxes, and
in carrying the other burdens of citizenship,
before he began to receive value from it.
If any State granted Old Age Pensions with-
out a residence qualification it would become
a home for the Aged of all Nations, and on
the deck of every incoming ship the white
heads of the applicants for State aid would
be like Kiandra’s snows in winter time.
Therefore, if the matter is left to the States,
Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria, Tasmania,
S. A., and Westralia can each pension, out
of its own resources, the aged inhabitant
who has spent, say, 25 years of his working
life in Queensland or N.S.W., or Victoria,
or Tasmania, or S.A., or Westralia, as the
case may be. That is all they can do. And
when this has been done there will remain,
unprovided for and rejected all round, a
multitude of good old deserving Australians,
who have spent, it may be, all their
years in this country, and done it good
service, but who never remained long
enough in one State to qualify anywhere.
The hardiest of the pioneers—the prospec-
tors, carriers, shearers, cattle-men, pearl-
fishers, the men who find new goldfields,
new pastoral country and new stock-routes,
the great legion that never was listed, the
whole tribe of Clancy of the Overflow—
would be shut out. The inhabitants who
vegetated would qualify for pensions because
their vegetable souls kept them in one place.
The inhabitants who opened up Australia
wouldn’t. But if the Old Age Pension sys-
tem is the concern of the Federal Govern-
ment, then the only needful qualifications
will be that the applicant must be in need,
of the prescribed age, and have put in so
many years’ residence anywhere in Aus-
tralia or Tasmania—or in Maoriland, when
Maoriiand comes in. To leave the pension
system in the hands of the States when
there is a Federal Government to attend to
it would be to greatly reduce its usefulness
and make it a gratuitous mockery.
• • • *
And when the Federal Government does
take the matter up it will probably find it
one of the best investments any Government
ever undertook. Australia wants to gain
population, and when it is known as the
one country in the world where an old
citizen can’t come to absolute want, that will
bring population. It will probably do more
in that direction than all the millions of
revenue money and loan money that have
been spent in promoting immigration. Aus-
tralia wants to retain its own population,
and there will be a strong inducement for
population to, remain in the only country
which offers something better and more
reliable than a workhouse or Benevolent
Asylum to the impecunious citizen-in his
last years. The Old Age Pension system
will probably cost the Commonwealth
£1,000,000 or £1,500,000 a year for a popu-
lation of 4,000,000, and the bill will naturally
increase as population increases. But at
present the charitable votes of the various
States amount to about £600,000 or £700,000
a year, and that also tends to increase as
population grows., In all probability about
half of this amount will be saved. Private
charity absorbs a sum which it is im-
possible to accurately estimate, though
it is certainly a vast amount, and much
of this will also be saved. There will
be a saving in gaol expenses when the
broken-down pioneer is no longer run in
under the vagrancy laws. There will be a
certain saving in hospital expenses, in
coroner’s inquests upon the ancient who is
“ found dead,” and in pauper funerals. The
pressure on the labour market will be some-
what relieved when the fringe of broken-
down toilers, who are now ready to sell such,
powers as they still possess at almost any
price, are no longer on the market, and that
will make things better for those who re-
main, and reduce the expenditure on relief
works and other like expedients. The grant
of a pension of, say, 10s. per week to every
impecunious male and female of 60 or 65
who can show a reasonable residence qualifi-
cation may look a large order, but the
burden will be alleviated by so many savings
that it will probably be found only half as
large as it appears. So far as concerns the
other half, it will be merely a question of
systematising the relief already granted,
making it a matter of right instead of
a favour, abolishing the pauper element
in the receiver and the arrogant pat-
ronage of the giver, placing the pen-
sioner in the position of a free citizen
who has simply come into the annuity he
has himself paid for by his tax-contributions
in better days, and enabling him to collect
iFwilhout cringingTo any unpleasant person
clothed in a little brief authority.
* • *9
Probably, in the long run, an Old Age
Pension system which costs £1,500,000 a
.year will be less of a burden on the com-
munity than a £5,000,000 British loan at
per cent.—and heaven knows that is a bur-
den on which this country enters lightly
enough. It is always made a condition that
the old-age pensioner must live in the
country, and therefore circulate his little
income in the country. By spending' his
money here he improves trade and helps the
grocer and butcher and other deserving and
more or less greasy fellow-creatures on their
path, and if in 40 years perhaps
£60,000,000 has been spent in Old Age Pen-
sions, the money hasn’t left the country, and
the country is none the poorer in the aggre-
gate. And in 40 years a £5,000,000 British
loan at 3| per cent, takes £7,000,000 in cash
out of the country and out of circulation,
and leaves the country that much poorer,
and its expenditure doesn’t aid the local
grocer and butcher in any way. The differ-
ence between £7,000,000 and £60,000,000
may be large, but £7,000,000 taken out of
the country is certainly much more costly
than £60,000,000 left in it.
* * * *
When the Commonwealth Government
takes over the vexed Old Age Pension ques-
tion it is to be hoped that it will do it in a
wide and generous spirit. Above all it is to
be hoped that it will drop the weary shib-
boleth about confining State aid to the “ de-
serving.” If a citizen has the requisite age
and residence qualifications, and is in need,
no further questions should.be asked. If he
is undeserving by reason of drink it should
be remembered that drink is one of the
principal sources of revenue, and he has
manfully aided the State with his mouth,
and helped to keep down the deficit. If he
is undeserving through mere shiftlessness it
should be remembered that the man who
gets up does it through being a little more
energetic and intelligent than those who re-
main down, and many a man who
is a semi-millionaire to-day attained to
that giddy elevation through being a
great fool but not quite so great a fool as
others. If the bulk of the community wasn't
shiftless, most of the members of the Upper
House would be in the road-metal business,
and they should bear lightly on those whose
incapacity enabled such incapable persons as
themselves to be less incapable than the
average, and to profit by it. If the undeserv-
ing person spends his pension in drink, the
State has the satisfaction of getting three-
fourths of it back in liquor duties, and of
knowing that it will be rid of an undesirable
person all the sooner. A thoroughly un-
deserving citizen may drink himself to death
with one year s pension, and the economist
who would keep him alive and miserable and
useless for 20 years by confinement in some
kind of dry asylum is a person whose £ s. d.
is defective. Even if the pension applicant
is a chronic gaol-bird there is at least the
satisfaction of- knowing that, should he
relapse and go back to gaol, his pension will
be saved during his confinement. And if he
doesn’t relapse—if the possession of a small
regular income induces him to keep out of gaol
—it probably costs less to pay him his pension
when he is out of prison than to maintain
him in it, so there is satisfaction that way
also. At all events, when the undeserving
person is old and past work he must live on
the community somehow , unless he is pole-
axed by special Act of Parliament, If he
gets a pension he will probably live on the
community as cheaply that way as any other,
and the public will at least have the advant-
age of having his address, which will make
it easier for the police to find him should he
relapse into crimes and be wanted. There
is no real reason to believe that the State
would save anything appreciable by refusing
the undeserving patriarch his pension. It
is even possible that it would lose heavily. It
might economise by refusing his little
allowance to the good man ; he would pro-
bably starve to death quietly and-inexpen-
sively. The bad man helps himself and
breaks things, and runs the country into
grievous outlay. The community has itself
to consider in other ways also. If it refuses
a pension to the otherwise qualified old man
who is past work merely because he has been
a frequent law-breaker, and he is driven
thereby to drown himself in the com-
munity’s water-butt, his moral badness
doesn’t make the circumstance any the less
unwholesome.
The Victorian Political Crisis—A Man
Wanted.
Last week, the M‘Lean-Shiels Government
ascertained, to its surprise, that it hadn’t fully
sized-up the vastness of the effrontery of Vic-
toria’s famous House of Landlords. That body
has already rejected, on five different occasions,
a Womanhood Suffrage Bill, passed in the Assem-
bly by overwhelming majorities. One Liberal
Government after another has gone to the people
with Womanhood Suffrage as an essential feature
of its programme, and the people have endorsed
it in the only way hitherto open to them, bv
electing supporters of the measure with huge
majorities. But, despite this unanimous support,
the House of 48 Rich Landlords has persistently de-
clared that “ the people hadn’t demanded Woman-
hood Suffrage,” and rejected the measure accord-
ingly. Of course, it was impossible to absolutely
prove that the people had demanded it. A General
Election turns on a dozen large questions, and a
hundred small ones, and on the personal character
and influence and private or political or social or
sectarian record of one or two or three hundred
candidates. The people can’t clearly specify
which were the especial items in the Ministerial
policy that made them put the Ministry m power.
The Victorian electors have kept the Liberal
party in office for many years and by huge majori-
ties. And the Victorian House of Landlords has
rejected or grievously mangled almost every
important item of the policy on which the Liberal
party was returned, and declared regarding each
one that it wasn’t the particular item which made
the people return the Liberal party.
* * * *
The Womanhood Suffrage Bill being rejected
for the fifth time on the old, weary, lying Tory gag
“the people,haven’t demanded this measure” the
M‘Lean Government decided to find out for
certain whether the people did demand it or not.
It proposed that, at next month’s General
Election, a referendum should be taken on the
Woman’s Suffrage question separately. And the
House of Landlords rejected the Referendum Bill
also, with a shriek of horror and by a large majority.
It intimated that it was absolutely opposed to the
principle of the Referendum on this question and
on every other question. So, to all intents and
purposes, it defines its positional! us :
You (the Assembly) represent 252,000 electors. We
(the Council) nominally represent 129,000.
You actually represent your 252,000 electors, for they
are allowed to select practically any one of their own
number to represent them.
We (the Council) don’t in any sense represent our
129,000 electors. By reason of the property qualification
for membership these 129,000 mostly-Liberal electors are
allowed to choose their representatives from among about
2000 hopelessly Tory capitalists. The position is just the
same as if Oom Paul had given the British residents the
suffrage with the proviso that they might elect any Boer
they pleased, but no one save a Boer, to represent* them.
We insist on our right to block all progressive legis-
lation. We reject every important item of the pro-
gramme which the people accepted at many successive
Assembly elections, on the ground that the people haven’t
demanded that item separately, individually and dis-
tinctly. They can’t, in the. turmoil of a General Election,
demand any one measure separately, so we reject every-
thing on this ground.
The only way they can do what we insist on ( i.e .,
demand any measure separately and unmistakeably) is
by the Referendum. And we reject that also.
*****
And now, if there is one real Man (not a mere
part of a man) among Victoria’s political leaders,
that Man’s time and opportunity nave come. The
Womanhood Suffrage question should be dropped;
it is only an item. It is no use wasting time and
labour and years of agitation to get it passed if
the Upper House is to be left in a position to
make the same trouble over every other progres-
sive measure. Victoria will never move with
the times, and keep its place among civilised
communities, if every small item of pro-
gress involves the passing of a Bill eight
or ten times, and five or six general elec-
tions, and thousands of acres of printed appeals
to the House of 48 petrified Egyptians. The pro-
cess is too slow. It is no longer a question of
dealing with the one small symptom, but with the
whole huge chronic disease. The Rich Landlords
have laid it down that, while ready to bow to the
will of the people (they always say they are ready
to bow to the will of the people) they have a right
to refuse to accept the result of a general election,
or of any number of general elections, as showing
that the people want any measure whatsoever, on
the ground that the people haven’t shown an un-
mistakable desire to have that one measure in
particular, but have merely accepted a lot of
measures and a lot of men in a heap. They also
lay it down that they have a right to refuse
the people a chance to even express an opinion
on any measure separately. Between these
two propositions they declare that the House
of Landlords has a right, by its constitu-
tion, to permanently veto every item in the policy
accepted by the people in the only way they can
accept anything (per medium of the Assembly
elections). And the only point worth fighting
about now is the abolition of the constitution
which makes this position possible. It isn’t worth
while to put any other item whatever in the pro-
gramme for the coming general elections. It
should be a one-line programme. There is noth-
ing to be gained by laying democratic propositions
before the people when there is an overshadowing
House of selfish dotards which throws every demo-
cratic proposition down the stairs, and which has
filled up the cup of its arrogance and iniquity by
declaring its right to even prevent the Assembly’s
constituents from clearly expressing their views on
a subject of the greatest possible concern to them-
selves.
* * _ • *
At the coming election the M'Lean Govern-
ment should make a new departure by taking on
its own authority a Referendum of the Council’s
constituents on these two simple questions:
(1) Are you in favor of making every male person who
has a vote for the Victorian House of Landlords eligible
for a seat in that House, and making it a paid body,
elected by single constituencies—a body which will ex-
pire and go bodily for re-election every’ three years?
(2) Or are you content with the present system wherebv
98 out of every 100 Council electors are declared to b’e
political lepers and outcasts, unfit to sit in the House
which they themselves elect ?
That is the question ; all the rest are side issues
and red herrings. The Council constituency is
quite wide enough to return a reasonably demo-
cratic Upper House if any and every Council
elector was qualified to sit in the House of Land-
lords. If the electors had a free hand it would be
possible to get a Council liberal enough to make
every other reform easy of attainment. It is the
property and other qualifications for membership
that confine the representation to a handful of the
worst, and meanest, and most selfish Tories —a
corrupt handful, who use their political power
solely to promote the private interests of their
own little class. It is a case of 130,000 teetotal
electors being informed that they can freely choose
anyone they please out of, say, 1500 confirmed
drunkards, to represent them. And this handful
declares at times that it is quite willing to widen
the suffrage so that a larger number of teetotal
electors will elect these same drunkards —which
won’t _ make the drunkards any more repre-
sentative of their teetotal constituents. The
Council’s constituents are also the Assembly’s
constituents and the Assembly has a perfect right
to consult its own constituents, or as many of
them as it pleases, on any subject it pleases. The
action of Kingston in South Australia has
established an invaluable precedent to that effect.
* * * »
The Victorian House of Rich Landlords bases
its claim to annul the work of the House which
represents 252,000 electors by the plea that it rep-
resents 129,000 electors. The referendum above
suggested, if it was taken alone with the General
Election, when it would be possible to get a large
and comprehensive vote, and if the issue was
clearly put before the people, would show that the
disgraceful House of Landlords docs not represent
129,000 electors, nor a quarter* of them, They
only let its members wander back unopposed year
after year because the men they want
to elect, and who would really repre-
sent them, ate disqualified. If the M‘LeaN
Government put the same issue to the Assembly’s
constituents, anct'ex plained clearly and strongly
that every reform which Victoria has been de-
manding for years is included in this one question,
it would come back with two overwhelming man-
dates—one from the people at large and one from
the Council's electors separately—to make an end
of the brutally arrogant House of Landlords.
The Council would doubtless be as bitterly Tory
as ever, and it would still voice the exploded lie
that it represented 129,000 electors, but it would
haVe received a valuable Moral Shock ; that un-


reasoning section of the community which hitherto
believed that the House of Landlords really rep-
resented these electors, merely because it said so,
would be enlightened as to the real state of
affairs; and the utter hollowness of the great
political Fraud would be exposed.
♦ * * *
Then when the Assembly had passed a measure
for the reform of the Council on these lines, and
the Council had rejected it, a strong and capable
Man (not the half men who make up the M’Shiels
Ministry but one of Kingston’s stamp) would be
wanted to engineer the fight that must ensue. It
is a foregone conclusion that the Council would
reject such a measure, and unless it was fully
intended to fight the matter to a finish, and break
anything and everything that might stand in the
road, the matter might be as well left alone.
There is no existing way out of the
difficulty; what is wanted is a man who can
make a way. One possibility would be by
appealing to the British Government to pro-
vide Victoria with a new Constitution, and
the referendum of the Council’s constituents
would be a valuable means to that end.
Another method would be to buy out the existing
48 by providing that, on the abolition of the old
Council, each member should have a free railway
gass and £IOOO a year pension. There are many
ouncillors to whom the cash bribe and the
degradation would be almost irresistible. In fact,
a body which uses its political authority so exclu-
sively to further its own private interests is just
the degraded kind of institution to which a cash
bribe would appeal; and though this policy would
cost £48,000 a year, till the ex-M.’sL.C.
died out, it would make many things possible
(a graduated land-tax among them) that would
more than compensate for the loss. And, again,
if the Assembly really meant fight, it could inti-
mate that it refused to vote the sum required for
interest on the public debt till the Council
Reform Bill was passed, and that, if it didn’t pass,
the payment of interest would stop short, the
Fat Man’s securities would depreciate to about
nothing, the Fat Man’s banks would be struck by
a panic, and there would be Chaos. This would
be a hard expedient, but the case fully justifies it,
and it would pay to get reform even at that price.
The insolence and tyranny of the Victorian
House of Landlords can’t be ended by gentle
means.
* * * »
But it requires a Man to meet such an emerg-
ency as the present, and the M‘S riels Ministry
of half-men doesn’t look equal to the case.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Latest Freetrade Budget.
When Temporary-Freetrader W. Lyne presented
his recent Financial Statement his principal
enemy, Temporary-Federalist G. Reid, found him-
self in a difficulty. He had to say that the
Financial Statement showed N.S.W. to be in a
glorious state of prosperity because N.S.W. is
still being run on tne Reid customs policy—that
Revenue - Tariff - with - a-small-dash-of-Protection
policy which Reid says is Freetrade while he
knows it isn’t. So he said the Financial State-
ment showed N.S.W. to be in a glorious state of
prosperity. And at the same time he had to say
that the statement showed N.S.W. to be languish-
ing through the loss of the guiding hand of G.
Reid and to be blighted and unprosperous through
Lyne’s misgovernment. So he said that too, or
strongly hinted it. As a matter of fact Temporary-
Freetrader Lyne’s budget is apparently a gross
political swindle and a shameful fake, like most
other allegedly Freetrade budgets, and it shows
N.S.W. to be in the usual ditch of partial insolv-
ency. It ran up against the old, old trouble.
The Reid tariff doesn’t yield enough revenue
to cover expenses. Nobody has courage enough
to put on Is. 6d in the £ income-tax
and 3d. in the £ land-tax to meet the
shortage, or the gag about “ lifting the burdens off
the people” would be blighted and exploded.
Nobody dares cut down Civil Service salaries one-
half and make ends meet that way. So N.S.W.
last year, as per usual, spent more loan money on
unproductive works which should be paid for out of
revenue than Victoria, S.A., Westralia, and Tas-
mania put together; and ended up as usual with a
huge deficit, and covered it up by putting £242,000
of expenditure into the accumulated shortage;
and it squares this year’s accounts as usual by a
cold-blooded fake. Premier Lyne reckons as part
of his revenue £218,000 to be received as rent
from properties in the resumed area of Sydney.
It isn’t probable that, at the most, more than
£lOOO or £2OOO of this will be real revenue. The
value of the properties has to. be decided by a
slow legal process, and when it is decided the
State has to pay for them, with interest from the
day of resumption. After that it will have to
meet the interest on the money borrowed to pay
for the properties, so the interest started at the
moment when the resumption was effected, and
foes right on for ever afterwards. But while
’rentier Lyne takes credit as revenue for the rent*
to be received this year from the properties
resumed, he doesn’t charge himself among his ex-
penditure with the interest payable on the value
of these properties. His apparent idea is that the
law will move so slowly that the interest bill
won’t come in till after Ist Julv, 1901, and it won’t
have to be actually paid till next financial year.
But the interest will have to be paid for this year,
whether it is paid in this year or not, and this
year’s interest will probably amount to over
£200,000. Every penny of the money received
this year as rent from the resumed area should
have been put to a suspense account to pay this
year’s interest on the cost of the properties, and
the item is a gross political fraud to the
extent of over £200,000. Possibly Lyne means to
pay this year’s interest on the cost of resumption
out of loans ! The item is another striking
example of the dry-rot and moral leprosy which
has come in recent years over N.S.W. finance.
Reid ousted Dibbs with the assurance that his
Revenue Tariff system would greatly reduce the
people’s burdens as com pared with Dibbs’s Protec-
tion. And as he couldn’t run the country any more
cheaply than Dibbs did, he had to make his
policy look cheaper by charging enormous
amounts of ordinary expenditure to loans, lhe
unreasoning public asked no questions, and
assumed that all was well. Then the office-hungry
Lyne ousted Reid on the promise not to raise more
taxes to keep the accounts square, so he had to
do the same political fakes that Reid did. I he
Protectionist in office dare not be. straight and
honest, beoause he can’t be straight without
increasing taxes, and he is afraid the public will
set down the increase to the blighting effects of
Protection and throw him out. The fi reetrader
in office dare not be straight and honest lest the
public sets down the necessary increase of taxes to
the blighting influence of Freetrade and throws
him out. The financial leprosy assumed its worst
form when Reid put N.S.W. up to Dutch auction
and advertised himself as the Great Cheap
Premier, and now it is a struggle to find the
cheapest Premier. And the latest Freetrade
Financial Statement, wherein a deficit of £200,000
or more is covered up by not providing for that
amount of inevitable outgoings, is the natural
result. In any country that was accustomed to
honest finance the Lyne Government would be
thrown out of office on this Financial Statement,
and neither Lyne nor Reid would even get the
chance to hold a portfolio again.

The Queensland Stonewall.
A fruitful subject for a constitutional moralist is
“The Decadence of Parliamentary Institutions in
Australia.” A pregnant example is Queensland.
Q. Parliament is almost wholly degraded to sub-
serve the personal ends of the ruling party.
Members are dismissed for seven or eight months
of the year, during which period the Ministry
rule as autocrats, sure that their acts will be up-
held by their pliable majority. When at length
the tardy session commences, private measures
in which members of the Govt, party are in-
terested are put before public business ; and the
minority is told in effect that no public business
whatever will be done until the private measures
are passed ! As the House prorogues at
Christmas probably till July or August
next year—this means that unless the
private measures are hurried through no pub-
lic business at all will be done. No less
than five private railway and tramway bills, in-
volving big State land-concessions and other
privileges, are thus before Q. Parliament at pre-
sent ; and every one of them requires the closest
inspection before Parliamentary assent is given.
They establish a new policy—that of hand-
ing over the making of railways to pri-
vate syndicates, whose members are not even
known, and who give no guarantee whatever that
they will do any work of value in return for the
enormous privileges granted them. Q. Labor-
party has steadfastly opposed these Bills; and
the Govt., in revenge, proposed to abolish the
right of free speech in Parliament by
making the closure of the debate depend upon the
will of the chairman for the time being—who
might easily be a mere Govt. tool. This would
practically destroy the minority’s, rights alto-
gether, and disfranchise their constituents. The
Govt, party already outnumbers them at a. vote ;
and if they are not allowed the fullest right of
debate their Parliamentary office would be void.
The result of three days’ stonewalling is a com-
promise by which the Govt, can closure de-
bate if 30 members desire it, and as the
Govt, fighting strength goes up to 40 in a house
of 72 this merely necessitates whipping up sup-
porters to a closure division. So the Opposition
really cedes the point at issue. The whole course
of the conflict gives point to The Bulletin’s
frequent assertions that Parliamentary Govt, in
Queensland is a farce. And Queensland for the
most part is content ! Australia came by her
Parliamentary institutions too easily. If we had
fought for them, as elsewhere, we should respect
them more.

Outside the Act.
Melbourne Trades Hall leaders, who seem to
have got hold of a master organiser and have been
lately forming new unions and enlarging existing
ones at a great rate, have at last tackled the farm-
hand question. Beginning with a few milkers
and ploughmen gathered locally, they formed a
union which they hope to extend all over the
State. The first meeting had no lack of evidence
before it that the farm-hand is, perhaps, the most
sweated worker in the country, as he is in every
other country. Speaker after speaker detailed
his own personal experience of t"hat pastime,
which begins in the registry-office, where the
milker has to part with a week’s wages before he
can get a job at all, and ends only when he
becomes himself a farmer-sweater, or kicks
the soil from his boots and gives the
whole thing best finally. Average wages for
milkers in Victoria now run from 10s. to
15s. and found ; for ploughmen and skilled hands
a trifle higher. The hours average from 10 to 15
a day, and the accommodation is generally vile.
Probably a vast majority of farm hands don’t
know what it is to live and sleep in a decent,
healthy room. (Writer knows a number of dairy
farmers in a long-settled district of Victoria, not
50 miles from Melbourne, most of whom own their
200 to 400 acres and clear over <£2oo a year out of
milk alone, but he cannot recollect one who
houses and feeds his hands as a city tradesman or
factory-hand would expect to be housed.) The
cry of the “ poor farmer ” has been heard in Vic-
toria ad nauseam. The railway department studies
him. When seed wheat is supplied by Govt, it
is carried free; it puts on cool trucks and special
trains for his butter, and helps him in many other
ways. The Department of Agriculture also lays
itself out to assist him, in the matter of cool
storage, of ocean freight, and of proper supervision
and inspection. This is not done purely out of
love for the farming persbn, it is done to benefit
the State, but so far the man on the land—
especially the dairyman on the land—seems to
t have gathered in most of the shekels. Notwith-
standing that his butter brings (through the
aforesaid Govt, supervision and assistance) a
steady, remunerative price for as much as he can
supply, and though his factories and creameries
pay him handsome dividends in addition to a good
price for his milk, he habitually sweats his em-
ployes. If the Union, formed the other even-
ing, can extend its branches only moderately
during the next two years it should then be
strong enough to demand, when the Factories
Act comes up for re-enactment, or the Federal
Legislature takes over the whole business, that
the trade of farm-hand generally shall be brought
under said Act, and that the country worker shall
have the same rights and benefits as his town
brother. There is no reason why a wages Board
should not be empowered to fix varying wages and
hours, on a sort of sliding scale, for winter and
summer, for the different branches of the farm-
hand profession. There will always be thousands
of applicants for employment on public works and
for city billets, and there will always be unem-
ployed, while employment on the land, in addi-
tion to its social drawbacks, is made so profitless
and so hopeless for those who would otherwise
willingly take it up with a view of gaining the
necessary experience and capital to start on their
own.

The N.S.W. Hospice for Dying Toryism.
“ Hermes ” takes a look in :
The Legislative Council lives next to Sydney Hospital,
presumably in case of emergencies, for there is a strangely
infirm look about the whole body. The chamber itself
is a comfortable room with long, high-backed, leather
benches in which hon. members can bury their aching
frames. Round the cornice are numerous electric-lights,
and there is a large one let into the centre of the ceiling,
so that the room gets the henefit of its illumination while
the edge is taken off the glare. Below is a parliamentary
table littered over with papers and dotted with electric
candles with shades of beautiful emerald green. Round
the table sit most of the Councillors. Round the walls
are white busts of dead-and-gone Presidents. It doesn’t
look like a Parliament—more like an ideal Old Men’s
Club. Members get up and jerk out a few remarks in
low, tired tones, and then subside suddenly, and one
wonders how on earth the writers in the gallery above
manage to get even half of what they say. In the matter
of dress there is a most amazing absence of frill. The
cheerful Jack Want, the night writer was present, wan-
dered about in a shabby sac coat that would have done
excellently to potter round in the garden with. Yoko-
hama Jack is beginning to show signs of a good
healthy crop of barnacles, and at a distance, in the
uncertain light, he appears to be wearing a pigtail care-
fully coiled round the hinder part of his cranial nobility.
He seems to realise his own proper value as the boss
skiter of the Chamber, and treads the carpeted floor with
the satisfied air of an Angora tom, the most dignified and
self-conscious of all the cat tribe. This suggestion of
feline attributes is not peculiar to the man from Japan ;
it seems to pervade about three-fourths of the Council.
They are nearly all more or less reminiscent of the pre-
datory animals.
The watch-word of the Council is Justice—of a sort.
The Councillors prate and gabble eternally of justice—to
the man in the nail-keg. They were discussing the Den-
tists’ Bill, and man after man arose and drivelled that he
hoped the Council would “ do injustice to no man.”
Jack Want also got up and hoped that no injustice would
be done. He would like to say a word or two for the un-
qualified gentleman in the Ned Kelly head-armor, who,
by reason of long legislative laxity, had acquired a vested
interest in defrauding the public by getting its money
under pretence of being a qualified tooth-smith. It would
be harsh in the extreme, after a man had been thus defraud-
ing the public for years, and perhaps doing them an
irreparable injury as well by his incompetence, for the
State to step in and take away his means of living in a
Ned Kelly nail-keg. Mr. Want didn’t use those very
words, but that is the brutal meaning of what he did say,
and altogether it was a very touching appeal. Then that
pawky statesman and financier, Dr. M'Lauiun, Chancellor
of the University, arose and (in effect) hoped that no injus-
tice would be done, and that the Bill would recognise every
individual who had been wrecking people’s jaws for two
years before the passing of the Act, no matter how incap-
able he might be, as a" lawful practitioner, and give him
a diploma or something, whereby the law would practic-
ally guarantee him a reliable dentist when he wasn’t one,
and consequently become a liar by implication and an
accomplice of swindlers and quacks. It never seemed to
strike any of the statesmen of the Hospice that it was
time some little consideration was given to the public.
Messrs. Want, M’Laurin and Co. exist only, judging by
the tone of their remarks, to conserve the interests of, and
see that no injustice is done to, such as wear nail-kegs.
Any elector who is dubious about the necessity of sweep-
ing away the Council should attend a few of its debates.
A week will convert any ordinary individual to an earnest
advocate of a meat-axe method. The Councillors, as a
body, are smug, comfortable, and utterly oblivious of the
public. No matter bow dilatory and backward the
Assembly may be, the Council always lags behind it. Its
only apparent emotions are greed and fear—greed for the
interests of the men who wear pot-hats and fear of politi-
cal extinction.

The Adelaide Tramways Grab.
Owing to the vacillation of the Holder Govt.,
Adelaide is in great danger of losing its valuable
tramway rights for an indefinite period. Awhile
ago one F. Gentry Bingham, an English promo-
ter, agreed with Adelaide City Council to take
over its street rights, paying the Council £l5OO
per annum and 10 per cent, on yearly profits, but
the suburban councils objected and the scheme
went bung. Then the numerous parish coun-
cillors endeavored to formulate a Municipal Trust;
but they quarrelled, as everyone expected they
would, and the Trust scheme went bung
too. Meanwhile Railway Commissioner Pen-
dleton had formulated a sound business-like
scheme of nationalisation, and reported that the
tramways—converted into an electric traction
system—would return the State a handsome profit.
Premier Holder, notwithstanding the fact that
State ownership of tramways was part of the policy
of the old Kingston-Holder Government —fa-
vored the municipal trust idea, and said that only
failure to inaugurate it would lead him to advocate
State purchase. Months went by, and nothing was
done. One Snow, who represents the wealthy
Westinghouse Co. and has already purchased
most of the big lines, now has a Bill before the
Legislature which is about the biggest and most
blushless grab, in S.A. history. Snow proposes
that the citizens shall be allowed to repurchase
his lines in ten years time for (say) £400,000,
paying him in addition £lO,OOO per mile
(£700,000 for 70 miles) for converting to electric
traction (Railway Commissioner Pendleton esti-
mates cost of conversion at £BOOO per mile, so
this is a clean present to Snow of £140,000 !), also
costs of flotation of companies (estimated at
£100,000), and interest at 5 per cent, (estimated
at £140,000.) This makes a total of £1,340,000,
but Snow doesn’t stop there. He asks for a
bonus of 10 per cent, in the event of
repurchase rights being availed of, and £134,000
is therefore added to the sum. Snow paid nothing
for getting the use of the streets, but for giving
them up again he asks practically £514,000 over
and above the actual value of the lines, and
Adelaide is to eventually pay Snow’s absentee
company about £1,474,000 ! Railway Commis-
sioner Pendleton estimates that during the
first 10 years the profits, under State con-
trol, would average £lO,OOO a year, and S.A.
isn’t so rich that it can afford to make Snow
a present of £lO,OOO a year. And over and
above this there is the saving of £514,000 in the
matter of buying back Snow’s privileges. Yet,
astounding as it may seem, the Snow promotion
is supported by the great bulk of the members of
Adelaide City Council and the suburban corpora-
tions (the latter rejected the much more generous
Bingham scheme), who, with one accord, condemn
State ownership ! And will it also be believed
that the democrat Holder and his colleagues still
remain dumb as to State purchase, although their
Railway Commissioner strongly urges it ? What
has Kingston to say about this matter ?
From Launceston (Tas.) Examiner :
As there is no longer a vestige of Boer Government re-
maining Lord Roberts will be justified in proclaiming
outlawry against those still in the field. Under a civi-
lised Govt, the war would have been brought to a conclu-
sion long since. The Boers, however, are not civilised in
this sense of the word, and therefore those remaining can
be treated only as disturbers of the public peace. ... It
may be that a few irreconcilables will fight to the last,
but they will do so with the knowledge that in the future
when caught they will be tried as criminals, and not
treated as prisoners of war.
These may seem hard words, but still they are
solid truth. De Wet and the. few Boer leaders
who still remain in arms have now sunk to the
degraded level of William Wallace, Robert
Bruce, Judas Maccabeus, Kosciusko, Gari-
baldi, Kossuth, Benito Juarez, Diaz, the
Greek insurgents of Byron’s day (Ipsilante,
Kolokotrones and Co.), William Tell (if there
ever was such a person), Washington, and other
depraved characters. None of these had a
recognised Government behind him, and people
like that should be put down—or hanged
up. Some day, when the -writer has a holi-
day, he is going to wander over to the Scotch
city of Dunedin, and there publicly burn
William Wallace and Christian de Wet in
effigy. Wallace, as the worse offender, will be
kicked in the stomach before burning. Both
these individuals fought against the spread of
civilisation—England was as much ahead of Scot-
land in what is conventionally termed civilisation
in Wallace’s day as it is ahead of the Transvaal
now. Both fought against the unity of the Anglo-
Saxon race. Both wanted to burst up the Empire.
In fact, Wallace, being obviously a low-minded
ffian, was a great factor in a process of revolt
that did burst it up ; and the big Empire which
Henry 11. founded, and which extended most or
the way from the Orkneys to the Pyrenees, went
to pieces through him and others like him, and
there was no Empire for 300 years as a, conse-
quence. He was agin’ the Flag and the civilised
British government —in fact, he was ade \V et,
and a very bad sort. And all good Jingo Scots
should meet once a year to curse Wallace and
Bruce (another Empire-burster) all over the place,
and to damn the Covenanters with a big D.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, in a recent leader,
urged N.S.W. not to worry about getting the best
site for the Federal capital, or the one which
would be most suited to the interests of Australia
in general and of the future Australians of thou-
sands of years to come—but to select and barrack
for some site, even if a less suitable one, where the
capital could be fixed up quickly, so as to get the
Federal Government moved into N.S.W. a year or
two sooner and proceed to grab the profits for
“ our State ” :
The fact that the means of communication by rail
(with Bombala) would have to he furnished before the
building of the Federal city was entered upon would
mean a very long delay. It cannot be thought that the
many preliminaries would be completed and the neces-
sary lines opened for traffic until several years had
elapsed. It may be granted that'the other provinces are
prepared to act in good faith towards N.S. Wales, and
will be quite ready to expedite the removal of the Federal
capital from its temporary location in Melbourne. Bat it
would, surely be folly for N.S. Wales to fix her eye on the
far .future, and, in order to provide for the widest pos-
sibilities, place obstacles of time in the way of the trans-
ference of the Federal capital to some spot within her
own border.
Don’t trouble about the far future and the best
interests of Australia, says the Telegraph ! _ The
great consideration is to get the capital as quickly
as possible within “our” own borders, and get to
work land-booming and syndicating, and darn the
interests of Australia.

The N.S.W. Old Age Pension Bill, introduced by
the Lyne Ministry, is a reasonably satisfactory
measure in most ways. It demands 25 years’
residence in Australia, of which 10 must have
been spent in N.S.W., and, in this matter, it is
more liberal than the Maoriland Act. The
pension-age is fixed at 65 years; the pensioner
must not have an income of over £52 a-year or
property worth more than £390 ; and he or she
must be of good moral character, and have
a continuous record of five years’ sobriety.
Where a husband and wife are both qualified,
they will receive £l9 10s. a-year each
(£39 between them), and the single pensioner
will receive £26 a year. This is provided these
amounts do not raise their income above £7B a
year in the case of a husband and wife or £52 a
year in the case of a single pensioner. Where it
does the pension will lie diminished so as to leave
the total income at £7B a year for husband and
wife or £52 for a single pensioner. But there is a
great and glaring silence as to where the money is
to come from. That problem of raising the money
is always shirked as long as possible.
The state of Brisbane dirty, unregulated, un-
drained Brisbane, with its smells crying out to
heaven, and the Plague at its back door, and the
hot breath of summer coming in at its front
window—has at last drawn the Philp Government
to introduce a large and rather comprehensive
Health Bill, which promises, if decently adminis-
tered, to work a revolution in things. The Health
Bill made the bowed-and-bent ghost of old B. D.
Morehead emerge from its grave to protest
in the Upper House. One clause provides that,
when a house is so utterly foul and decayed
as to be unfit for human occupation, and the
owner won’t repair or pull it down, the local autho-
rities may do it without compensation. As the local
authorities are mostly landlords, this proviso
isn’t likely to be very rigidly enforced But old
B.D. declared that if the dirty landlord who mur-
dered not only his own tenants but the surround-
ing population with his foul properties, and who
made his town an eyesore, wasn’t “ compensated ”
at the expense of the community he had injured
for the loss of his fever-den, “ it would be a dis-
grace to our legislation.” He also said he would
approve of the dirty landlord resisting by violence
the destruction of his precious plague-spot. But
Morehead tottered alone in his protest—a solitary
advocate of the rights of Property to spread fever
and plague as it pleased. The others feared for
their Property much, but for the Plague more,
and the clause was passed.
N.S.W. Labor-party has, in the projected Hope-
toun orgy, the chance of its existence. It brags
of holding the balance of power. Let it work the
balance by backing-up the non - Labor members
of the Leg. Assembly (some Reidites and Lyneites)
who are ready to vote-down all eating and drink-
ing at public expense, and insist on the sum esti-
mated to be thus saved being donated to
the Children’s Hospital or a National Con-
sumptives’ Home, or some other worthy ob-
ject. The contemplated gorging alone —the cham-
pagne and turkey and so on, which even washer-
women must help to pay for, but won’t get
bite or sup of—will figure out at £20,000. Is the
Labor-party game to move ? One Minister says it
won’t move. Another says the Labor-party’s
teeth and stomach will prove it the greediest cor-
morant of the show. The chance, however, is
there, and, if MGo wen won’t take it, some other
should lead the van against national waste in
general, and sponging on washerwomen and
kindred classes in particular. If there must be
an eating and drinking carousal, let it be at so
much a head, and the tickets be paid for by the
participants.

Premier Lyne draws in his horns. He now says
no road is to go through Sydney Botanic Gardens,
but a fence is to be erected along the sea-wall;
the Nicest People are to be admitted to the Gar-
dens by ticket when Hopetoun lands; and the
procession is to prance along the present narrow
track. Why does he want to drag the procession
through the Gardens at all ? Aren’t the Domain
and the city streets good enough for vehicles ? If
Sydney citizens have any grit they will protest
against the public Gardens being reserved for the
use of a few. Mrs. Smith, of Waterloo, in her
four-bob-robbo, has just as much right there as
Mrs. De Smythe, of Darling Point, in her C-
spring carriage, and the Labor members who rule
Lyne should see that she gets her rights. Mean-
while, The Bulletin believes that the Gardens
road project is only put by for the present. If it
is put by for good, there is no sense in making a
new road round Lady Macquarie’s Chair to end
in a blank wall. The advocates of the new road
guite misconceive the spirit of the opposition. It
is an opposition to extending the sights and
smells and noises of the city to the only natural
beauty-spot accessible within the city. The
natural charm of plain rocks and water is
infinitely preferable to the dust and rattle
of vehicles and the odour of horse-drop-
pings. Ninety-nine hundredths of Sydney’s
population are very well satisfied with the Gardens
as they are. The city tumult is precisely the thing
