“Ingomar” is a drama of very rare
appearance nowadays. It is revived occasionally
to suit some man of might whose brawny limbs
Step out like the supports of the diprotodon in the
ancient Gallic forest, or some lady who feels that
she looks well in the long clinging white garment
and the very little underneath of the Greek
heroine. In the present case it was revived
for the benefit of Miss Nance O’Neil, and
the lady is plainly the central character, and
though Plimmer is a respectable Ingomar, he
doesn’t light out like the diprotodon afore-
mentioned. Maria Lovell’s old drama must needs
strike the average reader as an unsatisfactory
capsizing of the usual order of things. Ingomar
was a Gallic chief, a mighty Cow-shaker before the
Lord, an untrammelled and long-haired son of the
wilderness, a fighting man, and a scorner of
women and the effeminate joys of marriage. And
one day, when this Samson was throwing up the
gates of Gaza for his casual amusement, and
catching them as they fell, there came to his
camp, among the wild-haired barbarians, and the
bones of the shaken cows, and the cow-horn
drinking cups, Parthenia, the Greek girl
of Massilia. She offered herself as a host-
age for the release of her father, an
ancient damp gentleman whom the band had
unfortunately captured and enslaved, and who
spent his time in weeping and bringing his masters
liquor in the cow-horn. They accepted the lady
as a hostage, and she enslaved Ingomar. The
mighty Cow-shaker abandoned his beloved forests,
his chieftainship, his fighting record and his sword,
and cut his hair short, and went down to Massilia
to be one of the street-bred people about whom
Kipling kipples. He ploughed and harrowed,
and became a beast of burden and a bill of lading.
He learned to be humble before his future mother-
in-law, and was ordered about by his future wife,
and bore himself respectfully before the Timarch
and the aldermen. He even became the slave of
a bent worm to whom Parthenia’s family owed
money, in order to prevent the family being sold
up. And in the end he had his reward, for
he was allowed to marry his Parthenia, and
have her mother’s society all the rest of his
days, and his hair was cut permanently,
and he never went back to his forests, but
settled down in Massilia where, it is to be
hoped, the local aldermen took notice of him occa-
sionally. He became tame and fed out of the
hand, and so the tribes lost a good Gaul, and the
city gained a man who was too honest ever to be
a good Greek, and whose weight must have cost
a great deal in pavement-repairs.
* m * *
The character who stands out as an un-
questionable success and a success all the time is
Atholwood, as Polydor, the money-lender, who
desires to sell the family up. Atholwood made
his first distinct mark in Sydney as the very
remarkable drunk in the “Royal Divorce,” and
he has made his mark on several occasions since.
Polydor is a very fine piece of work, and as he
wanders to and fro, looking hard on the floor for
the main chance, he is a person whom it is a
pleasure to meet. Plimmer’s Ingomar, on the first
night at all events, ended better than he began.
He is a fine, large, hefty barbarian, but on Saturday
there were times when lie seemed oppressed with
doubts as to how the part should be played.
Later on he forgot to nave doubts and simply
played it, and then the result was reasonably suc-
cessful. As for Parthenia, Sydney hasn’t even
yet quite forgotten Essie Jenyns, and Nance
O’Neil isn’t quite an Essie Jenyns. Yet she fills
the sandals of Parthenia much better than she
did the costly raiment of Countess Fedora; she
manages to subdue herself in Parthenia’s gentler
moments, and the result is tolerably satisfactory.
Yet, where a big opportunity presented itself
Essie Jenyns took it with both hands, and Nance
O’Neil only seizes it with one. Parthenia is
rather a mystery. She gives herself up to the
Gauls without any carpet-bag or brown-paper
parcel, and with obviously nothing save the one
snow-white robe and the very little underneath.
She is detained by them some time, and returns
home in the same spotless tog. What she
wears, or what bush she hides herself behind,
while her outer garment is hung on a tree
to dry is a matter of grave uncertainty. The
three persons above-mentioned are the bulk
of the drama. Joseph Carne does the little part
of the Timarch like a true artist, and makes him
stand out'as a much larger and more gentlemanly
potentate than he usually is. The Timarch has
only , three appearances, but when the city is
besieged, and strange sounds like the frying of
many eggs in the distance betoken the approach
of the enemy, he faces the tumult like a real ruler
of men. Mrs. Bracy, George Becks, Harry Over-
ton, Frank Hawthorne, Barton Hill, J. W.
Sweeney, junior, and other deserving characters
ANOTHER OF HAGENBRECK'S HAPPY FAMILIES
(Under Enyayement to Fitzyerald Bros.).
also hold up the glory of old Massilia in a greater
or less degree.
* * * •
The fine tragedy of “La Tosca,”
sacred to the memory of Mrs. Brown-Potter’s
immense hat, is to be staged at Her Majesty’s on
Saturday.
# * # Mf
“All for Gold,” now enjoying a brief run
at Sydney Criterion, is founded on Sue’s “ Wan-
dering Jew,” and, like most plays taken from
novels, neglects to explain matters as much as it
should. Hence there is no apparent cause why a
weird old Hebrew should appear at the back of
the stage now and then with an Aurora Borealis,
like a new sample of halo, round him—though he
is a change from the large silver angel who is
generally deputed to save little Florrie at the last
moment. For the rest, the audience gradually
gets to understand after an act or two that an
immense fortune is waiting in Paris for the
descendants of a certain family, who seem to be
identified by a sort of dog-collar with a medal
attached. Anyway, when anyone finds a little
boy with this arrangement on his neck, he is en-
titled to assume that the boy is part heir of the pro-
perty, and that charity towards him will be in
no wise thrown away. The only condition regard-
ing the property is that the heirs must claim it
at Paris at a certain hour of a certain day, other-
wise their claim is “off.” This happening in
stageland, two of the claimants, who are small
girls under age, are naturally brought from Russia
at the last moment, with just enough time to get
to Paris if no accident occurs. The father, who
can’t come himself because he is fighting somebody
somewhere, is so placid about the matter that he
gives the care of the children into the hands of a
rather lunatic and elderly soldier, who, when he
loses his passport or gets into trouble through
allowing his horse to be eaten by somebody’s valu-
able panther, can only exculpate himself by saying
that his ear was once pulled by the great Napo-
leon, or something to that effect ; said pulling
evermore branding the pulled one as an honest
man. The father bears the loss of the fortune and
the reputed loss of his children with equal forti-
tude, and when he is finally pitched over a preci-
pice by a villain in long boots he is still calm.
* • • *
It goes without saying that Dampier acts
well the old man whose maunderings about the
empire foreshadow what our grandchildren will
have to put up with, when our present Transvaal
heroes are old and garrulous, and* inclined to get
out of police-court cases on the ground that
Baden-Powell once spoke to them. John Forde,
as the faithful and humorous servant, tries to con-
sole the audience for the continued absence of
Buffer, and succeeds, towards the end, in his
attempt; while Appleton shines rather brightly
as a money-lender with an immense bald head.
Two small girls take the difficult parts
of the co-heiresses, and show a surprising
equanimity in the day of trouble, and resign
themselves to frequent deaths with undue cheer-
fulness. Stiff thereisagood deal more promise about
the two than can be said for the average child-
performer, who glares across the footlights in a
wooden manner when informing her dear papa
how she loves him.
*# * *
That unavoidable calamity, “East Lynne,”
will occupy the Criterion stage on Saturday.
Some day, perhaps, Ibsen or Pinero will re-write
“ East Lynne,” and make Lady Isabel a real flesh
and blood woman, who leaves Carlyle because the
ordinariness of that good, plain, dull solicitor is
sadder and flatter than the Old Man Plain;
and who leaves Levison afterwards because he is
only an ordinary, duff, bell-toppered villain ; and
dies at last unrepentant, unmusical, and
unspectacled, a rebel to the end. “ East
Lynne ”as it might be, would be worth seeing.
As it is it is a kind of tea-meeting trying to be
wicked and failing badly.
** * *
“ Banjo ” Paterson is overlanding to
Adelaide, enlightening a few of the smaller towns
on his way. He appears at Adelaide Town Hall
on the 22nd, and the Holy City will meet with a
very different “ Banjo” from the one who looked
uneasily at his boots on his first Sydney appear-
ance. The lecturer’s nervousness has worn off,
and he faces his audience now as unconcernedly
as if it was one of Botha’s batteries. Paterson
saw more of the war than most correspondents—
far more than any other Australian correspondent
—but at first he was packed so tightly with facts
that he couldn’t get them out as he wanted them,
and he was unaccustomed to the work and got lost
among his vast stock of material. He was like a
universal provider whose six-storey emporium had
become mixed-up, and who was looking for a
stove-lid in the drapery department. But that is
all over now, and “Banjo” has not only a good
story to tell but knows exactly where his story is.
* * *
The Tivolians of Sydney Tivoli have
vacated Carldam Woods and settled in Arcadia,
which has a background of ferns and a fountain.
The revellers have also adopted a more becoming
dress, so that the countenance of Harry Diver no
more rests on a large white collar looking like
.lohn the Baptist’s head on a charger. The
Catnbry Sisters make their first appearance.
They take the place of the Clarence couple, and
make a pleasanter impression. Miss Priscilla
Verne is somewhat worse. Mr. Will Whitburn
has one good story of the Trilby Tragedy reduced
to goat life, and one long joke which has run
through the saddest comic papers during the last
three or four years. The Georgia Magnet remains
as immovable on her two small feet as she was
last week, but when a lady is immovable one
week, and quite fixed the next, and can't be
shifted the week after, and later on several people
try hard to lift her but can’t, the fact begins to -
have a sameness about it. The “great” Lottie
Collins, from London, makes her first Australian
appearance at the Tivoli next Saturday.
m» # *
“ New Babylon,” at Sydney Royal, ends
this week, and the public will hear with regret
that its ever-beloved Bland Holt closes one week
later. “The War of Wealth,” to be staged on
Saturday, is the final production of the season.
“Jones” also-ends his long career at the Palace
to-night (Thursday). The circus has already de-
parted, and “ Banjo ” Paterson has done likewise,
and the silence which always falls on Sydney at
Melbourne Cup-time is coming down like a big
cloud.
** * »
Of George Musgrove’s grand-opera ven-
t_iro at Melbourne Princess’s The Bulletin
wishes to say, in the first place, that it made an
admirable start on Saturday. There are so many
P- aiseworthy items in a first-class production of
even “II Trovatore ” that a brief notice must
m .ke one word cover a lot of ground. ' “ Admir-
able ” is intended to represent fully a dozen
different notes of admiration.
* * * *
“Trovatore,” all the same, is a less im-
posing fable in its present English form than when
warbled in the original Italian, so probably a lot
of people will deplore its translation into a lan-
guage which they can understand. As a matter
of fact, Verdi’s opera runs a neck-and-neck race
with “ The Bohemian Girl ” for literary demerit.
It is a luridly melodramatic story, without
the least sense of humor in its antique
situations, and the new co. plays it strictly
according to tradition. Manrico encompasseth
himself with tin armour, Leonora wears
a black dress for the miserere duet, and the
prisoner within the dungeon accepts an encore
without pausing to consider his grief. Happily,
the prima-donna doesn’t shatter the illusion by
shaking hands with the conductor at the conclu-
sion of a triumphant solo. The novelty of this
“Trovatore” revival is in its artistic refinement.
* • • «
Costumes and scenery are good ; the
choristers are not ranged in an absurd semi-circle,
and the low orchestral pitch certainly makes for
realism in the performance. The principals are
able to sing without fear of bursting a blood-
vessel, and their notes come forth rounder and more
sweet than if they were produced under the high-
pressure system. There’s an unexpected delicacy
in the musical effects. You leave the opera with- <
out a headache. Of course, it is a question whether
the new charm will have a greater attraction for
everybody than the grand old operatic tumult of
yore. The first company of big-voiced Italians
whom Simonsen brought to Australia were a
popular success. They sang furiously, and the
orchestra backed them up, and the excited multi-
tude in front enjoyed a “blow-out” of melody.
The Musgrove entertainment is a lighter, daintier
musical repast, easily digested, and remarkably
tasteful. Five of the imported principals appeared
on Saturday, when a critical audience found a
verdict in favor of them all. The smart German
baritone, Max Eugene, scored the least success,
but he gave the impression that his voice was not
doing itself justice. Prima-donna Miss Cecilie
Lorraine is a charming, not very passionate,
singer, and a fairly good actress. She fills the
bill. Salvi is the typical plump Italian tenor,
somewhat taller and less puffy and mechanical
than most; and his full, mellow voice is
a treat. Charles Tilbury, the basso, commands
respect as a fine vocalist who can act, whilst
Miss Agnes Jansen, the chief contralto of the
co., promises to be a particular favorite. She sang
and acted with fine dramatic force as the lurid
gipsy woman who had mixed two babies up and
thrown the wrong one into the fire. The charac-
ter of Azucena naturally suggests Little Buttercup,
in the last act of “Pinafore,” yet Miss Jansen com-
pels one to take her seriously. Messrs. John
Crook and Slapoffski have the orchestra and chorus
under perfect control, and their work can’t be over-
rated in an ordinary way of eulogising the men
who rule the forces. “ Faust,” introducing the
other half of the co., is to be alternated with
“Trovatore” three days this week—Tuesday,
Thursday and Saturday. “Carmen” presum-
ably will come out next week, Miss Jansen taking
the name part.

Her Majesty’s (Melb.) went into recess on
Saturday after three turns of “Trilby.” Just
now the house is taking on a new d.c. entrance, a
crush room and other improvements, including a
lounge whither the male animal may pass the
time ’twixt acts in an odour of tobacco smoke.
All this by way of introduction to “The Rose of
Persia,” which falls due on the 27th.
*« * *
At the Royal they have decided to take
away “The New East Lynne ” after Friday. The
despondent lady in blue spectacles has not grown
on the public to any great extent, though her
burst of grief when she discovers that Willie is no
more, and her energetic attempt to shake the dear
departed back to animation, are rather impressive
in their way. Perhaps The Bulletin ought to
reconsider its suggestion re placing all the mem-
bers of the co. in the forefront of the fatal railway
calamity. Some of them don’t deserve anything
worse than a shock to their nerves, and these
might be allowed to get a gentle upset in the
guard’s van, whilst the worst offenders were suffer-
ing sudden death next to the engine. On Satur-
day our well-beloved sister, John F. Sheridan,
revives himself in his female capacity. He will
appear as Widow O’Brien for six last farewell
nights, and then he will depart for Sydney,
Maoriland, Westralia, and foreign-lands. Mean-
while he hopes to inaugurate the Widow’s fare-
wells by backing the Caulfield Cup winner, in the
interests of “ fun, sunshine, and jollity.” Sheri-
dan’s desire to promote “jollity” is very notice-
able in his advts.
** * *
Nothing fresh has happened at the Bijou
since the two cheerful brown comedians and the
two ladies to match were added to the first part.
The very volatile lady who has got her faithful
Walker dead is a decidedly original success ; also
the cake-walk retains some of its bloom of novelty.
Little Fanny Powers, too, is a bright first-part
item. Fanny’s precocious talent for singing about
dollies, silvery moons, &c., no longer takes Mel-
bourne by surprise. All the same it is a great
talent and it seems to be developing with the
nipper’s growth, and as for her sturdy young legs
they are the most intelligent, active and dis-
ciplined members of their age that Australia has
ever seen or dreamt of.
** * *
Rev. Haskett Smith has abandoned Melb.
Athemeum Hall to its fate, disgusted, prob-
ably, at the general lack of interest in his
lectures. Haskett, the Egyptologist and
shatterer of Conventional delusions, is really
a more fascinating entertainment than ten
melodramas for any who approach him in
a teachable spirit, but Melbourne is just now
“off” lecturers, unless they talk war-gore and
kharki. Which reminds The Bulletin that
Uitlander Sampson, of the Akgus, the invincible
Jingo who painted South Africa red on the map
before the trouble started, is now about to lecture
on the History of the War. And, by the way,
Fitzgerald’s Circus is understood to be marching
upon the Victorian capital, but it has to march
slowly on account of the ruminative elephant.
The fattest Melbourne policeman weighs
225 t., and is known as “ The Blue Mountain.”
The S.A. Protector of Aborigines estimates
that there are now 2861 in the province; there
was an excess of 273 deaths over births in the 12
months ending June 30.
Dr. Laure (whose portrait appears in this
issue, proposes shortly, after a residence of 32
years m Sydney, to return to his native France
He was born in Toulon, and having studied medi-
cine in Paris, joined the French navy, retiring
after several years into private practice in the
island of Bourbon. Thence, 17 years later, he
came to Sydney, where he is well known. Out-
side his professional work, Dr. Laure has been
closely identified with music, in which he took an
active interest. He takes with him the good
wishes of many friends of all nationalities.

* • • *
Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the “ Lu.hrig ” Vanners, as supplied by Messrs. W. &
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Royal Bank of Australia (Melbourne)
finished another prosperous half-year on 30th September,
1900. Starting from the date when the Royal wrote off
£150,000 capital and the last of its £26,521 reserves and
undivided profits, progress has been as follows :
Profits. Dividends.
March, 1897 .. .. £3,399 3 per cent. = £2,250
September, 1897 .. 3,625 3 2,250
March, 1898.. ... 3,865 3 „ 2,250
September, 1898 .. 4,088 3 „ = 2,250
March, 1899 .. .. 4,314 3 „ 2,250
September, 1899 .. 4,624 3 „ 2;250
March, 1900 .. .. 5,843 4 „ 3,000
September, 1900 ~ 5,863 4 „ = 3,000
£35,621 £19,500
This leaves the Royal Bank with reserves of £16,121
(reserve fund, £lO,OOO, and undivided profit, £6121), so
that some little progress has been made towards replacing
the £176,521 of shareholders’ funds which disappeared.
The last half-year’s earnings are equal to £7 6s. 7d. per
cent, per annum on the capital and reserve fund—a very
high average as things go nowadays. During the half-
year deposits have increased by £61,800 (from £363,538 to
£425,342), and the reserve of cash and liquid securities
has expanded from £125,497 to £185,628 —equal to the
very solid proportion of 40 per cent, on all liabilities to
the public. The Royal is in a decidedly prosperous con-
dition nowadays, but it wants to avoid anything like
undue haste after large dividends if the management
intends to make it a large and strong institution. It
would be interesting to know whether the said manage-
ment looks forward to some day replacing the lost capital
out of earnings, and restoring the institution to the
position it occupied before the Great Slump fell on Mel-
bourne. In fact, it would be interesting to know that re
quite a number of institutions. The Royal management,
by the way, makes a wise departure this half-year by
putting its reserves in State and municipal securities, and
it is to be hoped this practice will be permanent.
* « * ♦

tal and total reserves of £235,089, but it transferred
£75,000 from the latter to the former, making the paid-up
capital £125,000 and the reserves £160,089. In the fol-
lowing table, however, the dividend is calculated on the
£50,000 capital actually paid up by the shareholders, in
order to show the real return which these fortunate indi-
viduals have been receiving on their money :

♦ • * *
There are many interesting points about these
figures. The Australian Alliance had, in 1893, reserves
amounting to £160,089. The reserves are now only
£96,094—a falling off of £63,995. The ordinary profits
have been £83,646, and against that the management has
paid dividends amounting to £96,250 —this at a time
when there was heavy depreciation of the assets to be
provided for. At the last two triennial valuations of the
life department the whole surplus was transferred to pro-
fit and loss account to cover depreciation and to help in
paying dividends of from 15 to 40 per cent, to the share-
holders. Even now no systematic attempt seems to have
been made at writing down all the assets to their real
present worth, and it is carefully specified that £315,000
worth of them (out of a total of £504,000) are merely
“ book value.” “ Book value ” may be the same as real
value, or it may even be less than real value. Or, again,
it may be so much more than real value that, on a per-
fectly fair valuation, all the rest of the reserves are swal-
lowed up, and the Australian Alliance may have no real
reserves at all. “ Book-value ”is an unsatisfactory kind
of remark.
• • * •
The life department seems to be expensively
managed. The Australian Alliance does practically no
new business (only four new policies were issued last
year), and, as it is new business which is most costly, the
expense-rate should be very low. Something like 8 per
cent, should be ample. Yet, last year, the total life-
income was £24,349 and expenses £3761 —equal to per
cent. “Commission and branch charges” were only
£139, so it actually cost £3622 (nearly 15 per cent.) in
mere management. The Australian Alliance estimates
future interest-earnings in its life department at £3 10s.
per cent., which should be low enough if the assets are
worth their book value ; it is the rate assumed by such
excellent offices as the A.M.P. and Maoriland State Life
Department. But last year it only earned £3 10s. lOd.
per cent, on the average funds of its life department,
which leaves a very small margin of safety indeed. Ana
the smallness of the interest earned strongly suggests
that the assets are not worth full book value just now,
and that to bring them down to real present value
another heavy writing-down is required—which the al-
ready depleted reserves can stand none too well.
The Australian Alliance, with its immense
paid-up capital of £125,000 and £96,094 (on paper, at all
events) of reserves is a sufficiently safe concern . for
insurers. But it looks as if it might (unless there is a
great increase in property values) be, ere long, none too
good a concern for shareholders. The dividend should
be rigidly cut down to 5 per cent, on the nominal paid-up
capital of £125,000 (this would be 12J per cent, on the
£50,000 actually paid up by shareholders, and 121- per
cent, is a very fine dividend). And then a systematic
process of writing off depreciation out of earnings—not
out of reserves—should be initiated.
* * * *
Brisbane Permanent Building and Banking Co.,
a large concern with £112,624 of paid-up capital, com-
pleted its 26th year on 31st August, 1900. In 1893 this
concern, then burdened with about £250,000 of deposits,
reconstructed, agreeing to pay 4 per cent, interest on
current accounts and 6 per cent, on fixed deposits. Since
then it has progressed as follows :

Reserves have been reduced in seven years by £4984,
from £23,024 in 1893 to £18,040 in 1900. Still, the reserves
have been increasing again for the last two years, and
there seems no reason why they should not get up to
their old figure again in a year or two. The Brisbane
Permanent Building and Banking Co. has now £144,500
of deposits, so it has paid off over £105,000 of them, and,
in doing so, has realised a large quantity of assets in a
very bad time with remarkably little loss. Also, it has
performed a remarkable feat in keeping up the payment
of dividends through the worst days of the slump ; and it
has long since released all its reconstructed deposits, pay-
ing the 6 per cent, interest in full to the last. It has
solid reserves of cash and liquid securities, and seems a
most prosperous institution. Ex-Premier Dickson, chair-
man of directors, has read (or intoned) the report to the
shareholders at 26 consecutive annual meetings.

The capital (£11,020) is still intact, on paper anyhow, and

small balance towards writing off depreciation. And
though the assets (about £19,600) yield barely 3 per cent,

much more than £lO,OOO or so j'ust now, they seem to be
improving materially, and might improve much more if
held for a better market.
* * * *
The balance-sheet of the Dunlop Pneumatic
Tyre Co. of Australasia (Melbourne), for the year ended
26th July, 1900, shows a profit of £16,257 —equal to £9 1 Is.
3d. per cent, on the paid-up capital of £170,000. The
biggest asset is the paper or goodwill item, “goodwill,
trade marks, patent rights, leases, &c., £130,716.” Of
this, £3216 is written off, reducing the profits to £13,041.
This is £7 13s. sd. per cent, per annum on capital, which
isn’t equal to a very gorgeous dividend. And, at this
rate of progress, it "will take very many years to fully
write off the goodwill item, so that the shareholders’ capi-
tal will all be represented by saleable assets—and the
bike and the Dunlop tyre, great as they are, are both
liable to be killed dead any day, and the remaining
£127,500 worth of patent rights and goodwill turned into
waste-paper, when some better device comes along.
» * * *
Great Cobar Copper Mine : Consternation at
Lithgow over the decision of the Cobar tributors to
change the system of smelting, abandoning the reverbera-
tory furnaces at Lithgow and establishing at Cobar the
Bessemer system of turning matte into blister copper.
Also stated that the tributors have exercised, or are about to
exercise, their option to buy the big mine right out, and
it is unlikely that the determination to change the smelt-
ing system would have been arrived at unless they had
also decided to become owners instead of tributors. Lith-
gow doesn’t like the idea of 200 men being thrown out,
but takes consolation in the fact that most of them have
been offered work at Cobar. When the tributors become
absolute owners of the Great Cobar copper mine the em-
bargo by way of royalty charged on all ores smelted there
will be removed, and a" good thing it will be for the other
local mines. At present the system is to heap-roast about
one-half of the sulphides, putting the whole into blast
furnaces and making a 30 per cent, to 35 per cent, matte,
largely by one operation ; training this to Lithgow,
making copper by means of reverberatory furnaces,
a small blast furnace being there used to work
up the reverberatory slags. The new system will consist
of doing away with all heap-roasting, bringing the matte
up to 45 or 50 per cent, by a second operation in the
blast furnaces, and turning this into blister copper per
converters. No doubt in the latter operation there will
be a great saving in fuel, none being required in the con-
verter itself, and only that necessary for the production
of the hot blast. Naturally there would need to be a
great saving in this direction at Cobar. Their excess of
iron and deficiency of silica will be further accentuated
in the new system, as they will need silicious ores in the
bringing up of their matte. Here comes another oppor-
tunity of arranging with the Cobar Chesney for the
blending of its high-grade silicious picked ores and con-
centrates. It is just as good an idea for these two mines
to work in unison as it was for the Mt. Lyell to acquire
the South Tharsis mine for the sake of the latter’s silica.
* * » *
Eldorado, Cobar : Said to have struck 6oz. sul-
phide ore. “ Three tons have been dispatched from the
mine, and bagging of the same-grade ore is continuing.
The lode appears defined and permanent.” The Dr.vsdale,

Gallymont (N.S.W.) : “It is ..reported .that a
reef has been discovered going 6ozs. to the ton.” The
60-head stamp mill and other machinery on this mine
are being dismantled to go to Mt. Boppy, Cobar district.
Shares in London a shilling, but that is a better price
than offered for the Gibraltar Consolidated, the initial
float of the Angdo-Australia’n Co. ; buyers 6d, sellers Is.
Yet the Gibraltar is'producing from 800 to 1200ozs. a
month, and the price of the shares does not pay for
more than a tenth of the cost of the plant. It is the
way of all mining things in London ; shares are either
monstrously over or under valued. Think of it—
Gibraltar shares were once £2 . 10s in London. That was
no greater an anomaly than their present price.

Hillgrove Proprietary: “ During the last few
feet the reef in the face has been very thin, but this is
quite a characteristic of Smith’s reef, so it is not altoge-
ther-unexpected.” It is, anyhow, refreshing to have the
truth about the reef. In N.S. W. reefs are nearly always
widening. No details given, but from what the writer
knows of Smith’s reef, “ aery thin ” would mean the thick-
ness of a penknife-blade.

Turon River dredge, N.S.W. The tipster last
Sabbath : “ Recent events all tend to show that the high
estimate will be realised. The results themselves are
eloquent enough and there are miles of splendid ground
and a progressive average may be fairly anticipated.
The monthly profit from one dredge alone must reach
between £6OO and £7OO, and in the operations of No. 1
dredge a still bigger result is being obtained.” The
Bulletin last week : “ Now if a dredge works for many
months at a certain average return that is suddenly
doubled, which is more likely—that it will go back to the
old average or keep up the new ” ? Sydney D.T. this
week : “ The decreased return from the Turon dredges
for last week depressed the shares somewhat. The
returns were 30oz. from No. 1 dredge and 39 from No. 2.
Total 690 z. Just a little over one-half previous week’s
yield.
* * * *
Dayspring mine (N.S.W.) has been resuscitated,
and is going to have a new shaft and a new battery. All
it will want then will be a new mine or a new shute in the
old lode which is well defined, but too poor to pay. It
has one fairly good shute of ore in it. If it were double
the length with the same richness, or double the richness
with the same length, it would do.

Tindal’s, outside Coolgardie, claims to have
made a profit of something over £3OO from Aug. 18 to
Sept. 15 out of 1042 tons of stuff averaging 3dwt. 20gr.
This is no douht the Westralian record to date. Of
course, Tindal’s formation is all in favor of records of
that sort—an 80ft. body, mostly easily-worked quartz,
broken out in a quarrv. Down to this, 6dwt. at Hope’s
Hill, and 7dwt. at the Craigie More (Mt. Margaret), were
claimed as the best results in the Western province.
* * • ■
Lake View and Boulder Junction. Five leases,
4 acres in all. Main shaft 764 ft., crosscuts put out at
750 ft. level. Gold found in most of the rock passed
through, but nowhere in payable quantities. Thousands
of feet of driving and sinking done without finding any-
thing worth crushing. At the 100 ft. level there is a for-
mation 20ft. wide, worth 5 to 6 dwts., but not good
enough to pay in this camp ; doubtful if it ever will. It
is wider still in the 200 ft. level and also poorer. There is
another formation 10ft. wide at 370 ft. level. Plenty
mineralised country, but no payable gold. It is outside
of the Golden Mile, a poor mine surrounded by others
equally poor—Boulder North Extended, Golden Pipe and
Lake View East, Boulder Central, Hannan’s Central
Extended, and the Ironsides North.
» » * *
Kalgoorlie Mint and Iron King intends to try
another parcel of oxidised ore. Development work from
the 500 ft. level having proved barren, sinking is to be
renewed to test the formation at greater depth. The
share-market shows that the public are getting very
tired, but at Hannan’s mines are prospected before they
are abandoned to an infinitely greater extent than in any
other part of Australasia. Why ? Because the patient
—and pig-headed—Bull owns them.
* • * »
Hannan’s Block 45. “ It is probable that when
sufficient stone has been raised a parcel will he crushed.”
Quite a usual circumstance. Telluride was first found on
this block, which is surrounded by the Federal, Croesus
South, Venture, and other leases. It is outside the
Golden Mile, surrounded by a lot of wild cats, strikes an
occasional rich seam of ore having no abiding place, rich
to-day and poor to-morrow ; and ekes out a languishing
existence by means of favorable reports, constant calls,
and water. It produces about 20,000 gals, daily—which is
something to he proud of at Hannan’s, wheresome mines
having the least gold have the most water.
* * * *
Perseverance : Milled 1950 tons, 8330 z. ; cya-
nided 1394 tons, 1185 oz. Sulphide plant treated in Aug.,
291 tons, and in Sept., 3210 tons, yielding by amalgama-
tion, 27140 z. ; by cyanide, 301 oz. Estimated value of
three tons of zinc sludge shipped to Fremantle smelters,
981 oz. ; total, 61840 z., including 170 oz. silver. The market
regarded the result as a great disappointment, and shares
fell nearly £2 in a few days. It is not so much the result
as the capacity of the mill which is disappointing.
Manager Nichols says the extraction is up to his expecta-
tion, that the mill was hung up some time in Sept., and
so far only three furnaces have been in operation. It will
probably be some time ere any of the Hannan’s sulphide
plants are brought up to their full capacity, and it is
impossible to arrive at the cost of treatment until they
are. Impossible, too, to start plants of such magnitude
without small breakdowns, and it is doubtful whether
any of them will work up to the capacity estimated ;
chances also are that the cost will be heavier than antici-
pated. Roasting is the trouble. The main difficulty is to
get a “ sweet ” roast at small cost, and the great feature
of the Diehl process is that it does without roasting.

Golden Zone : Payable stone is being raised
from the winze. Good news is always regular from the
Golden Zone; so are its calls. It. is a “ wild cat ” sur-
rounded by wild cats. It was never anything else, and it
is much cheaper to forfeit shares than to pay calls on a
wild cat’s shares. It has been kept alive mainly by a
journalist’s big interest in it. Probably better for the
journalist if he had allowed it to die naturally.

Paringa Consolidated : Has had some assays
averaging 20dwts. to the ton and some more that only
averaged 3dwts. “West Australian” still barracks for
this “ wild cat ” in the Financial Times, but never gives
his name. He says : “ After anxious thought I have come
to the conclusion that the holiday season and the general
dulness may account for the apathy and want of public
interest displayed, and surely those that are in the know
must be availing themselves of the circumstances.” Those
in the know would leave Paringas religiously alone. It is
an amalgamation of several “ cats,” each of which ex-
hausted its working capital in failing to find gold, and it
will not be long before it will collectively repeat its in-
dividual experience. There are more “ wild cats” within
a few miles of the big mines of Kalgoorlie than in all the
rest of Australasia.

SOCIETY
Lovely item from S.M. Herald of Friday
last:—
The works for the construction of Hopetoun Avenue
were visited yesterday afternoon by the Premier, who
found that they were not being carried out in precise
accord with his instructions, and that a great deal of
damage had been already done along the foreshore of the
Domain to trees, shrubs, and some of the familiar rock
surfaces. This destruction had mostly occurred on the
previous day, before it was made clear to the officer in
charge that it was the Premier’s wish that the road
should be constructed outside the foreshore on a founda-
tion reclaimed from the harbour.
Before it was made clear to the officer in charge,
<kc, I I Was there ever a more miserable attempt
to drop the responsibility on to the man under-
neath ? But perhaps N.S.W. is a country where
the officer in charge receives vague, verbal in-
structions to make some kind of a road round the
Domain, somewhere near Mrs. Macquarie’s Chair,
and goes down with a crowd of men and starts
work without a plan or anything else !
* * • *
There is no doubt that the long, grovel-
some centipede who devised the “ Hopetoun
Avenue ” intended to perpetrate the boss piece of
vandalism in the history of N.S.W. Along the
east and north of Sydney Domain foreshore the
men had already cut down several picturesque
native trees, and tell-tale pegs of red show where
the rounded bluff was to have been cut and blasted
away till it looked like a hunk of a railway-cutting
dropped into the middle of Paradise. At Mrs.
Macquarie’s Chair heaps of broken stone have
begun to obliterate the natural baby reefs that
were such an ornament to the place ; but, pre-
sumably, now the project of bringing the road
round the east side has been abandoned, these dis-
figuring heaps will be removed —when the people
have revolusned about three times and threatened
to hang the persons responsible,
• • • •
Editor Bulletin.— Be Premier Lyne’s ex-
planation that the proposed drive round Sydney
Domain and Botanic Gardens “would be for
private conveyances only.” What regulation
could prevent the butcher or baker, or the green-
grocer, from taking his missus and kids for an
airing in his “ robbo,” right under the noses of the
Naicest? I hardly think the community would
stand the shutting-out of aforesaid citizens from
their own property because their equipages didn’t
take rank as “kerridges.”—H.S.
• * * •
According to the London correspondent
of Sydney D.T. :
The Princess Louise’s intense dislike for publicity no
doubt influenced her refusal of the Governor-Generalship
of Australia. She “ could never make a friend there. It
would be worse than in Canada.” At Rideau-hall she
might be able to preserve a provisional incognita.
If that’s so, Australia has escaped an infliction.
Funny, though, to find that the Governor-General-
ship was apparently offered to the lady instead of
to her marital appendage. The whilom Lome,
nevertheless, would have been precious glad of the
screw to help out the somewhat scanty revenue of
his dukedom. Anyhow, wherever she got it, Louise
has a pretty good idea of what Australian polite
society is like. There would be no “provisional
incognita” in Australia. It would be “ Yer
R’yal Highness” every time, and poor Argyle
would have to sit around in dark corners to get
out of the blinding glare of his wife’s exaltation.
• * • *
The Yorks’ visit to Australia may prove a
good thing democratically. Nine-tenths of our
people have never seen Royalty, and probably
never would; they used their imaginations and
servile newspaper versions in their conceptions,
with the result that Royalty as they think it, is
greatly superior to and immeasurably more
magnificent than the real article. When the
sft. 4in. Duke arrives, representing Royalty
as it is (representing it fittingly, too,) this game-
loving and hero-worshipping country will receive
a shock that will take very much condescension
and Royal favor to soften.
* * * *
Toby Barton was the only one of about a
dozen speakers at the Melb. Mayor’s luncheon
who did not slobber like -a boa-constrictor over the
Yorks’ impending visit. In a half-hour speech
Toby did not even refer to the coming Crawl, and
dodged all contentious questions in a mass of
rhetoric that made the assembled fatmen say
afterwards he had made a magnificent speech,
but they were hanged if they could remember
what it was all about.

Nobody can say Griffith, C.J., doesn’t rule

cold iron. In ’9B, the head-mistress was, to all

A very tremendous recent Sydney wedding
led to an unfortunate adventure which befell a
young man from Melbourne travelling for pleasure
with the missus and the kid. It was announced
that the principals of the magnificent marriage
hereinbefore alluded to were going honey-moon-
ing in a certain picturesque locality not far distant
from the metropolis, but this announcement was
merely a “ blind ” to preserve the fond but shrink-
ing couple from the offensive curiosity of the vul-
gar. The bride and groom took another direction
altogether, but fate carried the travelling Vic-
torian with his missus and his kid to the salubri-
ous township mentioned in the papers, and to the
man’s surprise he found the station platform
packed with a sympathetic crowd. When he
stepped from the carriage his surprise heightened
to amazement, for the sympathetic populace
howled joyously at the sight of him, and threw
up its hat and cheered till it went purple in the
face. _ But when that bewildered stranger handed
his wife on to the platform, the delighted popula-
tion cheered more furiously still, and discharged
about 2ijcwt. of rice down the backs of the dis-
tracted couple. The young man arose to protest,
but when he opened his mouth to speak the popu-
lace heaved lbs. of rice into it, and howled like a
delighted insane asylum, so the man grabbed
his baby from under a rug, and turned and fled.
At the sight of the infant an Arctic coldness and
an awful calm fell upon the populace, and as the
couple broke from the station the engine whistled
a long, low, insinuating whistle.
* * * *
Re Premier Lyne’s proposed bonus to the
Australian poetical industry, what a dreary hope
we have of getting an Australian ode at this day
and date ! Think of the garish foreign “tripe”
that would result from the Imperialistic inspira-
tion now in the air—a Kiplingesque yap mainly
about motherland, Joe Chamberlain, African
slaughter, and Duke of York.
When Melb. City Council was talking of
painting Melbourne red and planting foreign trees
all over the place in honor of the visit of the Duke
of York, one timid councillor ran the risk of being
cursed black as a pro-Boer by hinting in an
apologetic way that a few Australian trees might
be planted too. Whereupon a ponderous alderman
named Maloney squelched him with the declara-
tion that ‘ there is no beauty in Australian trees
compared with the trees of other lands.” This is
the true Imperialistic and “patriotic” attitude.
Ihe Council greeted it with approving grunts,
and no Australian trees are to be planted.
* * * *
A pleasing little circumstance elicited by
the current N.S.W. Military Inquiry Board is
that, through the miserable bungling of the local
military authorities, some of the N.S.W. officers
in S’Africa have been reduced practically to rags,
and to the necessity of infringing discipline by
borrowing money from the men under their
command.
The Tramway dispute has grown so hot in
Adelaide that everybody who holds tram-shares is
under suspicion, while everyone who might, could,
should or would be connected with the proposed
conversion of the city system to electric traction
is accused of either giving or taking bribes.
* * * *
Prof. Morris, of Melb. University, at the
close of a lecture on “Capt. Mathew Flinders,”
in Adelaide, advised S.A. to alter its absurd name
and to substitute “Flinders” for it. Once upon
a time an electorate stretching from the south to
the north coast of the province, and from the
eastern to the western boundaries, was called
Flinders, but in recent days the Northern Terri-
tory and Newcastle have been taken from it.
Still, even now the district of Flinders is one of.
the biggest constituencies in the world.

Minor offenders brought up at Sydney
police courts extenuate the charges against them
by alleging they have taken part in the Boer war.
On two occasions lately magistrates reduced the
fine one-half on this allegation.

I Bat one fifelit eve and dreamed %
Of many things, and most of you—
No, all of you. Till so it seemed r
That what I longed for to ensue
V f as what had come:
For by me, in the lamp’s red glow,
I heard a quick breath taken low.
A flame leapt up alert with fear,
Then died in ecstasy of light— :
All blue and gold. I strained to hear
That breath—impatient of the nighj. .
It breathed upon ;
Then thought I waked (.q find it all
A dream of you at evenfall.
But now, once more, warm ’gainst my hair.
That breath again in faint caress
Falls soft. No dream could be more fair
Than this that you are here—unless
You kissed me in’t.
Beloved I—that kiss 1 God lis it true

CAULD KAIL.
“It has been alleged—and not denied-
that there are in Victoria 80,000 lapsed Presby-
terians.”— The Rev. W. O. Wallace, Moderator
of the Victorian Presbyterian Assembly. Age 7 th
Sept.
Na doobt I’m o’ the non-elect
Ear-marked ahint o’ Time,
An’ but a puir, daft, fashous chiel
Wha blethers feckless rhyme ;
I ken richt weel I’ve had a fa’,
A michtie fa’ frae grace,
I mind me o’ the days lang gane
When I ken’d naething base.
Ah ! then I was a sonsie bairn
Wha trod the narrow ways ;
I mind me o’ the dear auld kirk
Where cauld kail gaed for praise
Na organ tones wad desecrate
Those bare, unlovely wa’s ;
The Sabbath was a lang, lang, day ;;
There were na ja’nts, nor ca’s.
I mind me weel I dare na rin,
To whustle was a crime ;
I dare na sing aught but a psalm,.
There was na wee bit chime ;
Na carnal joys the day to soil,
But aye the same cauld kail;
But hout awa ! the deid are deid,.
I wadna at them rail.
Aye, there were giants in those days*.
I ken that verra weel;
Gude fearin’ men o’ michtie deed,
Hail-hairted, upricht, leal;
An’ there were dour an’ drumly loons,.
Wha made o’ life a greet:
But hout awa ! the deid are deid,
An’ Time is awfu’ fleet.
I wadna at my forbears rail,
I lo’e them much ta weel;
Na doobt we aw have had a fa’’
Frae grace, an’ doon we reel::
But hout awa ! we fa’ to rise,
The warld gangs aft agley—
I lo’e a drap o’ steaming punch*
No mair cauld kail for me.
VIO.
ANDREW SARD.

(The hearing of the charges against certain
Marist Brethren in connection with the Stoke
(M.L.) Industrial School Inquiry promises to be
of a very sensational character. The head of the
order in N.S.W. instructed the accused to return
to Maoriland and submit themselves for trial —
the charges then being considered trivial ones of
common assault. Immediately on landing in
M.L. defendants were arrested on other charges
of a much more serious character. There are no
less than 16 charges of common assault against
certain of the brothers audio charges of indecent
assault on boys against another. Of toe boys
alleged to have been assaulted by the latter, one is
now silly and the other a consumptive.

Victoria's romantic small female with the
small dirty story has turned up again—at Castle-
maine this time. She said that she had been
criminally assaulted by a man named Beggs, but
shg apparently thought so little of the matter that
she didn’t even mention it for some time after-
wards. Then she withdrew the charge against
Beggs, and said that she had really been assaulted
by one Smyth, but had wilfully brought a false
accusation against Beggs in order to screen
Smyth. Thus the Crown’s only important
witness was, by her own confession, a liar,
who had tried to get an innocent man a long
term of imprisonment on a false charge. All
the same the Crown prosecuted Smyth on her
evidence, and got him committed, and but for his
good fortune in getting bail he would have spent
two. months in gaol awaiting trial. . When his
case came on the small female (she is about 16)
broke down again and Smytli was acquitted.
Net though the law knows that its witness laid
a false accusation against Beggs, and has every
reason to believe that she did the same against
Smyth, nothing has so far been heard about a pro-
secution. There never is in such cases, though
they happen by the dozen. And this is the reason
why The Bulletin says again that the Victorian
Government is the aider, abetter, and accomplice
of perjurers, and that the announcement is written
large over the Vic. law offices where the eye of
faith may see it: “The Law cordially admires
and approves of any Unclean-Minded Female
who tries to get a Man condemned to years ot
Imprisonment on a False Charge—always pro-
vided that the False Charge is of the dirty kind
and pattern which appeal to the Laws Dirty
Instincts. And it hereby Guarantees that the
said Girl won’t be Prosecuted if her attempt fails,
but will receive every assistance to bring more
False Charges, and it will be Dashed Bad -Buck
if, with the Law’s aid and connivance, she doesn't
get some Innocent Man into Gaol before she is
done.” On the Word of a Woman !

The sea-serpent has been “ seen again—
this time by the people of s.s. Nemesis, en route
from Fremantle (W. A.) eastwards. It s a very
curious thing that in these days of the übiquitous
camera none of the persons thus favoured appear
ever to be provided with the means of getting a
corroborative snapshot. A single photograph
would outvalue bushels of protestation.
The Hospice for the Dying, conducted by
the Sydney Sisters of Charity, is a unique institu-
tion in that it is entirely unsectarian, and that
as pointed out by Mr. Hughes, M.L.C., at the re-
cent laying of the foundation-stone of a new budd-
ing—“the only conditions asked of inmates are
that they be poor and miserable, and in want of a
place wherein to die.” The new quarters, to the
cost of which all classes of the community have
freely contributed, will be a great improvement
on the present more or less makeshift accommoda-
tion.
An Adelaide resident recently lost, from
consumption, within three weeks, three daughters
aged respectively 18, 21, and 27 —-the third death
occurring with appalling suddenness. A brother
of the girls suffers also from phthisis ; and alto-
gether it looks as if our bubonic scare was a bit
absurd in view of the more deadly plague we have
with us all the time.

APOLOGETIC EMPLOYE: “Sir, I would like to go to my mother-in-

Queensland Parliament, on the motion of
Labor-member Turley, lately committed a curious
foolishness. It amended the Health Bill to pro-
vide that, if a parent or guardian has conscien-
tious objections, he need not vaccinate his child.
Vaccination is either good or bad. If it is bad it
should be abolished. If it is good, and all the
evidence points that way, then to deprive a child
of the benefits of it because of the ignorance or
bigotry of its guardian is very rough on the child.
Will Turley go to the logical conclusion and pro-
pose to let a child remain quite ignorant, quite
naked, or wholly unwashed if its parent has con-
scientious objections to education, or clothes, or
cold water?

A recent Australasian devotes 23
lines to working out the interesting sum : If a
snail crawls up 2 - 58333 ft. of a 7ft. post in 12 hours
of day, and slips back l‘333ft. in the succeeding
12 hours of night, how long will it take it to reach
the top of the pole ? The answer having been
given, arrangements for the inauguration of the
Commonwealth can now proceed without further
delay.
* * • *
P.M.-General Watt (Vic.) is still puzzled
how to deal with the employes in his Department
who take undue advantage of the fact that full
pay is given to sick absentees. The average of
sick absentees is now three times as large as when
half-rates were paid. But for all that, civil ser-
vants are remarkably tenacious of life—probably
because the State does not undertake to pay their
funeral expenses.

repentance ; or blindness in one eye ?
* * * *
The drivers on the George-street (Syd-
ney) electric trams complain that the schedule
to which they are compelled to run gives them
insufficient margin for unavoidable delays.
Drivers are fined for being late in consequence of
stoppages for which they are often in no way to
blame. In consequence they are obliged to hustle
their vehicles after a block, with often fatal re-
sults to a stray citizen or two. Anyone gazing up
George-street from the front of an electric tram
must marvel that the driver ever gets through
the traffic at all. The idea of rigidly keeping him
to a hard-and-fast time-table might explain the
frequency of accidents in George-street.

A brave captain-commanding addressed
his men on parade-ground in Sydney lately:—
“I’ve got a lot of your letters requesting me to
have a church-parade next Sunday. I don’t mind
once in a while, but I’m not going to take you
every Sunday, merely to mash the girls.”

A novel application was recently; l.nade to.
N.S.W. Justice Cohen by a man who, two years
back, had been sentenced to imprisonment for
forgery, but had been released on surety under
the First Offender’s Act. He asked now to be
sent to gaol to save him from an apprehended
lapse into liquor and possibly thereafter into such
conduct as might cause the forfeiture of his
securities. The Judge was nonplussed. The law
leaves entirely out of sight the possible preven-
tion of crimes which it is prompt to punish when
committed.

When N.S. Wales P.S. Commissioners were
at Broken Hill listening to the reasons advanced
for extra allowances to officials, one gentleman,
somewhat famed out-back for his eccentricities,
gtavely informed them that he thought he was
entitled to an extra allowance as “confinements
cost much more here than in Sydney.” Also, the
wife of a Broken Hill civil-servant pleaded for an
increase for her husband as in Sydney a shave cost
3d. as against 6d. at Broken Hill.

Talk about the heathen in his blindness !
There are some people round Sydney who can
knock spots off the heathen for eyeless supersti-
tion. A woman of Port Jackson lately paid a
Syrian gentleman £3 12s. to cure her of an inter-
nal disorder by sacrificing goats and making a
sweet savour of burnt William to the Almighty.
The Syrian accordingly slew three unfortunate
nannies in the bush at Mosman’s Bay, but the
fourth victim, or intended victim, was a large bill-
goat, which refused to be offered up. It butted
the prophet into a mud-hole, and then left,* and
the incompleteness of the sacrifice is supposed to
account for the patient’s non-recovery.

“ Star ” on a point of municipal law :
The Armidale (N.S.W.) cabbies are driving their vehicles
through the local municipal by-laws. They were sum-
moned for alleged breaches of the code, originally drafted
by N.S.W. Municipal Association as a model one( and the
Bench dismissed the cases, alleging that as the broken by-
law prescribed no penalty the magistrates were not com-
petent to impose one. The Crown Law authorities, too, re-
fused to direct the Bench, and the cabbies were trium-
phant. But how about clause 252 of part XXIII. of the
Act itself, which says: “Every person committing a
breach of any provision of this Act, or of any by-law
made hereunder, by wilful act, or refusal or neglect to
act or otherwise, shall, when no specific penalty has been
provided for such offence, be liable to a penalty not ex-
ceeding £20.” What is the matter with the array of legal
talent ■at Armidale ? And also the Crown Law authori-
ties. •

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ Sydney, October 16, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
Vice-regally, socially, Sydney is taking her
October sleep, beauty-sleep for November, and
Cup week’s exactions.
At Govt. House, the chintz comes off and on.
Last week Lady Lamington and Miss Briscoe
popped in for a couple of days, and this
week see 3 Lady Ranfurly and two little
Miss Ranfurlys putting up at our State man-
sion for a while. Mr. Perceval Landon, the
latest aide, with a face like Clement Shorter,
turns out to be such an ardent journalist, such an
enthusiast _ in Ink and Kipling and Modern
London print, that one can’t help wondering how
he comes to be inviting Mrs. De Jones to At
’Omes at Sydney Govt. House. My entirely
unauthorised explanation is that he has come all
this way to detect literals in the thrilling book of
impressions that His Ex. is said to be promulgating
up there about—or should I say against—Australia
as it struck ME.
Ah, but it is all very well to jest! In spite of
jests, in spite of birth-stains, blue-tickets, in spite
even of spite, Sydney is getting ready to be truly
sorry for the coining departure of Little Billee.
Did you read the Premier’s speech at Yass last
week? He eulogised Lord Beauchamp in warm
words, remarking that some people in the State
did not know what our present Governor was
worth; that his instincts were of the highest
character and that lie had endeared himself to a
large section. And do you know, judging by all
one hears and sees, I fancy the Premier has not
overstated the case. Certainly Potts Point is
sore still, and society people en mo-use consider
that the Dignity of the Points has oeen
decimated by some of his Ex.’s doings,
but the real, living, actuating part of the
Erovince knows better. It realizes that in
little Billee we have had a Gov. who hasn’t tied
himself to stale old customs—“ the Majority
Truths ” Ibsen denounces, truths so stale that they
are almost crumbling to pieces. And artists,
poets, musicians, poor clergymen—theothah class,
m fact, that is not supposed to know the front
Govt. House door from the back—they have
nothing but regret to utter at the loss of their in-
formal young patron.
You may remark that it is a little late in the
day to recognise this, but the violet that wasn’t
prized till the lovely flower was blown is the his-
toric precedent to which I must point you if you
are not already looking thitherwards.
By the way, a desperate struggle to get on
the Federal A. D.C.sship lias lately been happen-
ing. Applications extraordinary overwhelmed
the Governor-General, and the choice was a terri-
fically complicated affair. “Wally” was a fore-
gone conclusion, with Mr. Saville Gore as assistant
sec. As far as I can discover, one of the staff
is a Benedict—Lieutenant B. F. Corbet, whose
wife .is an intimate friend of Lady Hopetoun.
Lieut. Corbet ought to be a success in this land of
blanks and asterisks. He enjoys the distinction
of being son of a sporting gent whose language in
the field has become a hunting classic.
Quite English in its tone was “Squire” Alec.
Hay's return from his honeymoon trip. The
popular manager of Coolangatta and his bride
were welcomed home by tenants and employes
gala fashion, and escorted to the picnic grounds
by the Berry Lancers and the Berry Brass Band
Pretty blonde Mrs. Alec. Hay (Miss Burdekin) is
the first bride to make her home at Coolangatta
since the founding of the homestead, many years
ago.
A bride of last week was Miss Annie Nugent,
who married Mr. T. A. Dalton at North Sydney
last Tuesday. Card. Moran tied the knot, and a
large and festive wedding party, including every
prominent Catholic in the city, was entertained
afterwards at “ Wheatleigli.” The bridal gown
was tucked white satin, with transparent yoke
and sleeves of chiffon ; and the two pretty brides-
maids wore white glace and ecru lace, and black
crinoline hats. After a spirited interval of toasts
and cake-cutting, the happy couple left for a
honeymoon in Melbourne, the bride in turquoise
voile skirt and bolero over clusy lace blouse
topped with a black hat.
A small, select gathering, including Lady
Darley, came together last Monday to hear Miss
Maud FitzStubbs play her own waltz, the
“Governor-General.” The Governor-General is
nicely got. up in duck-egg green with small crowns
on a pale-blue band. The melody itself is not
more reminiscent than new dance-music generally
is. The introductory bars express the agonised
longing of this country to have its Governor-
General, and the rest is joyful triumph at having
at last got him. The waltz may not be strikingly
original, but it is certain to take, as it is the kind
of stirring piece that gives a man strength to
whirl round 12st. of partner, and that, in this tired
climate, is the main thing. The Federal Orchestra,
conducted by Mr. N. Gehde, played it over once,
and then encored itself three times, with intervals
for conversation.
Verdi’s “ Requiem,” produced bv Sydney Phil-
harmonic Society on Thursday, with full chorus
and orchestra led by Allpress, had for soloists
Edith and Jessie King, tenor Edgar Fulton and
bass W. T. Collyer. There was a large audience,
including a sprinkling of Catholic priests and
heaps of Catholic laity, who could be picked out
by their non-applauding—for that would be sacri-
lege. _ Hazon conducted, looking more Mephis-
tophelian than ever, even when gazing upwards
towards the chandeliers with both arms gracefully
extended in the soft andantes as if about to em-
bark on an aerial flight to the regions where
“ John Harris, Mayor,” is embedded in architec-
ture above the organ. The Mass is not very
massy, but rather operatic—not a bit devotional,
but rather crashy. There were several really blood-
curdling crashes in the “ Dies irae ” chorus and
elsewhere. The soloists had heaps of “ fat,” as
veteran Verdi has provided most luscious melodies
for them. Both those queens of song—the Kings
—sang beautifully, but the men did not rise to
their opportunities. The orchestra was in first-
rate form, but the chorus was the most perfect
feature of the show, and the fugue “ Sanctus ” for
double chorus and orchestra had to be given twice
over. The principal feature of the chorus is the
elderly sweet-faced lady in the sopranos who
sings with such energy and go, beating time
eagerly with her head. She must be nearly as old
as Patti, I should say. Rumor says that she was
once a distinguished prima-donna.
“Ingomar” following “Fedora,” shows Nance
O’Neil in a quiet part of two frocks only, that
allows her no opportunity to make scenes, and
let her voice out. Consequently, she moves
heavily about as the fair young maid in Greek
robes, and wears an air of awkwardness when-
ever there is nothing much for her to do. A
novelty in her impersonation is that she no
longer swishes up and down the stage with the
loud swish that marked her in other plays.
Also, she scarcely ever turns her back
on the house. The discontinuance of the
former trick is due to the skimpy, silent white
robe, unaided by silk linings, that couldn’t be
made to swish for love or money. And the latter,
the back attitude, has been abandoned probably
because the back view of a big, plump woman in
Greek white cashmere would raise no {esthetic
thrills in the jaded modern bosom. If only for
these reasons it is interesting to see her Parthenia
and know that she can get through an evening
without the two characteristics that have marked
her career hitherto. Magda, Camille, Fedora, all
her silk-lined heroines—how they rushed, how
they tore, how they swished !
By the way, Nance’s favorite entry is a thing
worth noting. Have you observed how she loves
to come in, covered with an enormous cloak,
generally black, with a gorgeous flowered pattern,
a high collar reaching past her ears, and her head
tossed back imperiously. Entering rapidly, she
takes her reception with her cloak a little loosened
as her body gives several large bends. And then,
ere the eye has time to grasp the cloak properly,
she is out of it and has flung it aside across a chair.
Women who like to “copy” things find thf
brief visions so tantalising that they are obliged
to come again for another glimpse.
As Parthenia, Nance wears first a white robe
with pink flowers in her hair and at her neck.
Her hair is plaited tightly round her head, and
her features look heavier and older under this
trying coitfure. In the last act she dons bright
flowing yellow, with red flowers in her belt. Mrs.
Henry Bracy, as Parthenia’s mother, looks cold
and homeless in dark, sleeveless brown ; and Miss
Rica Allen is the best-suited female in the play,
with red bands in her dark hair, and a picturesque
robe. Speaking of clothes, a little man in very
little pale-yellow singlet with a frightened expres-
sion, looks as if he had arisen hastily in the night
to get away from a fire. It quite hurts one’s
feelings when Nance turns to this forlorn-looking
being and begs money from him to pay her father’s
ransom.
Harry Plimmer’s Ingomar relieved Sydney of
an awful pressure—the threat of M'Kee Rankin
to play Ingomar himself. Harry wears, with
commendable grace, two pigtails, tied with red
ribbon, and a forest of beard, but he walks like a
Johnny, in spite of his wolfskin clothing. Joseph
Came, looking real thrilling in black hair and a
scarlet mantle, plays the Tim arch with a muffled
voice and stiff gesture. And Athol wood, as Poly-
dor, rants so violently from a badly-assumed
old-man larnyx that one can’t help doubting if a
miser would give away such an unnecessary lot of
voice.
Young Sweeny, looking picturesque at the
right-hand comer, reminded me of an ingenuous
little episode that happened lately. Two little
stage-struck sisters were taken behind to be in-
troduced to their adored Nance. As the
party stood talking in the green-room, handsome
Harry passed through. Instantly the eyes of
the sisters grew round and eager. Staring after
the retreating figure they gazed in hushed silence,
until one burst out ecstatically right in Nance’s
face, “ That was the loveliest thing in Queen
Elizabeth 1” The queeu smiled, and took it like
a woman.
At the Tivoli the Georgia Magnet is the attrac-
tion. “ Little ” Georgia is an ill-fitting epithet, for
the Magnet looks just an ordinary-sized woman,
dressed in white satin with huge red flowers
in her hair ala Jap. Every night her manager
calls a committee from the audience. A
study in masculine bashfulness follows. On
Monday night it was a long time before any one
moved. At last Mr. Percival Waddy rose from
his seat and marched down through theD.C. with
the air of a man who has a sacred mission to per-
form and who'll do it, by the gods. Then Mr.
■ Charlie Bull slowly emerged from his velvet chair
and passed out amid loud applause. Two Jack
Tars waddled up from the stalls, Mr. Alfred
Milson appeared from somewhere, and after a con-
siderable time the circle of chairs was filled.
Once on the platform, however, marvellous to
see how bashfulness fled ! The Committee’s time
was agreeably taken up with clasping black-eyed
Georgia in its arms, lifting her, then failing to lift
her. At their first essay she was lightly danced
up off her feet. Then she would cry, in a loud,
twangy voice, “ Now, get a furrum grasp and lift
me as neigh as ever you ceau.” Nobody succeeded
in lifting her an inch after she said that. Jack
Tar rushed at her with a contemptuous air, seized
her in his arms, and began a heated struggle. It
ended in his retiring, crestfallen and ashamed.
Some of her tricks come near failing, but as soon
as she shows signs of being worsted she cries:
“ Theank you vurry much, that will deo ! ” And
the investigator is obliged to unhand her
promptly.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“First night of ‘Trovatore’ a huge, cheery
crowd packed the Princess. The Maddens, with-
out one of their decorative daughters, made rather
a dull appearance. Madame, pale and fagged, in
sky-blue gown and powder-pink cape, brought her
mother, Mrs. Stephen, in all-black. This white-
haired old lady likes to take a hand in vice-regal
junketings and at smart race-meetings, &c., and
gets herself boosted upon a bench for a better
view of a ‘pretty finish.’ (N.B. —She’s a great-
grandmother, so there’s something in Australian
air, after all.) Aides Nevill and Campbell, in
their pale-blue revers, looked like faint pencil-
sketches in comparison with the theatre staff
trimmed effectively with cerise.
“ Most of the human gems present glittered in
the boxes. Nellie Stewart, in bediamonded black,
had a pancake of plaits atop, flat as a Chow’s.
Round the base she sported a circular, mop-
like halo; but pray don’t gloat over these
details, girls. It’s a coiffure nut meant for non-
theatrical heads. Mrs. Menzies (Dorothy Vane)
looked nice in black, with lily-of-the-valley in her
low-down tresses. Mdme. Slapoffski, much
petted by the Conservatorium clique, sat in the
d.c. with Mrs. Pinschof (Mdme. Wiedermann)
and party. The newcomer’s reddish blouse clashed
with her warm-colored thatch, piled in a touzled
heap.
“ Cecilie Lorraine, who sang soprano Leonora’s
music first night, is blonde and buxom, with a
shortish nose, a low-cut bodice, and well-fed facial
expression. Her first gown, of greeny brocade
strewn with black lovers’ knots set in pink haloes,
looked a heavy garment to trail about, and neither
the wearer nor Maud Harrington (Inez) in white,
brocaded with black, seem expert drapery-car-
riers. Miss Lorraine’s sweet, bird-like voice is
not phenomenally strong, though Nellie Stewart
says it reminds her of Melba’s. Contralto Agnes
Jansen, as the old gipsy, put plenty of fire into
her songs and scooped most applause.
“Can’t say yet whether feminine hearts will
beat high at a glance from Tenor Salvi. He’s
dark, with a hooked nose and a cynical smile, but
don’t conjure up a swarthy, sinister vision—lie is
too plump and placid for that. He is more effec-
tive as the gipsy’s operatic son than as Leonora’s
lover. I liked his wedding suit, lilac-velvet tunic
with puffings and slashings of ivory satin and
heliotrope hose —it went so well with the bride’s
white gown. Not a bad idea if bridegrooms of
to-day were encouraged to face the altar in some-
thing more artistic than tail-coats and tweed
pants. The chance of looking really nice for once
—or twice —in their lives might coax a few beauty-
loving bachelors into matrimony. Haven’t you
noticed how some men snap eagerly at every ex-
cuse for getting into uniforms, kilts, knee-
breeches, &c. ?
“ Baritone Max Eugene is a German, and looks
it. His sour glances at his rival are exactly those
of one’s own German friend discussing the Boer
war. Then his smile, when Leonora agrees to
marry him, is own cousin to that of Herr What’s-
his-name, scenting a successful herring salad as he
passes the kitchen-door.

plauding his sometime partner’s success, observers
routed up a mare’s nest and surmised that the
alleged breach between \V. and M. must have been
a put-up job, arranged for business reasons.
“1/ Madame Slapoffski is unconnected with the
opera co. at the outset, ’tis said that her inclusion
is only a matter of time. The Conservator! um
Heads declare that Chief Musgrove intended to
engage his pritna donna in Australia. Conductor
Slapoffski knew that this scheme would end in
smoke, so he brought along his wife, in the sure
and certain hope of an engagement. That’s why
Mdme. S. was so desperately anxious to catch on
(which she did) at Marshall Hall’s concert, t’other
Saturday.
“ Nellie Stewart looks blooming as ever, though
a shade stouter than of old. Her knockabout suit
seems to be a trained black gown, chatelaine at
her belt, crinoline hat tilted up at one side and
down at t’other, and a diamond ‘ Nell ’ brooch.
“Theatre-goers were agreeably surprised by the
sympathetic and quiet tenderness which crept
into Rose Musgrove’s acting in ‘The Only Way.’
The severe illness of the young actress’s "mother,
by the way, is causing her family great anxiety.
“The Madden clan is just now under a little
cloud, in consequence of an insulting remark
made by the» impetuous Parliamentary brother
Frank. In a debate in the House Frank Madden
lost his temper and accused the Radical Higgins
of attending Fenian meetings, and turning his
political coat for the sake of the £.'loo a year
salary. Whereupon Dr. Nick O’Donnell, a pro-
minent Irishman, rose up in wrath and made a
dignified but scorching remark about religion and
social ambition, which remark it would be most
unwise to print in full.
• ‘ The Dowager at ‘ Cliveden ’ is about the only
entertainer in Melbourne who worries about
novelties in invitation cards. Those sent out for
Wednesday’s at-home (Oct. 17) at Cliveden are
illuminated with a teapot and cups, with crotchets
and quavers rising from the steam of the spout, in
one corner of the card, while decorating the other
is a cleff and what looks like a b-sharp—so that
there’s no room left for the usual R.S. V.P.
“ Minister Salmon brought his bride to Mel-
bourne in the Customs yacht Lady Loch. She
was taken round Parliament House in a wonderful
dress of lilac-colored silk, with a picture hat of the
same hue, setting off her dark charms to perfec-
tion. She looked so fascinating that, at that par-
ticular time, if her husband had wanted to carry a
Bill to impose a tax on bachelors, not even Gillies
and Peacock would have said a word against it.
After being shown over the building, the bride
was taken out into the beautiful Parliament Gar-
dens to read the newspaper paragraphs about the
wedding, under the shade of the Federal oak
planted by Parkes in 1891. The new Mrs. Salmon,
with wealth, comeliness, and intelligence, may, if
she likes, be something of a personal force, because
the ordinary run of politicians’ wives in Victoria
are lamentable errors of judgment. Their hus-
bands seem to have married them when tired
after an all-night sitting.
“ S’Malcolm's manner of entertaining Mus-
grove’s song-birds and a few Social Somebodies,
on Friday afternoon, was a droll one. All the
ordinaries arrived early, and sat round the
Council-chambei, while S’Malcolm gushed greet-
ings at the various stars. Nellie Stewart, in black
with Turkish-delight sleeves of cream lace and
turquoise jewels, came with Mr. Musgrove, who
carried a showy silver crooked stick. All the
operatic stars are fleshy ; Miss Lorraine is the
bast looking. Conductor ‘Slap’ was there,
fresh and fat and important. The gathering,
led by the Mayor, moved into the gallery and
listened to Una Bourne (in a pink frock) making
music, as a change to solemn organ strains—
during which all the theatrical stars waned, and
threatened to finally go out. They did steal out
at the first opportunity, after a long vacant stare
at the hundreds of chairs in the body of the hall.
They had tea in a monotonously grave way, and
then decamped. George Allan, of the Sing-Song
Warehouse, was almost the only one who had
anything to say to the company. The rest of the
gathering stood afar off and stared unblinkingly
at the new arrivals. Mrs. Gillott was surrounded
by felicitating friends. ‘ I suppose you’re tired of
hearing theße congratulations,’ insinuated one
rather learned lady from Melb. University to the
new Mayoress. ‘No,’ was the candid and truth-
ful reply, ‘ I love to hear them.
“ Blamire Young, the Melb. poster artist (his
work on the hoardings is not his only form of
advt. business) delivered a lecture a fewnights ago
on ‘ Impressionism and Poster Art ’ to a mixed
audience, comprising a few of the Naicest. Bla-
mire was painfully nervous, butjie managed to
escape any awful embarrassment until he came to
the subject of National Gallery encouragement
of local art, and stammered, ‘lt would—er—be a
pity to see Rob Thomas’s —I mean Tom Roberts’s
—splendid canvas, ‘ Milking the Rams ’ leave the
country.’ Before he could give any further par-
ticulars some dear girls began to giggle, and the
giggle expanded into a shriek, in which even the
men joined. ‘I mean Shearing the Rams ’ cor-
rected Blamire. But during the rest of the
lecture, every nowand then an explosive sniff told
how deep an impression had been made on
woman’s sense of humor.
“By the way, some songs and reciting’ were
tacked on to the ambitious Poster gentleman’s
entertainment, and among the amateurs who took
the floor was Miss Una Russell, the golden-haired
best pupil of Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis’s elocution
school. Critical people cried ‘ bravo !’ at the end
of her turn. She spoke ‘ The Passions ’ charm-
ingly, with any amount of intelligence, the sweet-
est of voice inflections, and the kindling eye that
suggests a soul. Miss Russell is the graceful
young girl who was rumored to be going to appear
somewhere as Parthenia in an “ Ingomar ” per-
formance after Nance O’Neil had dealt with the
character, but the show never came off. It seems,
though, that she is to play -the heroine in a one-
consecutive-night production of a brand new
drama, ‘The Wreck of the Inverness,’ by Mrs.
G.B.W.L. Which interesting event will come to
paso (D. V.) at the little Gaiety Theatre—of all
places!—before the end of the month. Miss
Athena Claudius and the manageress are in the
cast, so the assisting amateurs will not want for
helping hands to pull them through their trouble.
“ A belated story of that very short-lived sensa-
tion, the Poster Ball. Rupert Clarke, who
rather prides himself on the possession of a com-
mercial instinct, gave in his little advt. (there was
a little cheque attached to it, of course) as Ruperts-
wood Factory Butter. If the name of his
baronetorial estate was likely to add another half-
penny a pound to the price of his butter
Rupert wanted it set forth in all its glory. But
when his sister found the family pride in danger
of getting spotted with grease she quickly changed
the name of brother Rupert’s advt. exhibit to
‘ Waterlily Butter.’
“ The Melbourne Canterbury Pilgrimage affair
in celebration of the 500th Anniversary of
Chaucer’s death, on 25th inst., is off. Instead
Prof. Morris, Prof. Tucker, and other pedagogues
have hired a hall, and are going to spout their
opinions about Chaucer to an audience chiefly
pedantic. But these wordy celebrations are
weary. Let someone propose to celebrate Chaucer
by publicly reading some of his bluest produc-
tions unexpurgated. The reader would probably
be locked up by the police, but there would be more
real Chaucer in the thing than in the spoutings
of the urofessional fuzzy-wuzzies.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
Victorian damsels need not feel anxious
because Dr. Carty Salmon sought a rose from Syd-
ney’s garden of feminine loveliness. Wherever it
be, the beauty of the Australian girl, when en-
hanced by the P. D. corset, is absolutely irre-
sistible. When the P.D. knocks at the door of
even the least susceptible man’s heart he sur-
renders at discretion. He is lost. The girl has
“got him dead.”
