PERSONAL ITEMS.
Thomas Bowe died at Bacchus Marsh,
Vic., the other day, aged 102. The long Bowe.
Tom Roberts is painting a portrait of Gov.
Beauchamp to the order of the trustees of N.S.W.
National Gallery.
The late Mr. John Rae, Sydney’s first
town-clerk, who lately died, left £60,987, which
by his will is divided pretty equally among his
four children.
Lieut. Duff, who comes as one of Hope-
toun’s aides, was for a time in Sydney during his
father’s Governorship from 1893 to 1895. The
Duffs are of an old Liberal stock—Gladstoneites
and Home Rulers.
Gov.-General Hopetoun’s early ambition,
it is said, was to marry the lady who is now
Duchess of York. It had gone the length of being
discussed by Her Majesty, but Clarence courted
and York married her.
In death as in life, Henry Parkes shapes
badly from capitalistic standpoints. The State
Btatue of him was secondhand, the bursary to his
honor is a failure, and his grave at Faulconbridge
is dilapidated and overgrown with weeds.
Victorian ex - Attorney - General Isaacs,
lately returned from London, where he has been
arguing the case for Melb. Tramway Co., in
the action against Fitzroy Council. Always a
capital talker, Isaacs’ clever little chai-acter
sketches of eminent men he met in England
are keenly relished by Victorian politi-
cians. One anecdote illustrates the small
jokes that can be made by men with big
minds. One night, at dinner, Isaacs sat
near a great judge who would, he knew,
probably be one of the Appeal Court before
which he would appear. “I have heard,
Mr. Isaacs,” said the learned bigwig, “that
your brief in the case you are in is
marked at 3000 guineas.” “Quite true,
m’lud,” said the lawyer. “Then, sir,” said
the Judge, with a calf-bound equity smile,
“ when you have finished your argument I
shall say to you, ‘Mr. Isaacs, the Court
thanks you for your most valuable argument,’
and you, sir, will know what I mean.”
The £lOOO each on the heads of Jimmy
and Joe Governor raises these worthies to the
starring rank of Morgan, Clarke, Gilbert, Hall,
and Dunn. Morgan, Hall, and Gilbert were shot
dead ; Clarke and Dunn were captured and
hanged.
Mr. A. W. Howitt (son of William and
MaryHowitt), Commissioner of Audit in Victoria,
retires from the public service at the end of the
year. Mr. Howitt led the relief party which
discovered John King, survivor of the Burke and
Wills expedition.
Colonel Bull and his wife (Vic.) are prob-
ably the oldest married, couple in Australia. They
have been married over 70 years. The Colonel is
94, and whenever he hears that somebody he
knew has died, he says, “ Dear me, how on earth
did he manage to do it ? ”
Squatter Hugh Macdonald, who died lately
at Duntulin Station, at the age of 72, was a part-
ner with A. A. Dangar in Myall Creek Station in
the early days. This is the station which, in the
hands of Henry Dangar, was branded in the history
of over 60 years ago as the murder-field of twenty-
eight (that many bodies identified —how many
slaughtered is unknown) men, women and children
of the black race.
■' Chief Justice Darley is not quite entitled
by length of service to a pension, and those, there-
fore, who push him forward as first N.S.W. State
Governor are engaged on a scheme very much like
that which Chief Justice Griffith’s admirers are
trying to engineer in Queensland. If the State
Governor’s salary be cut down to £2OOO by Parlia-
ment, Judge Darley’s friends will endeavour to
have the full Chief Justice’s pension conferred at
once.

Rev. T. Howard Smith and wife are on
their way from Thursday Island to Sydney. Mrs.
Smith, a daughter of Mr. Edmund Burton, late
editor of Sydney Mail, was reported among the
massacred in Pekin, and her reappearance has
something of the character of a resurrection.
“Rolf Boldrewood ” is said to have got
from that brilliant bushman and gold-prospector,
Phil. Saunders, a narrative of the latter’s experi-
ences in Australia and elsewhere. Mr. Saunders’
adventures should make bright reading, especially
as he wields a pen well enough to put them into
good shape.
A Sydney lady is sending the much-
boosted Baden-Powell an Australian cabbage-tree
hat. These is nothing like a cabbage-tree to
make you bald ; but as B.P. is already hairless as
an egg he can safely wade in. Now which insane
Jingo will send the man of wind a stock-whip to
complete the kit ?

Investigation lias shown the number
of names of dead men which still remain on
the electoral-rolls of N.S.W., but this is a
tritie compared with the startling dis every
made by a Govt, audit-officer some years
ago. He was inspecting the books'of a
high public official to whom was entrusted
the paying of pensions in one of the pro-
vinces. The suspicions of the inspector
were aroused by a discrepancy or two, but
the officer concerned, a society light, was
so well known and trusted that it was
i hard to believe anything was seriously
wrong. Unluckily for the inspector, he
was in bed, ill, when the audit-man
j made his visit, and consequently the
latter was without the plausible ex-
planations which had before stood him
(the pension-man) in good stead. So
the inspector thought he would pay a
visit to the sick man. “Did you
know, ”he began, ‘ ‘ that W illiam Anderson
is dead ? ” “ What! ” broke in the other
“ not old Anderson ?—not Billy? Poor
old chap, I will miss his kind, old face. And
he’s dead ! —dead ! Well, well, we must all
coiny to it. He was pretty old.” “He was,” drily
replied the inspector; “ and he died in ’B3. He’d
be 119 to-day.” But as his pension did not die
with poor old Bill, nor with several others like
him, the official got five years.
Gresley Lukin, now editor of Wellington
(M.L.) Post, passed through Sydney last week
and was welcomed by many old friends. He had
come to Australia on a melancholy errand—to say
farewell to his brother, George Lukin, the much-
esteemed old-time Queensland P.M., now in a
hopeless state of health.
.Purves, Q. 0., is said to be likely to go
for one of the Vic. seats in the Federal Parlia-
ment. Should Purves go—he probably will not
go—what colors will he sport? The red cap of
Australian Republicanism, or the red jacket of
Imperial Jingoism ? Within the past few years
the truculent barrister has spouted both ways.
Grand-opera artists are rarely con-
spicuously gifted in the matter of beauty, and
consequently the very obvious absence of un-
earthly loveliness in Musgrove’s Grand Opera Co.
is not astonishing. The male members are too,
too solid in substance, and it must have cost
Muzzy a fortune to bring them out if they were
paid for as a bulk shipment.
Sam Gillott, who is to be Melbourne’s new
Mayor, is David S.yme’s solicitor. His firm was
formerly Gillott, Croker and Snowden, but the
firm somehow split up—it is said over the gigantic
Speight-Syme libel action. Gillott is very
wealthy, but childless —a hearty, good-natured,
sound-headed man. He is likely to be one of the
best Mayors Melbourne has ever had.
The English police are still exercised over
the identity of the supposed Australian, “ Walter
Morley Martin,” who died recently at the Dart-
ford Lunatic Asylum, after a short stay at a local
hotel. All that could be gathered from him was
that his real name was not Martin, and that he
had relatives in Sydney. He was tall, well-
looking, and blonde-moustached, and had plenty
of money.
Pastor Sugden, the big, brainy, cheery fel-
low who has just taken his seat on Melbourne
University Council, is always doing something to
shock the Wesleyan mind. He will think for
himself, sings lively songs, and, alas—smokes !
Smokes a pipe ! In the Wesleyan compartment
of the Heavenly Kingdom there is very little sal-
vation to be found by the parson who colors a
meerschaum.
Thomas Bent—the rejected of Portland
and Brighton—addressing the electors of latter
district, t’other day, besought them not to
disgrace themselves by returning a gentleman
to Parliament when there was a reliable
market-gardener to hand. This is the time when
Thomas’s old commendable occupation stands him
in good stead. Thomas the Land Boomer is not
mentioned now, but Thomas the ’orny-’anded
ton of soil is all to the fore.
Big John Forrest says that he will resign
the Westralian Premiership early next year. Con -
sidering that it is growing almost impossible to
borrow money, except at exorbitant interest, that
a huge sum must be raised unless the Coolgardie
water scheme is to be left unfinished and all the
expenditure on it wasted, and that the shiversome
time is coming near when that affair must either
prove a success or the record failure of Australia,
this is as good a time as any to get from under.
Murray Smith’s retirement from Vic. poli-
tics has been accounted for by three different
yarns. Tlie first story, which had a lady in the
case, was a pure fiction, founded on the giddiness
of quite another soit of Freetrader than M.S.
Then it was said that the little man had merely
decided to snuff himself out because the Cause
was lost. And now The Bulletin regrets to hear
some mention of alarming physical illness as the
reason for Mr. Murray Smith declining a renewal
of his Parliamentary lease.
When the first of Kipling’s “ Stalky and
Co.” stories appeared in Windsor Magazine
Prof. Haslam, of Canterbury (M.L.) College,
rushed into print to inform the public that he had
been an assistant master at Westward Ho in
Kipling’s time, and how very “stalky” Kipling
had been, and so on. But as the series progressed,
and Kipling proceeded to record his conviction
t! at the assistant masters were mostly a set of
fools, and generally objectionable, the amiable
Prof, was sorry he spoke.
Gov. Lamington’s peregrinations through
Queensland are to take book form. Some of his
views on the men in Queensland politics are
said to be startling. He will be far away when
the book is published, and he doesn’t hope to come
back as Gov. -General, His reputation in Aus-
tralia is wholly unlike what it was among his own
set in England. There he was a sort of Randolph
Churchill Conservative freelance with a Radical
diapason ; in Queensland he has been a combina-
tion of ladies’-man and small social tootler.
President Lackey, of N.S. W. Leg. Coun-
cil, touched 70 the other day. His marble bust
was placed in position in the Council chamber a
short time back, and the President himself said
that the incident was ominous. He has been an
ideal President. His bearing in Council proceed-
ings is absolutely devoid of friction. He is every
man’s friend, and every man’s opinion is handled
tenderly. And yet he has been 40 years toddling
about the two Houses, and was in his time a lusty
battler for one opinion and one cause only.
In N.S.W. Assembly last week Jawbone
Neild remarked that the proceedings of Parlia-
mentary committees should be conducted in a
manner not derogatory to the dignity of the
House. In the current military inquiry, for
instance, he said “ you had the chairman conduct-
ing the proceedings with a big pipe in his mouth,
and a pair of dirty boots on the table, and this
was the man who had formerly taken some
trouble to explain that his position as chairman
was analagous to that of a Supreme Court Judge.”
“S.” : The early death of Stephen Crane
has provoked appreciative paragraphs and sketches
about the young American all over the English-
speaking world. The writers gush most strenu-
ously about the author of ‘ ‘ The Red Badge of
Courage,” and wonder how he, a comparative
youth who had seen nothing of war, succeeded so
wonderfully in depicting its horror and its move-
ment, and in conveying so vividly the whole
atmosphere of the struggle and the slaughter. All
this makes me bitter, for it seems to me that the
source of Crane’s inspiration, his style, and his
knowledge, is so obviously Ambrose Bierce. Bierce
has given on the small canvas of his wonderful
short stories of war all that Crane gave in his
novel, and matter and manner in the younger man
bear so striking a resemblance to Bierce’s sin-
gularly original and graphic work, that discerning
readers greeted “The Red Badge” with the ex-
clamation, “Bierce!’’and all thought the Cali-
fornian was experimenting under an alias.
The old difficulty of insuring valuables against
fire and burglars has come up again. It is, however,
becoming an acknowledged fact that it is impossible to
arrange a satisfactory insurance. The Sydney Safe De-
posit has overcome the difficulty by offering to secure to
insurers the articles themselves in their fire and burglar
proof vaults, and thus, by the payment of a comparatively
small premiuin in the way of rent, avoiding all possibility
of loss. The system is most interesting, and the manage-
ment invite a call for inspection. The Safe Deposit is
situated at the back of Paling’s, off George-street.*

publicly announced that, though he had hot pota-

cooked by an atheist who lived next door.

When J. A. Froude, the historian, visited
Melb. 15 years ago, Mayor G. D. Carter gave him
a public luncheon in the Town Hall, indicating
a representative of the Argus, Froude said he
would much like to know who that was, because
he was the image of Joe Chamberlain, at that time
a noted radical with a three-aeres-and-a-cow scheme
for the regeneration of the masses. The required
information having been given, the historian ex :
pressed surprise that a journalist with that phy-
siognomy should bear the Conservative, brand.
Perhaps the scribe was a radical in disguise. At
all events, he subsequently transferred his alle-
giance to the Age.
Dear Bulletin, —Apropos a recent “cor-
rection.” It is recorded in authentic histories
that the father of John Pascoe Fawkner reached
Australia as one of the bond, with his wife and
son (who were free), in the ship Calcutta, which
anchored for several months in Port Phillip, off
what is now called Sorrento, in 1803. Col. Collins
afterwards removed the settlement-to Hobart, on
the ground that there was no fresh water in Port
Phillip. It was from the Sorrento settlement
that Wm. Buckley, “ the wild white man,” who
was a private soldier, ran away to escape a punish-
ment he had incurred. Fawkner, jun., met him
32 years later, soon after he had been discovered
among the blacks at Queenscliff by John Batman.
—Rex.
The Bulletin guesses that Dorrington’s
book of stories, to be published from this office
next month under the title of “ Castro’s Last
Sacrament,” will make literary Australians open
their eyes. No one who has merely read “ Alba
Dorion’s” yarns singly in this paper can have an
idea how good they are when taken together.
They will undoubtedly rank as the best book of
short stories yet printed in this country. Other
well-known men have given closer pictures of
Australian life, with more photographic detail;
but Dorrington, in losing something of the Aus-
tralian point of view, gains the universal. He
writes with a style, too ; and his experience covers
many fields. A considerable portion of the book
is composed of stories yet unprinted. The price
is 4s. 6d. post free from Bulletin office.
A somewhat eccentric individual unfavor-
ably known in old-time Vie. Tory politics
figures in a new and interesting light. The ex-
celebrity recently entered the house of a neighbor
to whom he is only known by sight, unannounced
and without apology, and, finding the family about
to sit down to dinner, calmly jumped the seat of
N.S. WALES’ OLDEST CIVIL-SERVANT.
honor at the head of the table. Then to the con-
sternation of those present, who sat back and
glared speechlessly, he began to examine and
criticise the joint in low, irascible tones, cursing
the cook back to the nether depths from whence
the devil sent her to ruin the digestions of men.
“I tell you, madam, you’ll have to get rid of that
; woman !” he cried, threatening the trembling
housewife with the carving-knife. Then suddenly
a new light dawned in his face, he glanced from
one face to another, his frightened eyes wandered
round the room, the carver fell from his trembling
hand, and moaning, “Good God ! I’m in the wrong
house ! ” he turned and bolted from the place,
leaving his big hat in the hall and his gamp in the
stand to be used as evidence against him.

Aboriginalities.
We sat down to dinner in the way-back
pub., and the landlady placed on the table a roast
“poult ”of some kind. The energetic little man
opposite offered himself as carver and was
accepted. He made a slash at the leg first, but,
to our dismay, the edge of the knife turned and
slid off that leg as a piece of tin would off the leg
of a chair. The carver paused in astonishment
for a second, then hitched his shoulders, and made
a jab at the breast. Same result. “Might I ask,
ma’am,” he said turning to the hostess, “what
this is ? ” “ Why, it’s a black duck ; my son shot
it yesterday down at the river.” “Shot it,”
echoed the little man in surprise ; “might I ask
what with ? ” “With a gun, of course,” snapped
the landlady. “Well, well; it’s wonderful,” the
carver returned in a musing tone “ I should have
thought he shot it with a thunderbolt. Your son
must have a very powerful pom-pom, madam.”
And he sadly relinquished the instruments.

A new phase of the alien question. The
Indians, who now form a fairly large percentage
of the Richmond River, N.S.W., population,
have begun dairying. And Lismore Chamber of
Commerce is moving for legislation for special
supervision over the dairies of these people,
although there is no reason to suppose that they
will not carry on the industry in as cleanly a man-
ner as most of the whites. But this is where the
shoe pinches. The Indians would never have got
a foothold in this country had the farming classes
refused to patronise them as hawkers. But
now that they are trenching on the farmers’ own
province the latter begin to kick, although they
cared nothing for the injury done to the store-
keepers by the hawkers’ competition. Same old
No. 1!
• * « •
After all, w',at marvellous country much
of the Centralian desert is ! The Lower Darling,
a sandy wilderness for four or five years past,
has had less than 7in. of rain this year; but the
showers came just at the right time, and there
have in consequence been hundreds of thousands
of acres of as fine grass-land as there is in Aus-
tralia—grass that will fatten like lucerne and
clover. But it is wasting; droughts have reduced
flocks to a quarter of their normal size, and the
owners can’t buy stock at the current price. A
few score miles further back, where some of the
timely showers were missing, the old desolation
prevails unbroken. Less than 4in. of rain scat-
tered over February, March, April, and May
made all the difference.

“Drought” : Re drought item about the
Warri bore, N.W. corner N.S.W. The con-
ditions there are fairly favorable compared
to what some of the bore-contractors have
to put up with in S.A. I know one con-
tractor who has to cart chaff out to feed
horses 170 miles from Hergott Springs, and has to
cart water 10 miles. Also, the horses have to go
20 miles from water to where the wood is, and
there is no feed for hundreds of miles.

A N.W. Queensland correspondent writes,
Sept. 24:
Have often heard reports of drought in N.Q., but no
idea can be formed as to the state of the country until
you take a trip. After leaving Hughenden, going towards
Oloncurry, nothing but heaps of dead sheep and catt e
meet the eye, and the few existing are walking skeletons.
On the country I have seen, from Hughenden to Olon-
curry, there is no more grass than on a doorstep ; and if
rain does not fall shortly everyone will have to abandon
the place. For the past year people have been sinking
shafts in the bed of the Flinders and Gilbert rivers for
water —about 12ft. deep ; and even this source is giving
out. Without rain there will soon be no “ Gulf country.”
♦ * • *
The Upper Hunter blacks once planned
the extermination of the whites. As a begin-
ning they murdered five employes
on Wattingbark Station, near the
Barrington. One of these had evi-
dently almost escaped. He ran
round the stockyard, as the imprint
of his hand in blood on the corner-
post showed, and got across the
river, making for Rawdon Vale, 3J
miles off. He probably thought
himself secure when, right in a gap
overlooking Rawdon Vale, he came
upon some niggers watching that
place, and was promptly dis-
patched. The whites banded to-
gether, but never caught any of
the slayers, though they came, one
day, upon the whole tribe swimming
in a big water-hole.
m * * *
One striking thing about the Way back
just at present is that, despite a good season and
abundance of grass and herbage in some parts,
the dust and sand storms are almost, if not quite, as
severe as when the country was all a wilderness.
Grass and the soft stuff have only appeared where
the surface is more or less protected by scrub of
some kind; where that has been destroyed—by
drought or by removal for firewood and fencing,
or through being cut down unskilfully for fodder
in drought time —the surface is more barren,
because more wind-swept, than ever it was.

*“ Drover ” : Recently met a man em-
ployed on a station in the vicinity of Croydon
(Q.). Told me he was getting 7s. per week, under
verbal agreement for three years, and that at end
of first year the boss was to give him a moke, but
if he left before expiration of his time he was to
refund £5 for fare up. On same station are
several others working at from £7 to £9 per
annum. Talk about white slavery !

Notice nailed round a Maori pah near
Picton (M.L.):
We, the elect of Te Aroha Nui, Waikawa, hereby give
notice that under the seventh rule of Jehovah men
women and children have strictly agreed not to take
spirits of any kind ; also, that any persons intoxicated,
or having any bottles of spirits on them, shall not be
allowed to enter our gates or round about.

From Tasmania :
At Launceston police court to-day Wm. Jones pro-
ceeded against W. 0. Malcolm for leaving his hired ser-
vice without leave. Defendant, who is 22 years of age,
was engaged as a farm hand for £5 a year and a suit of
clothes. The magistrate severely criticised the small
wage, and dismissed the case.

“ Peter K.” : Re strange tastes of animals.
I have a small spaniel very fond of cheese ; he
will also eat apples and grapes, and on two occa-
sions have known him drink a saucerful of weak
whisky and water without any after-effect save a
half-hour snooze. Also had a cat which lived
almost exclusively upon vegetables.

“ A.G. ” : Talk about Chow cruelties !
An old Araluen miner assured me that, in the
50’s, he and four mates caught a Chinky, pulled
out his pigtail, rammed him into a hollow log,
and sent the lot rolling down a gully 400 ft.
deep. The “ haythen ” had been sneaking
round after their wash-dirt. The same
party, he said, once surprised a Chow
m the bottom of their shaft. It
happened to be a “ duffer,” and they
“ just filled her in.” What price that
Chow’s chance of getting to the
“ Flowery Land ? ”

“ Haredale ” : “Alia” (8.,
18/8/’00) is correct about goats being
effective eradicators of briar. Goats
will eat and thrive on weeds and
shrubs that are poisonous to cattle
and sheep ; and will do well generally
on food that other animals won’t
taste, but will not thrive upon even
the best grass alone.

“ Coogee ” : Re animal craving
for salt. Horses that can’t otherwise
get it will hang around old huts
and camps, licking the ground which
is saltish with the water used in
boiling salt beef.

Re gulls breeding away from
the sea (“ P.K.” B. 29/9/’00). On Lake Eyre
there are myriads of gulls, and in 1895 I was on
the lower Bulloo when in flood, and found any
quantity of gulls’ eggs on the islands formed by
the flood waters on the plains below Bulloo
Downs stations. There were four eggs in each
nest —or rather dent in the ground with a few bits
of grass around. The blacks said that they
‘‘ penty times gettum that fellow copinga (egg)
when big water come up.” And this at 600 or 700
miles inland.
* * * *
“ Wat Tell” : Travelling up north once,
stone-broke and horribly tired and hungry, I
struck a bush pub. In the bar was a chap lolling
at his ease in a chair behind the counter. He at
once pushed a bottle towards me and invited me
to help myself. I suggested something to eat
and was told to go inside and take what I liked.
This I did, and when I felt comfortable I began
to look about and enquire. Discovered that the
landlord and his wife and the handy man of the
establishment were all “paralytic” drunk, that
the place was running itself, and that my friend
behind the bar was only a chance caller like
myself. It’s not often a dead-beat strikes such a
bonanza.
* * * *
“ Talk about ‘dealing with ’ the darkies !”
said Jones. “Me an’a mate was onct splittin’
on the Barcoo. We were pestered with the black
varmints cornin’ after tucker, and we decided to
give ’em a start. Well, one day four of ’em came
along and wanted tea and sugar. We had a log
open, with two wedges in, one at each end of her.
1 told the nigs, that we hadn’t enough left to go
round, and that when I’d drop the bag down the
crack they were to dive in their hands for it—the
one that got it to keep the lot. Bill stood ready
at one end of the log, and I at the other, and
when I said ‘ go ’ the darkies dived, and out came
those wedges like a shot. Yell ! I should smile !
Two years afterwards their skelingtons was found,
still fixed in the log, and that s the last I ever
heard of the biz.”

“ Lar ” throws a side-light on the nigger-
and-opium question :
If “Myall” inquires he will find that I am not far out
re quantity ot opium imported into W. Queensland.
Opium is almost universally smoked amongst the niggers,
and (granted they are well-fed) opium in moderation is
no more harmful than the vile tobacco they smoke.
From a station-verandah recently I could see tiny lights
dotted about the paddocks—niggers smoking opium with
the ingenious lamps they make for the purpose from a
stone ink-bottle, a piece of cane, and a broken glass-
bottle. A good many Queensland whites secretly sell and
smoke opium. I knew a coach-driver who was an in-
veterate opium-eater, yet remained always fat and jolly
on it Some years ago—time of the shearers’ strike —I
had to press niggers into the service to shear, and had to
send to Brisbane for opium for them. The drug was then
275. fid. a small boxful, and I soon found that I was losing
oyer the deal, so I got some burnt sugar and mixed it up
with the opium. The stuff looked all right, but the nig-
gers struck work and began to yabber. The sugar had
made the opium sticky, and they couldn’t smoke it, so I
went to the Ohow gardener on the station, bought him
out of opium (he had about 2 ) boxes of it stowed away
in all sorts of holes and corners), and told him if he didn’t
give me the straight tip about doctoring the stuff he
could roll up and “clear.” Was then informed that cold-
tea was the proper “fake,” and I got that shearing
through all right.

Aii M.P.’s Important Disclosures.
An eminent Queensland legislator, Mr. Andrew
Lang Petrie, M.P. for Toombul, has made some
important disclosures of peculiar interest. When
a reporter recently waited upon Mr. Petrie he
courteously granted the request for a short inter-
view. After speaking of current affairs, Mr.
Petrie, in giving some reminiscences of his life,
said : —“ Many years ago I suffered great discom-
fort from the dull, aching pains of rheumatism
which attacked my shoulders and limbs. Later
on the digestive organs became impaired. The
medical skill available to combat my complaints
gave me only slight relief. My. appetite deserted
me, and though I forced myself to eat I could not
do so with relish ; after meals came a sense of
weight, discomfort, and sleepiness. Headaches
now and again troubled me, and my ill-health
certainly affected my spirits. Noticing Dr.
Williams’ pink pills advertised, I tried them, and
with signal success. In a few weeks my troubles
perceptibly decreased, and before long I found
myself perfectly cured not only of the indigestion,
but of rheumatism also. As it is over two years
since then, and I have not used the pills again,
there is no doubt about the permanency of my cure.
Several of my friends and relatives have obtained
great benefit from them, and I have pleasure in
recommending them to all sufferers from rheuma-
tism and indigestion.”
“ How is it that Dr. Williams’ pink pills cure
so many diseases ? ” is the question often asked.
The answer is that most diseases, although appar-
ently diverse, owe their origin to impoverishep
blood and unstrung nerves. Dr. Williams’ pink
pills by their tonic action enrich the blood and
retone the nerves, thus removing the cause of
disease. They consequently cure neuralgia, bron-
chitis, consumption, St. Vitus’ dance, rickets,
paralysis, rheumatism, sciatica, nervous disorders,
eczema, effects of influenza and fevers, measles,
etc. Sold by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per box, six
boxes fifteen and three, post free, and by chemists
and storekeepers—but mind you ask for Dr.
Williams’. Mr. Petrie’s office and the extensive
works which he controls are situated at Toowong
(Q.).

“ Long-’un eulogises the camel:—
Bulletin- writers and others have just about succeeded
in persuading the jibbing horse to “ git-up ” ; but what
about the obstinate camel ? Old Whalebone isn’t in it
with that smellful animal. Ishmael was the least tired
of our overworked and leg-weary team. A new water-
cask stood alongside his hind-quarters as we saddled him.
After an hour’s fruitless effort to get him on his feet, Jack
tried the live-coal test as a last resort. Ishmael replied
with a wriggle ; and a short, sharp movement of his hock
staved-in the water cask. He was satisfied now, and did
the 35-mile trip for the mail in record time. Returning
rather late next day, attempted short-cut across a pad-
dock to save time. Strapped the fence-wires together
in usual fashion, and, after repeatedly failing to
lead him over, rushed him from behind. No hope !
Ilooshed him down and mounted. Too tired to rise.
Got off, whipped him up, and, being uncommonly
long-legged, mounted standing, and sent him at it.
Result, a graceful hooshta on his own account, a snaky
twist of his neck, and a shower of frothy slime over rider.
Heated and thirsty, I lifted the new water-bag.
It was dry 1 Unstrapped the wires, and sent Ish-
mael down the fence-track, hell-for-leather, to a gate
half-a-mile distant. A quarter-hour's fuming, and pulling
at nose-line ; 10yds. progress ; nearly sundown, and 15
miles from camp. Five bob for a drink. Dragged him
by sheer weight for another hour, walking backwards.
Out came his nose-peg, my heels ploughing through air
and flies. “ Done ! ” (A week previously he’d bitten a
hole through a man’s hand.) Cautiously and trem-
blingly' advanced, and hooshed. Great display of
green filth ; lassooed his nose and mouth, pulled tight,
and, in a new-chum’s sweat of terror, at length refixed
the peg. By sundown, another mile. Famished and
utterly tired of life, I sat on the edge of the red-clay
track in the treeless wilderness, and longed for a
gun for Ishmael and me especially for Ishmael.
I shook my' fist in his face and recited that
Bulletin poem, “ The Australian Adjective.” Then
I collared a blanket, and staggered back four miles to
a boundary-rider’s hut. Returned at sparrow-chirp,
breakfastless, but hoping to reach camp before the
heat further affected the old gent’s temper. Dragged
him half-a-mile to the saddle (same old drag). Mounted ;
calling him a good old chap, &c. No trouble this time ;
jogged along a couple of miles—and subsided comfort-
ably, like the tired woman after a dance, who yawns, and
remarks—“ Thank goodness, home at last 1 ” Hobbled
him short, bade him a disgusted “ good morning,” and
started tramping the last 11 miles to breakfast. A couple
of miles from camp met Jack coming out in search of the
new-chum, with an extra camel. He smiled sarcastically.
He’d bring Mr. Ishmael back all right. He
returned in the evening ; never had such a treat in his
life. Tied him on behind and started. Looked back ;
broken nose-line ; Ishmael grazing a quarter-mile away—
“Hooshta!” Got off and walked towards the latter,
whereupon the other beauty scooted in the opposite
direction. Jack’s description of his day’s outing was
balm for me, but he humped his swag next day, with a
parting thrust—“ If yez want me ter do any more camel-
dri vin’ for yez, yez’ll have ter get ’orses ! ”

“ F.H.” : T’other day a navvy “ out back
of here” pin-pricked a detonator and so r/moved
one of liis hands and most of the other. A pass-
ing teamster thereupon related, how when he
had a “place,” his pigs went wild, and, having no
means of cornering and killing them, but knowing
where they sometimes came to feed, lie stuffed
half-a-dozen potatoes with.a detonator apiece, and
left them in the track of the swine. Later on, he
heard the hoped-for smash, and going to the spot
found any quantity of fat pork lying about on its
back with its head blown off and its jugular rib-

boned. Also, some other pigs were eating it. Of
course teamsters never lie, nor fight, so he was be-
lieved.
* * * *
The children of a Watson’s Bay (Sydney)
resident possessed a Manx (tailless) cat, which
was the envy of all the neighboring kids, and when
said cat was about to have kittens the juvenile
owners were prouder than ever. But, alas, when
the kittens arrived; they were compromises—
papa, of course, having had the normal amount of
tail. Not to be beaten, however, the youngsters
brought chisel and mallet to the aid of nature :
and now those young cats are “best artificial
Manx.”
Some ladies were lately travel-
ling in a coach in a picturesque part
of Victoria. They alighted to enjoy
the scenery, and one of them re-
marked to the driver that it was a
shame the poor horses were not allowed
to view the magnificent prospect, and
persuaded him to remove the winkers.
The animals immediately bolted and
upset the vehicle. Sympathetic women
are common enough, but Jehus like
the one who participated in this inci-
dent are rare.

Dear Sir,—l want you to teil me if Arry
can make me marry im, George come up this

best of im, and did not think they wos in
ernust when they started and will you kindle
tell wot i can do as soon as posible Arry
seys iam too marry im—i never give him
no encoragtnent whatevere nor maid im
eny promises, Arry as treatned to shute
Georg if he comes ere i told im e cude do the
same to me as e done to Georg, pleas let me
no as soon as posible as e is goin inter
on saterday to by the rings and I am terihle
worryed, and i donot want im at no price.
Arry seys you carnt do nothink if you are a

for me—he dont know nothink abote the lor,
e thinks i am fritened of im now sins e give

• * *
“Geo. T.” re back-block cour-
tesy :
I’m an out-back station-hand—have lived
west of the Thompson nearly all my life
and put in years on the Diamantina, and I
never met any of those chaps who give you
the sour “G’day” (“ D. 8.,” B. l/9/[00), and
grudge you directions. A stranger is civilly
saluted by all out-back, and readily given
any directions he may want—provided he
is civil himself. Also, let “D. 8.” ask the
newsagents in Winton and the Reach
whether the station-hands prefer “ Dead-
woods ” to newspapers. Nearly all decent
stations about the Reach have Schools of Arts now (with
hundreds of volumes of good books), and at shearing-
time take in all the leading papers. I doubt if “ D.B.
has ever been 100 miles from the coast.
* * * »
“ W.T.” : On more than one occasion a
dog managed to take my salt-beef from a kerosene-
tin of boiling water upon the fire. How this was
done without spilling the water I can’t imagine,
as I didn’t witness the transaction. [A Bulletin
man once saw the thing done—when too sick with
fever to interfere. The thief—a big (more or less)
kangaroo dog —kept snatching at a tiny portion
of tne meat which projected from the boiling
water (keeping his lips carefully retracted out of
the way) until at last, with a deft jerk, he yanked
the prize clean out of the pot—scalding his muzzle
so in the act, however, that with a yelp he let the
meat drop on the ground. Then he mounted
guard over the spoil until it was cool enougli to
be carried off.]

“ Kedron ” : Can anyone tell me how to
prevent native cats (white-spotted sort) from
killing poultry ? They suck the blood, after
biting a hole in the head. Can they be poisoned ?
* * * *
“ Sept.” : Was once
sleeping in an upper room of
a two-storey pub., and during
the night I was awakened by
a most peculiar combination
of noises, amongst which I
could make out a man’s
snoring, accompanied by a
vibration which kept on in-
creasing until the whole fabric
shook, so that doors creaked,
windows rattled, and the iron
roof was shaking itself loose.
Then with a last quiver the
row stopped abruptly. Al-
ways afterwards supposed
we’d had a touch of earth-
quake, until I heard two
engineers discussing the sub-
ject of sympathetic vibration.
Then I understood. The
snorer must have struck the
keynote of that pub., and I am
convinced that if he had kept
on the building would have
been wrecked.

“W.S.D. ” : Can any
Bulletin reader explain the
significance of the rough semi-
circles of small stones so
common on the Bogan plains ?
Usually about 3ft. in dia. and
3 or 4in. high. Presumably
the work of aborigines, who,
however, seem loth to discuss
the matter.
* *
“ Weary” : To “ J.G.”
(B. 4/8/’00). The ibis desert
the western plains during
droughts (as do all other birds
that frequent the gilgais and
billabongs of the interior),
and migrate in flocks of
about 40, flying so high as to be almost invisible,
and keeping (like so many other species of birds)
a two-line formation, converging to an angle in
front. Like spoonbills, ibis travel during the day-
time only, whereas ducks and black swans make
their transit mostly by night.
A current proposal, suggestive of some
pretty appointments, is to the effect that the
N.S.W police force shall be placed under a board
of three commissioners, subject to the Colonial
Secretary’s department. The force at present
numbers 2000 men, and costs £330,000 a year.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

Mendo: Why talk of “peace” celebrations?
There is no peace, and there isn’t going to be
any. England has simply made for herself in

are full of staying power. If you are alive in 100 years
you may be able to talk of peace in Afrikanderdom

prima facie in the way of qualification. At tne local
police-station you can inspect the Medical Register....

A RELIEF AFTER MAFEKING.
“General Eaden-Powell has made a surprising statement with regard to the siege of Mafeking. He declares that the garrison could have slipped away at any time, and that in holding the town to the end he was simply adhering to his instruc tions, w hich were to keep Boe rs busy."-OABLE.

Murwillumbah, Monday Afternoon.—The
Premier and party arrived at Byron Bay, en
route for the turning of the first sod of the
Casino-Lismore railway, yesterday morning
earlier than was expected. After spending a
few hours in the Bay, the party decided to
take a run as far as Murwillumbah by the
special train. . . . Nobody knew anything
■of the visit, so nobody was present at the
station to receive the distinguished visitors.
The party only remained a few minutes, and
after inspecting the new bridge left again for
Lismore.,

ko ask the Ministerial party if it had a mouth
[ on it ?

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest, buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian erems. *
Attention is drawn to the advertisement
of the Australian Mutual Provident Society ap-
pearing in another column intimating that the
next yearly investigation into the affairs of the
Society and the consequent distribution of the
surplus will be made as at 31st December next.
Intending assurers are reminded that all partici-
pating policies issued before the close of the cur-
rent year will share in the cash bonus to be then
distributed. The amount of profit divided among
the members during the year 1899 exceeded half-a-
million sterling. Persons desirous of sharing the
privileges which membership with this well-
known and popular institution confers and of par-
ticipating in the profit to be earned for the current
year would do well to make application at the
head office or any of the district offices, or consult
one of the Society’s agents without delay.
Registered under the “Companies
Act, 1890,” as a Company, having
secured Assets in Victoria.
It has also deposits, as required by
law, in Great Britain , U.S. A., Canada,
and wherever operating in other parts
of Australasia.
Benefits Paid, over £1,650,000.
Monthly Income, ever £40,000.
Accumulated Funds, over £838,581.
Head Office, TORONTO.

At Poverty Point.
Wybert Reeve, in his younger days, had a good position
on the English stage. He made a hit, 25 or 30 years ago,
as Count Fosco, in an adaptation of Wilkie Collins’s
“ Woman in White ” ; and he is the author of a forgotten
novel, called “ Looking Back,” which was brought out by
a London publisher. At a Melbourne theatrical dinner,
Reeve once related how, when performing in Liverpool,
he acted temporarily as dramatic critic for a local paper,
unknown to his friends of the sock and buskin. Taking
exception on one occasion to some points in the perform-
ance of the “juvenile lead” in his co., that personage
said to him, the day after the notice appeared, “ Did you
ever read such a rotten criticism ? The writer must be a
born fool 1 ” A week later, Reeve was able to praise the
same actor, who thereupon remarked, “ Y«u should’ read
the paper to-day. They have now got a capable critic,
who really understands his business, and can write, too.”
Which story sets the attitude of the Thespian towards the
critic in a very clear light.
A well-known theatrical manager was doing the large
towns some years back. Writer had a front seat and
noticed that an old cornet player did not shatter the
atmosphere much. Next day was irrigating with
manager aforesaid who referred in comprehensive
language to the old boy. Said he : “Fancy having to
cart an old frame like that round the country ! He’s got
me right on toast. He’s my leading lady’s father and if I
shunt him off she goes too, and the old devil never blows
a dashed toot.”
Dion Boucicault the younger (and the present) made
his first appearance in London just 20 years ago, and was
immediately accepted as a chip off the old Dion.
Charles Dickens was almost the first Englishman who
discovered Fechter’s merit. The novelist saw him per-
form in Paris and described him as the best stage-lover
he had ever encountered. The reason that used to be
assigned for Fechter’s retirement from the stage was that
his passion for a beautiful woman left his brain of no
more value than a capful of porridge. Beautiful Alice
Dunning Lingard took some trouble to get a look at the
lady in an American hotel, and arrived at the conclusion
that there was nothing wonderful about her. After
Fechter settled down on his farm in America, he became
enormously fat. “ A plague on sighing and grief—it
blows a man out so,” as Falstaff says. And the figure
that runs to tallow is not much sought after in melo-
drama.
“Itan”: Some of those cinematograph “war-views”
are atrocious fakes. Saw a series of them in a N.S.W.
country town recently, and the clouds of white “ smoke ”
supposed to issue from the guns and rifles showed that
the “ fakers” had forgotten all about smokeless powder.
In another picture—supposed to represent an “ attack by
Australian cavalry ’’—same couple of dozen warriors kept
passing the same spot on the “olf-and-on” stage prin-
ciple. But the most colossal fake of the lot was an attack
by a force of Zulus, on an occasion when a British party
was wiped out—in defiance of the trifling circumstance
that nothing of the kind has taken place since art has
learned to cinematograph ! Again, there was an attack
by Boers on a British outpost, and a short-range battle
between two handfuls of men till only one on each side
remained. Then the Britisher fell, but when the Boer
rushed up to take an important despatch the other was
supposed to be carrying, the latter popped up, shot the
enemy dead with his revolver, and, waving his cap, ran
away ! War! Yet the audience appeared to swallow it
all as the real thing !
“ Alb.” : “ De Governor had come to hear me play Bee-
thoven. He sit in de front. I march up de steps to de
organ und de people stamp ver’ much. Den I put my
feet on de pedals und my hand on de stops und I find no
vind in de pipes. I ring de electric-bell to tell Joe to
send up de vind, und I get no answer. Den I sit dere
like dam fool while de people stamp and laugh. Yen I
could hold myself no longer I climb down 'from my seat
und go downstairs to find Joe. Und de Govenor still vait
for Beethoven. I did not find Joe near de engine. A
man came up de passage und he say, ‘ Joe have gone to
see de man about de dog.’ Den I burst with fury when I
kpow de Govenor still vait for Beethoven. Den Joe come
runnin’ along de passage wipin’ his mouth. ‘ You dam
scamp, Joe,’ I says ; ‘ why you go lookin’ for de dam dog
while de Govenor wait for Beethoven ? ’ Und Joe wipe
his face —it was ver’ hot —und he say, ‘ My cousin Jim
came in, und he take me over de way to have de beer.’
Den I feel sorry dat I did swear at Joe ; und I say, ‘ Joe,
nevar mind, send up de vind quick, de Govenor is
vaitin’.’ Und I go up to de big organ ,und play Bee-
thoven.”
Bland Holt’s full name is Joseph Bland Holt, and he
first faced the footlights as Joseph Bland—Bland was his
mother’s maiden name. Not generally known that, years
ago, Holt was at the top of his profession as a clown of
the modern pantomimic school, and was hailed in America
as the legitimate successor of Grimaldi. A queer little
sidelight is that, as a boy in Dunedin, he regarded the
prospects of a theatrical career with horror, and old
Clarence Holt more than once used a disciplinary birch to
encourage his son’s dramatic enthusiasm. The youthful
Bland had a great taste for figures, and his craving was
to sit in a counting-house.
George Musgrove has imported a male costumier, a
lady ditto, and an expert wig-maker to look after the
companies’ heads of hair.
Marchesi re the singing pupils who hearken to the
fairy tales of perfidious man : “So many dear, interest-
ing girls have come to me with heart-breaking confi-
dences ! And I have wept over so many others who had so
changed as to actually regard the most condemnable
freedom as necessary (that’s where the wicked male per-
suasion comes in) to their full development and to their
success ; indeed, as a natural and, on the whole, enjoy-
able privilege of all artists.” If all the things that have
been said, or suggested, about the full development of
the “artist nature” could only be published in a big
book, what an interesting volume we would get!
To every old Australian playgoer who cherishes recol-
lections of “ Tambour Major,” with Harwood and Johnny
Wallace as the Duke and Bambini (not to mention the
original production, which founded George Musgrove’sfor-
tune), it will be shocking to hear that Offenbach’s opera
was lately reproduced in a Jingo form. A new “ book ”
was fitted to the original music. Scene laid in S’Africa,
the fiirst act taking place near Capetown, the second at
Johannesburg, and the third concluding with a trium-
phal entry of the British Army into Pretoria. Offen-
bach, the Franco-German, must have turned in his grave
when the first performance of the reconstructed opera
came to pass at a London suburban theatre. He died in
1880, the year of the Boer revolt against the first annexa-
tion of the Transvaal, when all Paris was wishing good
luck to the veldt farmers.
“ Jim the Penman,” one of the very best comedy-
dramas ever written, and the sole good work of its
author, was lately revived in London. A lot of Australian
interest attaches to “Jim.” The name-part was well
played here by St. Maur, with Vincent, and afterwards
Christie Murray and Titheradge, as the Baron. The
original Jim, in London, was poor Arthur Dacre, to whom
Marius (who came out here with Mrs. Bernard Beere)
played Baron. Marius made him a Frenchman ; Beer-
bohm Tree afterwards made him a German ; Anson seems
to have given him a broken-English of doubtful character.
Probably this Baron comes from Alsace, says London
Referee.
Edward Lloyd, the English tenor, seems to have for-
gotten his intention of retiring into private life last year.
At latest dates the veteran had been engaged to sing the
name-part in a cantata setting of Cardinal Newman’s
poem, “The Dream of Gerontius.” Tenors take a long
time to retire from the platform.
Ben Fuller, “ the pioneer of permanent vaudeville in
Maoriland,” has now four shows running there. His
Dunedin co. has been going 80 weeks continuously ; his
Christchurch co. 53 weeks ; his Wellington co. 40 weeks ;
and his Auckland co. 18 weeks. His prices are Is. and 6d.,
and he seems to be making money. Anyhow, he lately
bought the old Alhambra Theatre, Dunedin, for £3OOO,
and intends starting another show in Invercargill ere
long. His show is the usual “variety,” and he has at
present the “ Flying Jordans ” amongst his other attrac-
tions.
Among the most charming exponents of English opera
that have ever appeared in Australia were the sisters
Emma and Olelia Howson, who flourished in the early
sixties. Brother Frank used to lead the orchestra and
Father John took a boss part in the opera. Clelia married
and retired from the stage after a couple of years’ ex-
perience of it. Emma went to England and was the original
Josephine in “ Pinafore.” She looked down on the part,
and one day petrified W. S. Gilbert (who had suggested
some improved “business”) with the remark, “But I
played in Italian opera.” Since retiring on her laurels
she has lived in England. Emma never married, though
she had many opportunities in the days of her attractive
youth.
“D. J.” : Once, when writer had contributed a notice
of Barry Sullivan’s performance of Falstaff to Melbourne
Herald (then a morning paper) which the great gun did
not like, he struck the paper off the free list. This did
not worry editor George Collins Levey. “ Write the
truth,” said he ; “if the thing is good £ive it praise, but
if it is bad (as most likely it will be) give it—well, some-
thing else.” Happened to meet Sullivan shortly after.
“ Well, sir,” said he, majestically, “ have you repented of
yourd libel?” “ Not a bit of it,” was the answer.
“ Further experience of your Falstaff has informed me
that an audience that can tolerate —” “ Stop, stop,”
shouted Barry. “ Man, do you know the infamy of blas-
phemy ! Come, sir, let us drink to your better reason.
And, by the way, I’ve restored your—er—journal to its
former—er—privileged position. And I do trust —I
earnestly pray that the—er—concession will serve to
improve your—ah—judgment.” But it didn’t while
Barry burlesqued Falstaff.
Dear Bulletin. —“F.,” in “Poverty Point,” Sept. 22,
in an airy par., sets a 2col. task. Ibsen has made no addi-
tion to European literature. So ? The Norwegian pro-
duces a play in every two years, his plays, as soon as pro-
duced, are pounced upon and translated into nearly every
European language. He is read and acted in French,
Dutch, Polish, Russian, English, Hungarian, Italian, Ger-
man, and his native tongue almost simultaneously : What
other living or dead author has enjoyed that distinction ?
He sells enormously in every language; the English alone
have purchased as many as 100,000 copies of his plays
within about four years. May the above justify the claim
of Ibsen readers that he has made an addition to Euro-
pean literature ? “The Ibsen influence is a cant cry.”
Is it indeed ? The most prominent German dramatists to-
day are all confessed disciples of the author of “ Hedda
Gabler ” —Sudermann. Hauptmann, Halbi, Hartlebin, and
others—and there is not a critic of note in Berlin who will
listen with patience to the sentimental inanities of the
bread-and-butter romance familiar on the English stage.
Antwerp has an “ Ibsen Theatre ” in active operation.
But those are low foreigners. Jones, in England, admits the
Ibsen influence in “ Michael and His Lost Angel,” “ The
Case of Rebellious Susan,” “ The Masqueraders,” “The
Crusaders,” &c. Bernard Shaw’s plays are all ihsenitish
in their outlook upon character and conventions. He is
a confessed disciple. The appearance of the so-called
problem-plays in England followed upon the sensation
created by the appearance of Ibsen on London boards.
Cause and effect. Closely examine the British drama for
ten years before and after that era, and note the change
from undiluted French romanticism to at least something
like a manful and artistic effort to put breathing, actual
men and women on the stage, and to handle them with
some measure of realism. “F.” trots out “The Money-
Spinner ” in proof of his assertion that Paula Tanqueray
is the natural outcome of Pinero’s early vein, and proves
how thoroughly he misapprehends the position. The
heroine in “ The Money-Spinner ” is treated in the purely
romantic vein ; she is the average angelic heroine who is
permitted a little slip which, if anything, rather enhances
her sentimental virtues. Paula is realistic ; she is drawn
as close to life as Pinero dared—or perhaps as close as
Pinero could. The bad and the good of the woman are
blended into a character that surprised patrons of the
British drama by its vivid humanity. But the flavor of
Ibsen in the new Pinero is more obvious still in “ The
Benefit of the Doubt” and “The Notorious Mrs. Ebb-
smith ” (the stone incident in the latter must recall
“Hedda Gabler”), and began with “Lady Bountiful.”
Possibly Pinero is not conscious of the Ibsen influence,
and no sane person will argue that he writes Ibsen plays.
He has merely added some of the vitality of Ibsen to the
literary flashness of Sardon and the dramatic craftsman-
ship and lady-like romanticism of his other masters,
Scribe and Robertson. — Edson. P.S. —“ F.,” says “Mrs.
Tanqueray” was written 40 years after “The Dolls
House.” A miracle 1 “The Doll's House” dates from
1879.—E.
In return for the testimonial with which some serious
people presented Dampier as a reward for his production
of “ Drink,” the great man tried to illustrate the influence
that Parisian drama had for good in the way of enticing
people off the Curse. A man of his acquaintance absorbed
liquor largely, resisting all attempts of the local sky pilot
to cure him of his sin. Then Dampier came to the fore
and offered him a small part in the drama, where he had
nothing to do but bring on a bucket of water in the
i laundry scene. The result was that, in a short while,
the drinker ceased from his drinking, and, as Dampier
pathetically adds, settled down and became the father of
seven. Thus, even after repentance, did his sin find him
out.
“ Skull ” : Australia promises to be a land of awful pro-
digies. We have had a woeful dose of them at the Melb.
theatres and music-halls during the last 20 months or so.
The cry is still they come, and they grow younger every
month, so that I foresee the time when the nurse will
amble on with her long-coated “ ickle ducksee ” to amuse
a grown audience with its amazing gurglings. At its best
the infant phenomenon is but an immature and miniature
serio-comic, and is bearable in brief and infrequent doses.
At its worst and as a steady infliction it is a more
aggravating form of that ancient horror, the happy
parent who would persist in holding you up for hours
while he recounted the astonishing witticisms of his
miraculous infant.
A dramatic co., doing N.S.W. backblox, struck a town
near which a party of blacks were camped. The co.
visited the camp and was entertained with an exhibition
of boomerang-throwing &c., in return for which the
manager invited the lot to the show. They came, and
when the performance was over a gin asked the manager
for “shillin’, buy sugar.” “Why,” said he, “ isn’t it
enough that I’ve let you all into the show for nothing ! ”
“Oh no, boss 1 Give it shillin’. We bin very good alonga
you. Sittem all night listen you yabber-yabber, an’ never
say plurry word 1 ”
A theatrical manager running a scratch co. about the
suburbs of an Australian metropolis, with occasional ex-
cursions into the interior, had a curious experience
recently. He discovered a new genius, a youth with
a wild and roving eye, who was particularly convincing as
15 different villains, and won tons of jam from the Bunyip
Bugle and the Paddyhanglb Banner for the realistic
manner in which he gouged the hero, or strangled the
virtuous maiden who scorned his dishonorable proposals.
The manager determined to exhibit his acquisition before
metropolitan audiences, but, alas! at the first suburban
performance the new tragedian was recognised by a
policeman, and promptly arrested, and returned to the
lunatic asylum from which he had escaped six weeks
earlier.
A conjuror travelling in the backblox had, as the last
item on the programme, “ The Great Disappearing Act
(lOmin. intervals).” While the natives sat waiting for the
turn the magician retreated from, the back of the hall,
having to scale a 7ft. fence on his way. He did not
return, and next morning it was found that he had
skipped without paying his board-bill.
“ Melb.” : The esteemed and demised W. Marshall,
theatrical printer, left a vast and indeed unique collection
of memoranda (as well as 30 years of programmes)
relating to the Melb. stage. They were compiled, it is
said, by H. H. Waters, known as “Cocky” Waters, one-
time accountant of the Herald, and a writer in the
Australasian under the name of “ Callboy.” His whim
was to collect the date of the first local appearance of all
performers. His accumulations would incalculably
shorten the labor of anyone starting to write a history of
the local stage.
“ Sievier” writes : " Please inform me, if you can, why
it is that theatrical managers in Australia often take a
successful piece off to make room for another play, thus
risking a failure. Take Bland Holt, frinstance, with
‘Women and Wine’ and ‘A. M. Beggar.’” Well the
answer is that managers like Bland Holt don’t take a
successful piece off until the treasury shows it to have
arrived at very near the limit of an average prosperous
run for a first production. It is bad policy to let a new
show outstay its welcome by even half-a-week. There are
revivals to consider. And, by the way, appearances are
often deceptive to a casual theatre-goer. A successful
piece may seem to finish its run at top speed, whereas the
house during the last week may contain a good many
passes or other “ stiffs.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
David Syme concludes that Vic. Leg.
Council is incurable, but is of opinion that “ what
cannot be cured need not be endured.”
Politically speaking Victoria is a woman
with a past —a past from which she cannot free
herself. Victoria’s past is the Legislative Council.
The Argus recently re cemetery sites :
“ We owe a higher duty to the living than to the
dead.” And yet the Argus is a strenuous sup'
porter of Vic. Leg. Council !
Q. Laborites are putting up for the House
of Representatives two brainy M’sL.A.—Turley
and Fisher —who look like certainties. Two other
good men—Dawson and M‘Donald—may repre-
sent N.Q.
From first to last South Australia has
naturalised 3588 foreigners, of whom no less than
2513 are Germans. Chinese come next—2B4 of
them. Of Frenchmen there have been only 40,
and of Americans 10.
As to the Australian flag. From the first,
let us have a flag that will need no altering in the
time when we shall cease saying “By your
leave ! ” when we would but make our country
greater and happier.
Premier Lyne declares that £2OOO a year
is an utterly inadequate salary for the N.S.W.
State Governor. Long Bill, as a man of strongly
provincial leanings, may be expected to magnify
the office of State Governor.
On the debate on the M.L. Flag Bill,
Monk, M.P., declared that, as Maoriland was the
most southern of the British colonies, “it is most
justly entitled to the Southern Cross.” Which
bears out Max O’Rell’s assertion that Bull had
already annexed the Heavens.
Sachse (Victorian M.L.C.) on the Woman
Suffrage Bill :
I have endeavored from my heart to concur in .giving
women a vote, but my wretched head would not allow
me.
Sachse’s description of his own head is beautifully
ac'curate.
Kingston got into S.A. Leg. Council just
in time to save an early-closing measure from
death. The democratic leader is now trying to
rescue a Workman’s Compensation Bill from being
destroyed for the fourth time. At present the
Tory barnacles are in a majority of one, and the
only prospect of getting a progressive measure
through is for an Ass. nominee to lie up with gout
or die of over-feeding.
PROBABLY.
GEM: “Mrs. Pegleg never misses a sale.”
CHIN : “Did she get Mr. Pegleg at the remnant-
counter?”
Kingston having converted Symon, Q.C.,
to a belief in the broadest suffrage for the Leg.
Council, has now great hopes of Play ford and
Glynn, who have hitherto been strong on the
Tory side, but who, like Symon, are anxious
to get into the Federal Parliament on the popular
vote.
The British Government states that “ it is
not prepared to consider any proposal for chang-
ing the political position of Fiji.” Which puts
a heavy and uncompromising boot down, for the
present at all events, on Premier Seddon’s great
scheme for making Maoriland the centre of a far-
reaching Brown Man’s Confederation in the
Pacific.
Libor leader Price publicly remarked
recently that he had never before seen so much
“lobbying” in S.A. Parliament as there has been
over a certain Tramway Bill. Now the names of
those who are said to have been successfully
“ approached ” are being mentioned very freely,
and the political institutions of S.A. smell a little
worse than usual.
A N.S.W. civil servant is under the
current law liable to be compelled to resign at 00
years of age, but Premier Lyne’s proposed Old-
Age Pension Bill lays down 05 as the age when
the private citizen is supposed to leave off work.
If the law doesn’t think a man can work for the
State at over 00, how can he be supposed able to
work for private employers ?
N.S.W. Parliament should, by resolution,
fix definitely the travelling expenses of Ministers
of the Crown. The custom is £3 per day for a
Premier and £2 for other Ministers while absent
from Sydney. Ministers lately reported that at
present they only charge out-of-pocket expenses.
But they may, when leaving office, draw the full
amount. This has been done before.
The Queensland Ministry complains bit-
terly of the loquacity of Labor-members during
the syndicate-railway discussions and of the scan-
dalous waste of time resulting therefrom. T’other
day Premier Philp, who is introducing the gag,
adjourned the Assembly so that members might
go and see a pumpkin-show up at Rosewood.
Time is, indeed, precious in Bananaland.
The property qualification fora Leg. Coun-
cil elector in S. A., which Kingston has set himself
to break down, naturally bars more female than
male Assembly-electors. For the Assembly the
proportion of female to male electors is about (58
to 83 ; for the Council 9to 38, Only every third
qualified Assembly male elector has a vote for the
Council; only every seventh female has one.
So restricted is the Leg. Council franchise
in S.A. still, in spite of frequent liberalisations,
that in the whole of the Northern Territory only
86 people are entitled to vote—tha't is, only
per cent, of the total entitled to vote for the
Assembly ! Yet the other per cent, continue
to send Opposition leader Solomon to the Assembly,
presumably to go on fighting against their com-
plete enfranchisement 1
Tasmania’s lost elector has Degun to ask
seriously and strenuously for his fair share of
representation. Zeehan and Lyell have one-fifth
of all the electors on the island and one-nineteenth
Of the representation. Lyell has 6000 voters and
one member ; Hobart has 5400 voters and six
members ; Cressy has 352 voters and one member
—the same as Lyell. No wonder the West Coast
is beginning to put on its big gum-boots
' and kick hard.
* “ The usual cry at the beginning of a
bayonet-charge.”—War Correspondent
“Locust”: Referring to King-
ston’s estimate of British public men
(Rosebery to wit), it is a strange fact that
leading Liberals seem to fight shy of
Australian visiting statesmen. Either
that or the cute Tories rush and get hold
of the Australian and put salt on his
tail. But in at least one case, one of
our men was not only ignored but
snubbed by leading Liberals. Heard
Dibbs say in London that Gladstone and
Rosebery were both “ cads,” and I knew
that lie had reason. Yet Gladstone will-
inglyl gave a Labor-man from Australia
an interview. It ma/ be, again, that
Democrats become violent Jingoes when
transplanted. “If all Our statesmen
were born in the colonies,” said H. W.
Massingham, “Jingoism would be un-
controllable.” But after all I think the
true explanation is that the Primrose
duchesses and countesses are adroit
political canvassers and assist in roping-in
our “statesmen,” for everyone who re-
turns (Kingston, for example, and Dibbs) is
never done skiting about the countesses he has
met—and, strange to say, the Countess of Warwick
is always one of those mentioned.
Premier Philp, in replying to a deputation
of clergymen advocating religious.instruction in
State schools, expressed the opinion that there
was not “ enough religion in this country.” Isn t
it peculiar that politicians of the Philp and Tozer
class should always so sincerely avow the import-
ance of giving the youth of the country a chance
of Biblical instruction ? They evidently recognise
that the country which tolerates them requires to
have its morals improved.
PROPHETIC .
P AT: “ Did ye hear that old Hogan was dead,
Mrs. Ryan? ”
MRS. R.: “Is he, thin, poor man? Sure . I
always knew that’d be the end of him.”
Ex-Minister Joe Cook, though asserting
that N.S.W. Labor Party has ratted to Lyne, has
had himself appointed by the Lithgow miners
Labor delegate to the Australian Labor Federa-
tion. This is a cute move of a man whose
interests have long ago separated him from the
Labor Party both in and out of Parliament. It
will enable him to play nexus between Ileidites
and Laborites, and to work the Labor cause to the
political advantage of his party.
Kingston is very proud of the fact that
his father, when S.A. Constitution was framed,
strongly opposed the property-restriction for the
Council which the son is now, 50 years afterwards,
striving to remove. Kingston pere was a curious
blend of Tory and Democrat, but it is memorable
that the old man, in 1845, strongly and successfully
opposed convict-exportation to S.A., and later
was one of the most prominent opponents of State
aid to religion. He died at sea when 73 years
old.
N.S.W. coal-mine owners have practically
defied the coal-weighing provisions of an Act of
Parliament for a couple of years, and now another
Act is being similarly defied by ferry-boat pro-
prietors. In each case Govt, directed that the
Act should stand in abeyance till one point or
another received fresh consideration. And the
plutocratic press is indulgently silent. Yet the
plutocratic press cry is, when the poorer sections
of the community are affected: “Obey the law
till Parliament alters it.”
Reform of the Vic. Leg. Council is “in
the air ” just now, and even the Tory feels bound
to mention it in his programme. A fine sample is
one J. Eklridge Healy, who appeals to the Mel-
bourne electors: —
It is to be earnestly hoped that Chamber reformers
will, without improper minatory methods, firmly urge
the adjustment of our State Legislature.
“Without improper minatory methods.” The
House of 48 Rich Landlords mustn’t be threatened.
It must simply be asked to pass a reform measure,
and cordially assured that nothing unpleasant
will happen to it if it refuses.
N.S. Wales and S.A. jumped ori Tas-
mania’s proposal of a joint Australian message to
Britain approving of the annexation of the Boer
States. They “ feared the message might be made
a political weapon in the proceeding British elec-
tions.” Yet, why not? Tasmania’s proposal was
the logical corollary of previous Jingo foolishness.
If Australia must have a hand in Britain’s wars,
why not also in Britain’s politics, which bring
wars about? If Australia must spend blood and
money in stealing for Britain, why should she
spare advice about the disposal of the plunder?
Wood’s amendment on the S.A. Land Bill,
making any further sales of State lands absolutely
illegal, was lost by only one vote. The Holder
Ministry went with the minority. South Aus-
tralia practically abandoned the sale of State lands
ten years ago, and has since contrived to pay its
way to some extent without assistance from thi s
source. But the solid Tory vote against formally
abolishing land sales by Act of Parliament sug-
gests that the Solomon party, whenever it gets
into power, means to resume the work of dissi-
pating the public assets ; therefore, if the Hol c i er
Ministry means business it should make another
strong fight for the insertion of Wood’s claus e i n
the Bill. And it should never have left it to a
private member to propose such a clause.
S.A. Parliament intends to veto the
Holder Govt.’s scheme of a national convention
to remodel the State constitution. The alterna-
tive scheme, to which a majority of both Houses is
stated to have agreed, is to leave the work to the
present Assembly and Council sitting together.
The Labor crowd strongly opposes any reduction
of members following Federation, and that is its
main reason for opposing the convention idea.
And the Laborists are consequently supported by
many members who fear effacement in the event
of reduction.
In the past year Victoria paid £311,107 to
retired public servants, or more than treble the'
pensions disbursed by all the rest of
Australia. Charles Cavan Duffy, in con-
sideration of having served two years as-
Lands Minister (in which position he
passed an Act under which squatters’
dummies were enabled to pick out the
eyes of the country), has drawn £lOOO a
year for 38 years. Andrew Clarke
(formerly Vic. Surveyor-General), at pre-
sent acting as Agent-General, is next on
the list with £37,000, his pension being
£BOO a year ; and the widow of Gov. Dar-
ling has been paid £lOOO a year for 30'
years. There is also an ex-County Court
Judge whose allowance is £7soayear, as
a recompense for five years’ bench-work.
Old-age pensions in Maoriland this
year will only cost £193,000 ; and Vic-
toria could provide for all her indi-
gent agfed population for £12,000 a
year less than she is at present paying
to retired .public servants. All the
same. The Bulletin proposes an
addition of £48,000 a year to
the Vic. pension-list—£looo a
year each to the 48 Rich Land-
lords as an inducement to pass
a Bill for their own abolition.
It would be the one profitable
item on the pension-roll.
Sydney Daily Telegraph
gravely observes that “the con-
verts that have been made from
Mohammedanism to Christianity
since the work was begun are pro-
bably less than the number of Chris-
tians who, within the same time,
have lapsed into agnosticism or infidelity.” “ Pro-
bably ” is good-considering that, while Chris-
tianity is practically, if not ostensibly, losing
votaries by the thousand, even the nominal con-
verts from Mohammedanism, the wide world over,
do not total a dozen per annum ; while of real con-
verts it is much to be doubted if there has ever
been, or ever will be, a single one !

THE DIVINE WILL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The teachings of our Lord the Host
In many manners come ;
Sometimes it is per Holy Ghost,
Sometimes by trump and drum.
Sometimes they say, “ Thou shalt not kill ”
At others, “ Give them hell! ”*
And we, who know the holy will,
The proper meaning spell.
The riddle we have solved, and teach
There, is one certain test,
And to the multitude we preach
To do what pays the best.
So put the Boer to the sword,
And shoot the rebels down ;
In things like-this you’ll find the Lord
Is with the British Crown.
’Tis thus the teachings of the Host
Preserve us all from sin ;
Sometimes they come per Holy Ghost,
Sometimes per Chamberlain.
OLERICTIS.


Mr. W. E. Toose, optician, of 422 George-
8t., Sydney, has just (after examination) been
made a Fellow of the “ Anglo-American Optical
Society ” —also a member of the British Optical
Association. Mr. Toose, it is understood, is the
first N.S.W. optician to acquire the former dis-
tinction.
Attention is called to the alteration in the
sailings of the Norddeutscher Lloyd steamers, as
per advertisement on page 26. The changes are
unavoidable, owing to the fire at the Hoboken
Docks of the Norddeutscher Lloyd in New York,
and the chartering of 15 of theN.D.L. steamers
to the German Government as troopships for
China. It will be noticed that the service for
March and April is now four-weekly instead of
three-weekly as originally intended, but the three-
weekly service will be inaugurated as soon as the
steamers are available.
Fifty electors of Toorak (Vic.) met
solemnly last week to pick a Liberal candidate to
oppose Duncan Gillies at the coming general elec-
tion. Jimmy Munro, of Federal Bank fame, was
seated at the table beside the chairman, and the
first speaker arose and moved that Mr. Munro, as
the only available Liberal candidate, be asked to
contest the election. There was no uproar. No
eggs were thrown. Nobody howled in derision.
The gathering failed utterly to see the sarcasm of
the situation. (Think of Federal Bank Jimmy as
the only available Liberal Candidate !) The meet-
ing accepted him unanimously. Then James
came forward and said that he would never have
consented to stand had it not been for the gutter
press having referred in slighting terms to him
and his deceased financial institutions. He
wanted an opportunity to refute those calumnies,
and therefore he would oblige the electors of
Toorak and go to the poll. Yet will Jimmy’s
awful banks be any better after Jimmy has been
at the poll ?

Premier Lyne says that the largest in-
crease in N.S.W. revenue came from spirits. Mr.
Reid’s surplus used to come from the spirit world,
too.—Star.
A Stipendiary in a Sydney court one day
lately requested that a case called on about
midday should, “if it were of the usual nauseating
kind,” be postponed till after lunch, as “he didn’t
want his appetite spoilt.”
Extract from the advt, of a frivolous tailor
named Whitlock, of Ulverston, Tasmania: —
Pants are made for men, and not for women. Women
are made for men, and not for pants. When a man pants
for a woman, and a woman pants for a man, they are a
pair of pants. Such pants don’t last. Pants are like
molasses—they are thinner in hot weather and thicker in
cold. Men are often mistaken in pants ; such mistakes
are breeches of promise. There has been much discussion
whether pants is singular or plural. Seems to me when
men wear pants it is plural, and when they don’t wear
any pants it is singular. Men go on a tear in' their pants,
and it is all right; when the pants go on a tear it is all
wrong. If you want to make pants last, make the coat
first.
A South Island (M.L.) paper describes
Dunedin tramways : —
As for the Dunedin tramcar system—.well, it may be
better some day but at present it is not much superior to
the trolly system in a coal-mine. A few wretched, untidy
mouldy-looking ears that are over-crowded and stuffed
with all'sorts and conditions like sardines in a box. No
one orders the people off ; no one compels the company
to put on more cars, and no one worries about these
wretched horses.
Which is all true and more. Dunedin is a godly
city utterly devoid of mercy for its tram-horses.
Sometimes this scribe, who spent years in that
town, has dreams to the effect that there may be a
heaven and a hell after all ; that every ratepayer
of Dunedin will deservedly go to the latter place
for his and her callousness to the sufferings of the
bony wrecks that break their hearts trying to drag
misshapen cars up the ghastly hill by Dowling-
street; and that the ratepayer will look up from
that future residence and see his despised tram-
horse in heaven and at rest.

Thousands Have Piles.
THEY ARE EASY TO CURE.
Perhaps there is no disease more irritating and
annoying than Piles. It is the result of imperfect
circulation of the blood. The venous blood settles
in the veins of the rectum, and expanding these
veins produces the nodules know as Piles. This
is sometimes produced by an inactive liver, which
does not permit the blood to return freely to the
heart; sometimes it is the result of a weak action
of the heart itself, and often it is due to large
masses of faeces lodging in the bowels and pressing
upon the veins, so that the blood cannot return to
the heart and lungs. This disease is not only
most painful, but also very dangerous, as the in-
flamed nodules are very apt to take on malignant
action, and cancer of the rectum is produced.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, in their action on
the liver, heart and bowels, strike at all these
causes of Piles and remove them.

TROUBLE.
