Dredging in Otago (M.L.) has of late assumed a
new phase. During the boom some 250 co’s were floated
and shares eagerly taken up for the few shillings pro-
spective premium. Someone had to nurse the baby, and
when the public dropped to the fact that no matter how
valuable the claim the dredge had to be built and
calls paid, down things came wallop. The fact
that local foundries could not take up the contracts
offered them has been the salvation of many rash specu-
lators. They had time to suffer a recovery, and whilst
things were thus it suddenly struck someone that it
might be wise to prospect and bore some of the
hidden mines of wealth, painted in plans and pro-
spectuses with such yellow paint. Result —many of
the bank claims and also some river ones proved
quite unworkable for dredging purposes. The fever
abated and as calls became pressing it was found
that hundreds of shareholders were servant-girls, lads in
offices, &e., and as they were responsible in some cases
for as much as £5OO or £6O0 —all the co.s were limited-
liability—they formed themselves into a “ Wreckers’
Association.” By judicious wire-pulling, and assisted
by other shareholders with full hands, they are liquidat-
ing and winding up companies day after day. Even
prospective Hartley and Rileys are not safe from them.
In several cases where contracts have been let they have
disposed of the contracts at a premium to other co.s
with a well-held share-list. Despite which there are scores
ofco.sonthe Mol.vneux only too willingto buy second-hand
dredges from claims which have proved unpayable on
other rivers. The Galtee More Dredge on Manuherikia
River was sold (to go to the West Coast) for £l5OO more
than she actually cost original owners. Some magnifi-
cent returns have been got lately. The Ranfurly Electric
got over 600 oz. for a week’s dredging and 30 other dredges
got over 25000 z. among them.
Cobar Gold Mines: One of the directors, .T.
Macdonald, after examining the property, some time back
cabled the London board that the mine would develop
into a good, steady dividend-payer, and was so satisfied
of this that he endorsed manager Buekland’s recom-
mendation to purchase a filter-pressing plant to deal with
the slimes, an enormous quantity of which have already
accumulated. Six filter-presses have now been ordered.

very high grade. , , »

mining and assay chemicals and apparatus, can be ob-
tained of Felton, Griimvade and Co., Melbourne, who
have issued a very handsome illustrated catalogue of all
mining requisites, price Is. 3d. post free.

* * * *
Wentworth Gold Mines, N.S.W., has been
wiped out of existence—otherwise absorbed by the
D’Arcy Wentworth Co. for 125,000 shares. It had a capi-
tal of its own of £500,000, and the shares have been as high
as £3. The falling-off must be enormous when it parts
with its mine, machinery and other assets, for 125,000
shares iri the D’Arcy, which has only a speculative value.
Earl Kilmorey, the chairman, advised the shareholders to
accept as the best thing to do under the circumstances.
A very discontented old lady shareholder turned up to
the meeting and cross-examined the Earl to such an ex-
tent that finally he said : “ My dear madam, will you
allow me to propose you as a director on the new Board?”
And the old lady immediately replied, “ Certainly I will.”
So it now remains for the Earl to carry out his con-
tract or to plead with Frank Gardiner to bring it about.
Many of the London mining boards consist already
largely of old women.
* • • •
A Leipzig correspondent to The Bulletin :— .
“ The wool-boom in Australia from Sept, to Dec. of last
season has now produced, as was expected by
the wise heads of the trade generally, a corresponding re-
action involving many Continental failures. Roubaix
and Tourcoing, the great French wool-centres, have had
three failures and 12 suspensions to the amount of
£1,000,000 in liabilities, but it is supposed that this sum
will be much reduced by assets in the shape of wool, etc.
The failure of many other merchants is said to have
been stopped by the prompt action of the Bank of France
and other French institutions. Venders, the Belgian
wool-centre, through local financial support of its banks,
has so far only announced one failure. Leipzig, the Ger-
man centre, has one failure for £150,000 —said to be the
Continental house that “ bulled ” the Sydney wool-mar-
ket last season. A strange feature of all these failures is
that amongst the claims mentioned in their liabilities is
gambling on the ‘ Terme ’ — i.e., ‘Futures' market. All
failures up to date have been wool merchants, and the
crisis does not seem to have affected manufacture.”
* * • •
It is proposed to fit with gold-saving appliances
the Govt, silt-dredges now at work on the southern
N.S.W. coastal-rivers.
* • * *
Norseman, which is very sick, is turning
towards the newer Phillips River field as Heaven-sent.
Not only are the men leaving for the newer place, but
some of the Co’s are even putting the remnant of their
capital into it. The latest estimate of the Phillips River
population put it at 300.
* • • *
Westralia sells its mining-township allotments
to the highest bidders, and thus gives the absentee
speculator the chance he doesn’t get under the N.S.W.
system of miners’ rights and buying through personal
residence only. So far the Groper Govt, has in this way
received £79,605 for Coolgardie allotments, and £77,061
for Kalgoorlie ditto, £41,115 for Boulder, £27,930 for
Menzies, and so on down to £4059 for Cue. All round,
the allotments have averaged £4B 12s. an acre.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
N.S.W. did better than most expected at Caul-
field on Suturday, through Severity appropriating Caul-
field, and Ilautain the Debutant Stakes. Severity’s
success caused surprise in Sydney, though he went to the
post a strong second favorite. The Held was a high-class
one at w.f.a., and embraced Tarquin, Paul Pry, Seahorse,
Merriwee, Dandy and Mora. In Wycombe Stakes, at
recent A.J.O. meet, Severity came home a bad
last, and yet now we hear of the gelding accounting
for the best hags (at four years old and over) on
the continent, and doing it with a bit to the
good, too. Three-year-old form has been further
mixed up through Kinglike’s win of the “Guineas,"
with Finland second, Maltster third, and Hautboy fourth.
October Stakes, a week before, saw Hautboy lead all the
journey, and Finland couldn’t get nearer than fifth—
which goes to endorse popular opinion that Finland is an
in-and-outer, with little heart. Kinglike is in Caulfield
Cup (7.2), Melbourne Cup (7st), V.R.O. Derby, and
several other events. Prior to annexing the Guineas only
Maiden Stakes and Nursery Handicap (at Randwick last
April) stood to his credit.
• * • •
Severity first saw light at Gobolion, near Wel-
lington (N.S.W.), and was bred by present owner, Mr. W.
A. Gardiner. He is by A.J.O. Derby winner Trenchant
from Loniia, by Welcome Jack (M.L.), from La Maresque,
by Maribyrnong, from Algeria (imp.). His previous best
feats were winning Squatters’ Handicap (A.J.C. Meeting
last year), and second to La Carabine in Sydney Oup.
• • • »
N.S.W. bred and trained filly Hautain, who
got away with the rich Debutant Stakes for 2-year-olds at
Caulfield on Saturday after leading 15 others throughout,
was foaled at Mr. Sam Hordern's Wilton Park stud farm,
and sold as a yearling for 700 gs. after keen bidding.
Hautain secured more than her cost at first time ot ask-
ing. Debutant Stakes event was inaugurated in 'Bl, since
which time nine fillies have got home. Other winners of
same race prepared by trainer of Hautain (Tom Payten)
were Cocos, Metford, and Trieste, and he also hail some-
thing to do with Victor Hugo and Acme. Mr. W. Bailey
(owner of Hautain) hasn’t been on a racecourse for years
through sickness. Hautain is by the St. Simon horse
Haut Brion from Spite (imp.).
* * * *
After Kinglike had beaten the more brilliant,
but less plucky, Finland for the Caulfield Guineas, there
was talk of the two favorites, Maltster and Hautboy,
having been much knocked about in the race. If they
were handicapped with “interference” as well as 71b.
penalties in the saddle, the defeat of said Maltster and
Hautboy leaves their Derby chances unchanged. But
what do the punting public think of a classic race in
which the two first favorites in a small field of seven are
not given a fair run ?

• • • •
A tip for Caulfield Cup needs a lot of luck
before it can bring any credit to the tipster. Last year
La Carabine was named in this column, hut the brave
mare that subsequently won an Australian Cup and a
Sydney Cup finished only fourth for the 1£ mile race. On
Saturday she will have to carry 281bs. more than the
weight she failed under last year—failed because the
course is not a good one for her long stride. Yet she is
fancied this time. Given a chance to work into a favor-
able position before rounding the turn for home La Cara-
bine should get very near to the prize. Which remark
applies more or less to the lot of them.
» * • *
Present scribe, whilst quite prepared to despise
his own tip before the flag falls, will now take seven
horses to choose from : La Carabine, Gauleon, G'ornquist,
Fleet Admiral, Ingliston, Sagacity, Kinglike, and Barba-
rossa. These, in preference to Mora, Vocalist, War God
(likely to make a bigger show in Melb. Cup), Kenley,
Fadladeen, Strathjoy, and others. The tipster who
doesn’t mention “others” runs an absurd risk of igno-
miny when the numbers go up. The old horses appear
to be holding theirown against the youngsters this season,
and as the old ones generally had the best of it at Caul-
field until of late years, and Fleet Admiral is an estab-
lished Bulletin favorite with recent “ form ” very much
in his favor, the tip goes
Fleet Admiral—l,
Cornquist—2,
La Carabine—3,
also Barbarossa as an emergency pick. This scribe’s two-
and-sixpence will be entrusted to Fleet Admiral if it sees
the post.
* * • •
Charles Arnold has drawn Rapid in Tattersall’s
sweep on Caulfield Cup, “ and,” says Arnold, “ there
might he something in the coincidence that Rapid is a
Tasmanian horse and my company and I are now off to
Tasmania. Once before I had a coincidence ; thus—three
horses, Theodore, Bertha, and Murdoch, were to run in
one day, Theodore is my brother’s name and Bertha
Murdoch is the name of my married sister. Ah ! 1 said,
if a coincidence is worth backing here goes. So I
backed ail three horses and they all won. But I don't
believe in my dreams. Oh, no 1 if 1 dream a horse will
win he loses for certain.”

Boxer: Kinglike, on performances , stands out the
best thing in Caulfield Cup to-day (Saturday). L. Kuhn,
who rode him in the Guineas last Saturday and won Caul-
field Oup on*Dewey last year, will have the mount to-day.
Kinglike is by Wallace-Bonnie Rosette and was secured at
the dispersal of St. Albans stud for 9uogs. by present
owner, Mr. C. L. M'Donald.
• • * •
On Saturday’s win, Severity with 8.7 looks good
for Melb. Cup, and is now first favorite—lo’s offered and
12’s wanted. Vic. touts were quite taken with his track
work, averring that no more attractive galloper had been
seen for years. • His action is so even as to almost place
question of staying beyond doubt, and he can always put
forth that spurt so useful in a long-distance handicap.
Severity’s trainer was a bit behind with the preparation
of Voyou and Positano in previous years (former, though
running second in last year's Oup, was a much better nag
on last day of meeting). Same mistake hasn’t occurred
with Severity, who is “ cherry ripe” and only needs to
keep going as at present to make his prospects of annex-
ing the big thing of the year second to none.
But half-a-dozen different lines of argument
might be drawn from some of the “ form” shown at Caul-
field last Saturday. For instance, Severity's victory in the
Stakeswould seem to dispose of Merriwee’s chance to win
Melbourne Cup, if the little black horse was wound up
for the nine-fUrlong race, which (in this scribe’s
opinion) he wasn’t. Also Cornquist, Gauleon, and
George Frederick (the latter did not accept) might
be reckoned “out” of the Caulfield Cup on their
Saturday running, if one could accept the Stakes as a
trustworthy guide to impending events. In the light of
experience, nothing that happens in the Caulfield first
day’s racing can be accepted as conclusive evidence
against' all the beaten nags. Thos : that win, or go near
to winning, are palpably fit and well, but among the
many that don’t run conspicuously only a few can
be blaekmarked with confidence. One thing is certain,
Severity beat Paul Pry on his merits over the Stakes
course. For the rest, Merriwee, Tarquin, George
Frederick, and Gauleon promise to cut better figures in
loriger-drstance races this Spring. Gungadin is another
soft chestnut that looks unlikely to distinguish himself
by November.
Monday s mile and three furlongs in 2.28 (best
gallop up to then this season) brought the M.L. crack,
Seahorse, into greater favor for Melb. Cup. Lancaster is
in the betting again ; and, with Severity, disputes first
favoritism at 100 to 9 as the result of a fast mile with his
stable chum Sagacity.

Last year The Bulletin fancied Lochaber for
Toorak Handicap, and the long-backed son of Lochiel
finished lame four lengths behind everything. This year
he carried Bst. 71b. four lengths in front of everything.
Seeing that Lochaber was scratched for the two Cups
some weeks ago,, it is reasonable to suspect trainer Phil.
Heywood of having something much better in the stable,
and George Frederick is presumably the one.
It is seldom a race-meeting passes without an
offspring of Lochiel's getting to the front. On Saturday
at Caulfield (Vic.) Lochaber won the Toorak Handicap;
and at Rosehill (Sydney) Rosehill October Handicap fell
to the Lochiel horse Flodden.

Westralian T.C. New Year programme is out.
Stakes bigger than ever. Perth Cup will be worth £lOOO,
and Railway Stakes £5OO. Meeting is to cover four days,
and cost £2400 altogether. Mo., for privilege of betting,
is asked to pay £6O for this, and £l2 for by-meetings, but
he calls it an outrage, and vows that he’ll emigrate first.
But the game is too good. Henceforth, Ikey won’t be
tolerated alongide the tote, as heretofore, but will have a
“ cage ” inside the paddock all to himself, so that he’ll be
found easily, and can’t play up with the machine’s opera-
tions.

“ X.” : Re the suggestion of a match between
bulky Fred Preston and Otto Cribb. It is printed that
Hegarty made a draw with Cribb, but fact is that they
never met, and are extremely unlikely to meet (Hegarty
is a feather and Cribb a middleweight). Of course 'twould
he ridiculous to talk of Hegarty fighting Preston, but not
so much so as is the assertion that he fought a draw with
Cribb.
• • * •
Pug. patrons flocked to Golden Gate A.C.
(Sydney), Saturday night, and filled every inch of space,
to see how the so-far unconquerable Otto Cribb's brother
Arthur would shape against Rollo. Arthur Cribb, like
his brother, is a barber, and, judged by his initial showing
in the ring, ought to go back to shampooing quick.
Nature never built him for fist-fighting—he hasn’t the
physique, and owns a most pronounced nose, which must
always be in the road, and bring him a couple of inches
nearer t’other fellow than he ought to be. Every swing
and jab (short and all, though most were) smashed up
against Arthur’s “smeller”; he caught all the stoush
without giving any back for close on 20min., and then
caved in to a man who wouldu’t be near a “ cert.” among
the novices.

Looks as if rough heavy-weights, Sydney Tim
Nolan and Victorian Tut Ryan, will meet in Melb. soon.
They’ll fight in about same style, and each’ll thump as
hard as t’other; but Nolan owns most vitality. “Oi’ll
bate him shure,” says Tiin.
* • • •
Famous black pugilist Peter Jackson has a good
engagement with Fitzgerald’s Circus that may build him
up again. Barring accidents he’ll be with the show in
Melbourne, S.A., Tas., and M.L., and back here next
April. Six months ago it looked any odds against him
surviving till now, but excepting occasional twinges of
sciatica he now feels fit enough for anything, and scales
close on Isst.—as heavy as ever.
« • * •
Home-coming sprint premier Stan. Rowley, in-
terviewed at Adelaide, attributes the remarkable
success of Yankee runners and jumpers (who had with
them a big retinue of skilled trainers and negro assistants)
at British championships and Olympic Games in Paris to
“systematic and scientific training.” The Englishmen
have more stamina, and always won over long distances,
hut in weight-putting, pole-leaping, jumping and sprint-
ing the U.S. reps, were unapproachable. The tendency
of Americans to overdo physical training to the neglect
of other things has long been noticed. Phenomenal
’Murrikan “iepper” Kraenzlein’s recent remark in Eng-
land, “It is about time I began to think of something
more serious,” has a plaintive ring in it. The U.S. ath-
lete makes training his whole aim and object for a time,
whereas the average Australian or Britisher makes it more
of a recreation than aught else.

Roberts-Weiss billiard match began in Sydney
on Wednesday last week, but up to time of writing hasn’t
caught on a bit. Reduced prices brought bigger crowds
on Saturday, but the two sessions didn’t draw 300.
Roberts brightened up a heap in the afternoon, showing
more dash and confidence than previously, and (after
getting 122) scored the break of the match (306) in
lSfmiri., bringing quite his best style' to bear. Later he
notched 84 and 166 while there was a corresponding fall-
ing off in Weiss’ play. The venue will be transferred to
Melb. this Thursday.

knocked up at different stages 89 (unfinished from
previous day) 240, 83, 169, (in 9ijmin.), 131, 135, 99and 155.

Australian champion swimmer Lane left Eng.
homeward bound last week. Hellings, who lias been
away for years, talked of leaving this month, and may be
aboard same boat. Leo, another front-ranker and winner
of all M. L. championships one year, is also here again
hence this should prove the most interesting swimming
season yet seen in Australia. Leo went to S’Africa four
or five years ago, and when war broke out joined the
Rhodesian Horse and was in Mafekmg throughout the
•iege.
• .• * ... *
Mailed that F. Lane, at Guernsey, last month,
covered the 109yds straightway in 59 l-ssec., and, Guern-
sey people say, without tidal aid. If English A.S.A.
passed this ’twould be the world's best, but writer is
afraid there’s no such luck, especially as British record-
conditions call for “ enclosed still water unaffected by
wind or tide.” In view of facts that Lane never before
got within 3sec. of time registered, and that Guernsey is
one of the Channel Islands, it seems hardly likely 7 that
there wasn’t some current, however little. That Lane
was in great form was made plain by his performance
against Englishman Derbyshire in the furlong champion-
ship.
* • * *
Australian Lane couldn’t start for British
quarter-mile supremacy, September Ist, through
illness, but cornstalks Lind berg and Jack Hellings
entered. Lindberg gave up before covering 100yds. ;
he had no right there at all. Hellings got out at
830yds. ; and English crack Jarvis won easily by 70yds
from next man in 12.25. A rough sea and strong cur-
rent much impeded progress. Last year in this race
Lane sprang a big surprise by beating the ’Ome
champion after a hot finish. Time then was 6.304-5.
# * * #
Quite a winter’s day on Saturday for the start
of Sydney cricket season. Paddington, playing
against Central Cumberland, scored 299, of which
Trumper made 94 and F. Gow 88. Gow made his debut
on Saturday in senior cricket, and looks likely to make a
worthy successor to Alex. Mackenzie, who now resides in
another borough. Trumper and Gow put on 163 for the
second wicket. Leichhardt-Balmain scored 117 against
Sydney District’s 113 for two wickets, which makes the
match a certainty for latter team. The only century
scored during the day was by F. Punch, who, playing for
North Sydney Eleven, scored. 142 against Waverley
Eleven.

Harry Trott made his appearance on the cricket
field in Melbourne on Saturday when he captained his old
club, the South Melbourne.

“ Bias ” : Composition of N.S.W. bowling team
for Vic. next month isn't finding favor again this year,
despite the fact that a specially-appointed committee of
five selected them. Biggest kick is against inclusion as
captain of an erstwhile N.S.W. bowler now domiciled in
Melbourne. Most of nine emergencies are complaining
about being asked to pay their own exes, over, on the
off-chance of getting a game, when each of the chosen
16 is a “cert” to go. Though the team may be a bit
stronger than any previously sent away, it isn’t nearly
up to expectations, as a lot of fore-front men held aloof.

Final day of Sydney League’s bike sports drew
but a small crowd and emphasised the opinion lately here
vented that the popularity of wheel races is waning very
much. Ben Goodson easily won the 5 miles amateur
supremacy by Isyds. in 11.58 3-ssec. Later he met
Megson in event—“ winner of pro. v. winner of amateur
premierships”—l mile, but had no hope, as Megson went
away towards the end and won as he liked in 2.9 l-ssec.
Megson also secured First Class Handicap (Hmiles) from
scratch with Tucker (a Sydney coming good ’un) 92yds.,
second and F. Beauchamp (Tas.), scratch, third. Time
3.9 3-5. Megson wound up by (with Walne) gathering in
laurels and money attached to Tandem premiership of the
continent (3 miles). Only three couples lined up. The
thing was a farce till the last quarter mile as each took a
turn at raking in lap-prizes ; then they had every oz. on
and almost finished ahreastr, Megson and Walne winning
by inches from Lewis and Forbes with Carpenter and
Hunt a close third. Time 7.01-5. Last lap was done in
20sec.

Although a rider may possess one of the very
best machines on the market, its merits are set at naught
by a pair of bad tyres, devoid of resilience and reliability;
but on the other hand a moderate machine is consider-
ably enhanced in value when fitted with Dunlops.*
AN IRRESPONSIBLE TIP FOR MELBOURNE CUP.
I’d been to the races, but very few traces
Had I of the pile I intended to win ;
’Twas easy to spot ’em, I reckoned, but rot ’em,
The cuddies I backed were the last to come in.
The lucre I let drop on Epsom and Metrop. ;
Oh, where, and oh where, is that hardly-earned tin ;
It snugly reposes in keeping of Moses,
In case I’d be tempted to waste it in sin.
As sitting disgusted, and pretty near busted,
The night of the Metrop., in Flanagan’s bar,
My chair in position against a partition,
In front of me, handy, a glass of three-star.
My thoughts went a-fleeting to Melbourne’s Spring Meet-
ing,
Wild yearnings I nourished to win back my pelf;
Oh, would that some airy beneficent fairy
Reveal the Oup winner, I said to myseif.
And then I upstarted, with lips widely parted,
Behind the partition a rusty voice said :
“ George Frederick ’ll win it; there’s nothing else in it;
It’s a moral, I tell yer, ’e’ll smother ’em dead.
“ An’ listen to me, sir. I’m no blanky geyser,
1 tell yer I know what the beggar can do ;
It’s no bloomin’ guiver ; you scrape up a fiver
An’ plank it on now ’e’s at fifty to two.
“ 'E showed ’em a trial —cut out the two mi-al
In 3.29 an’ not 'ardlv wound up ;
An’ look at the thumpin’ big weight ’e was humpin’,
A stone more’n ’e’s arst to put up in the Oup."
A wish to be seeing this tipstering being,
Impelled me behind the partition to glance,
And there stood a hairy and beer-sodden “ fairy,”
Beguiling a swell with his little romance.
It may be a goat is a man who takes notice
Of booze-iaden loafers with yarns readied up ;
But somehow I'm aching the risk to be taking,
And punt on George Fred’rick to pull off the Cup.
G.A.P.

Mr. A. 0. Godhard (formerly sub-manager
of the Union Bank of Australia, Sydney, now
managing-director of the Co-operative Coupon
Co.) informs The Bulletin that he considers cer-
tain unfavorable remarks upon the coupon system
printed by it in a recent issue as reflecting upon
the honesty of his particular institution, and
desires this paper to make it quite plain that no
such reflection was intended. This The Bulletin
cheerfully proceeds to do : After carefully reading
the remarks in question, it quite fails to see how
any such interpretation can reasonably be placed
upon them ; and, in any case, nothing was further
from the intention of the writer than to asperse
the personal character of Mr. Godhard or the
business-standing of his company, which, The
Bulletin has no reason- to doubt, conducts its
operations with an integrity commensurate with
the excellent character enjoyed by its manager.
Concerning the Co-operative coupon system, Mr.
Godhard writes thus :
THE CO-OPERATIVE COUPON SYSTEM.
The system provides a method by which retailers are
enabled to give a discount of 5 per cent, on every 6d
cash purchase. The company charges retailers 2s 6d
for every 100 coupons, and the retailer gives his cus-
tomer one coupon on every 6d cash purchase. One
coupon is worth 3-10ths of a penny. There is no coin to
represent this amount. The customer saves these
coupons until 100 or more are collected, when they are
brought to the company, who give goods to the retail
value of 2s 6d for every 100 coupons presented.
As some people consider that cash should he given for
coupons, it is necessary to point out that, if the com-
pany received 2s 6d from the retailer, it could not give
2s 6d cash to his customer, as working expenses have to
be paid : but the company can and xhould give goods to
the full retail value of 2s 6d for every 100 coupons pre-
sented ; as the difference between the wholesale price
at which goods are bought from the manufacturers, and
the retail price, at which they are redeemed, enables
the company to advertise tradesmen giving coupons,
establish showrooms, and pay working expenses. Re-
tail value is all that the public are entitled to.
► The retailer, who gives coupons, increases his cash
trade, and consequently avoids had debts, and saves cost
of booking. He also saves in advertising, as the company
do this for him. The gain thus arising is much more
than the cost of coupons.
The public, for the first time, are enabled to receive a
discount on small cash purchases, and those who collect
00-operative coupons are compelled to save them until
100 or more are collected, and thus are taught habits of
thrift.
The system acts as a sort of Savings Bank.
If it were practicable to give such discounts in coin of
the realm, the coins would individually be of such small
value that they would be frittered away.
The fundamental principle of the system is the en-
couragement of cash trading, and it is claimed that,
wherever it has been introduced, cash trading has in-
creased, and credit trading has proportionately decreased.
_ In districts where the system is in full swing insolven-
cies have largely decreased, while in the districts where it
has not been in operation, there has either been no re-
duction, or an increase in the number of schedules filed.
_ It must be admitted that it is just to make a distinc-
tion between the buyer for cash, and the person who
obtains credit.
■ Now, as both the trader who gives co-operative coupons
and the public who collect them benefit materially, it
may well be asked “ Who objects to the system?” and
here it must be remembered that most beneficent move-
ments have met with bitter opposition at the outset.
Who objects to the coupon system ? Not the trades-
people who have? adopted it. (1600 of these tradesmen
Sectioned the Victorian Parliament against the proposed
oupons Abolition Bill),
Tradesmen who do not give ooupons objeot, no doubt,
as they would object to anything which attracted trade
to their competitors.
Some wholesale houses object for the reason that the
retailer who gives coupons is generally a “ free man,”
and is not tied to one particular wholesale house; the
consequence being that the “ tied retailer ” leans more
heavily on the wholesaler, while the “ free man ” becomes
prosperous.
Large monopolistic firms object, because it enables the
small retailer to hold his own against them. In Mel-
bourne, the small grocers have been enabled to success-
fully compete with a monopolistic firm with 100 branches.
Some newspapers object—partly because they do not
understand the system, and partly because some of their
large advertisers object. In Melbourne suburbs it has
been proved that the system localises trade, especially
with regard to drapery, people buying their drapery
locally instead of going into the city.
Some novices in Political Economy object, on the
grounds that it introduces a middleman; but these
should remember that, as society is constituted, it is
necessary to have middlemen to bridge over the gulf
between the producer and the consumer—and if it can
be proved (and it can) that the coupon system saves
more than it costs, then it lessens the cost to the con-
sumer.
It is also objected that it diverts trade to those who
adopt it. It undoubtedly does ; and in this age of com-
petition this is what we are all striving for. This objec-
tion is so sentimental that it is not worth discussing.
With the exception of the “ Political Economist
Novice,” all the objectors are interested parties.
The 1500 Tradesmen, who give Coupons, stated in a
Parliamentary Petition, that they found the System
Most Beneficial to their business. It is clear that they do
not object.
The Public do not object.—Petitions signed by about
60,000 people were presented to the Victorian Parliament
against the proposed Bill.
The death “ blow ” —funeral eulogies.
At Grafton (N.S.W.) recently a man was
fined £5 for cruelly ill-using a horse. Same week
on Richmond River a man was fined £1 for
cruelly ill-using two children. But then a horse
is saleable property and a child isn’t. Good old
Property!

A tin-plate, llin. by Bin., was recently
sent uncovered to London from Singapore, the
address being pasted on the plate itself. It was
promptly returned by the English G.P.0., as not
“packed in accordance with regulations.” Thus,
though it could not be delivered to the addressee,
one mile from London G.P.Q., it could be
sent 9000 miles to Singapore.
One of the instructions given to Sir Chas.
Hotham, when he was appointed Governor of
Victoria in *53, was to enquire into the truth of a
report that several millions sterling had been em-
bezzled in the carelessly-managed Govt, depart-
ments of the province. The voucher system was
of such a lax character that the point could not be
settled.

EXTRACT FROM THE “ GREY RIVER ARGUS," DATED JULY 7th, 1900.
This very unusual sight of dredges winning gold within a stone’s-throw of a town-hall will shortly be witnessed
at Greymouth, when a Sydney syndicate, that has obtained the valuable concession to work Erua Moana and Karoro
Lakes from the Borough of Greymouth Corporation, starts to work. The gold-dredging record of the Molyneux is
undoubtedly a magnificent one, but, should the West Coast live up to its former marvellous yields, the Molyneux
Clutha, and other Otago rivers will certainly lose their proud pre-eminence. The Greymouth Lagoons Gold-Dredging
Company, which, we understand, is floated in Sydney, will, with the foreshores at their disposal, together with the
Borough Lease of 63 acres, have an area of at least 400 acres—sufficient to keep four companies busy for many years to
come. The careful series of bores put down on this Company’s area by that most careful of pessimistic
mining authorities, Mr. Daniel, go to prove the existence of immense deposits of rich wash, that must
give a great impetus to gold - dredging on the West Coast. When it is realised that a seam of sand
fourteen feet thick, and worth an ounce to the load, has been proved to extend over a large portion
of the area available for dredging, some idea of the big potentialities of this Company may be realised. The
former riches of the West Coast are matters of history. From the Greenstone diggings alone, official records
show that 40 tons of gold were taken out by lucky prospectors. From the beach-sands immense fortunes were
won. On the Darkies’ Terrace the poorest ground yielded 11 ounces to the load, and it must be borne in mind
the prospectors of those days won all their gold with the primitive long-handled shovel and sluice box. Imagine the
dredge of to-day putting through these golden sands at the rate of 12,000 to 20,000 tons a week. That the West
Coast is confidently expected to eclipse the best Molyneux records is not surprising. On the Molyneux but 10 per
cent, of the silt passes over the tables ; the remaining 90 per cent, passes from the screens into the elevators Our
beach sands being free from boulders, snags and heavy gravels, half, at least, of the silt brought up by the buckets
will pass over the tables. The cost of dredge construction on the Molyneux is much in excess of Greymouth and the
West Coast. Instance, timber, which on the Molyneux costs 40s. per 100 ft., in Greymouth can be bought for 8s 6d
Carriage of material by lumbering waggons to the Molyneux is terribly expensive ; in the case of the Greymouth
Lagoons, the Greymouth and Hokitika Railway actually skirts the borders of the claims to be worked, added to
which the great advantage of a foundry on the spot, capable of effecting without delay any necessary repairs to
dredges, is a boon that cannot be over-estimated. With the hundreds of dredging companies that have recently been
started to test the West Coast. Greymouth will probably occupy the proud position of being the centre of the biggest
development of this marvellous industry in the world.”
This Company is formed for the purpose of working a valuable concession obtained from the Borough of Grev-
mouth Town Corporation, comprising Erua Moana and Karoro Lakes, in all some 170 acres (without considering
available foreshores shown in plan), and also the adjoining “ Borough ” Claim, comprising 63 acres. *
Recognising the extreme value of this area for gold-dredging purposes, many efforts have been made to secure the
Erua Moana and Karoro Lakes, but hitherto without success. The marvellous yields formerly obtained in the district
by diggers (who were unable to go deeper than 13 feet without being flooded out bv the ocean) is well known
Claims 45 x 45 feet worked with long-handled shovels in this locality yielded from £7OO to £l5OO per man. On the
“ Darkies’ Terrace ” the poorest ground gave lloz. per square yard, andifliesbest claim yielded £3OO per man per week
Recently a great gold-dredging fever has sprung up at Greymouth and along the West Coast, 2600 claims having been
applied for during the past 12 months.
Mr. Daniel, F.G.S., M.1.M.E., at considerable expense, has put down a series of 25 bores on the “ Borough ” and
adjoining Ball Dredger leases, and the result is a remarkable confirmation of the statements as to the richness of this
area. These bores varied in depth from 18ft. 6in. to 35ft. Steel-shod perforated pipes were driven into the ground
and the sand extracted by a Douglas pump. Mr. Daniel, in the course of his report, which is attached remarks •
“ 1 sank bore No. 13, which cut the seam 18ft. from the surface ; here it proved to be 14ft. thick, and prospected over
an ounce to the cubic yard.” Further—“ So far as prospecting has been done on the Borough lease it has shown
that the ground actually tested averages 39 grains to the cubic yard over the whole extent. It is impossible to assert
with certainty what the yield will be in actual work. The data furnished bv 19 inch and a-half bore-holes is not
sufficient to estimate the value of over 2,000,000 yards of uneven layers of gold-bearing drift; but the magnificent
results obtained justify me in asserting that the ground is phenomenally rich dredging ground.’k “
The concession from the Greymouth Corporation has been obtained on very advantageous 'terms having bppn
secured before the advent of the boom. The rental for the first year is but one shilling per acre, the two years ensn
ing 2s. 6d. per acre, thenceforth ss. per acre. The Company is under an obligation to deposit the spoil under fhe
direction of the Borough Council Engineer, so as not to interfere with dock in course of construction The area i-o
be dredged is entirely free from boulders or snags, thus permitting very rapid treatment. It is intended to forth
with place a dredge in position, to be followed by others as opportunity permits.
The Hokitika Railway runs within a stone’s throw of these holdings. Sawn timber (which costs 40s on th»
Molyneux) can be brought for about for ss. fid. per hundred feet. ' I>ne
. ilJ r ““ * 7*, . f/ . ‘ J. J."* pump uitea sand only, as explained
in Mr. Daniel’s report, and, therefore, discarding the valuation of six shillings and sixpence per cubic ykrd and esti
mating the probable yield at less than one-third, it will be seen that a single dredge putting through but 12 500
a week should yield a profit of over t 50,000 a year. A gold-dredging venture with such brilliant prospects hwrorMv
been offered the public for subscription. rarely
In conclusion, to quote Mr. Daniel“ The facility of working, wealth of spoil, and evenness of gold distrihuHnn
of the virgin leads of the Lagoon system should make them rank amongst the best dividend pavers of anv New
land beach or river ventures 7 ’

HEAD OFFICE—MELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
Twenty-Fifth Report presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 31st October, 1900.
The Directors have pleasure in placing before Shareholders the results of the half-year's operations to 29tb Sep-
tember, as shown in the annexed Balance Sheet and Statement of Accounts, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £3,258 Is. 6d. brought from last half-year) amount to £9,121 0 6
out of which it is proposed
To pay a dividend at the rate of 4 per cent, per annum, absorbing , .. .. 3,000 0 0
And to carry forward the balance £6,121 0 6
The Directors are pleased to be able to report a very considerable increase in deposits during the half-year, which
is gratifying evidence of the confidence reposed in the bank, and will enhance profits when the additional funds thus
placed at the directors’ disposal can be fully utilised, but, in the meantime, the considerable proportion lying unem-
ployed restricts profit earnings, although it strengthens the depositors’ security.
' The Directors gladly recognise the assistance rendered by shareholders in introducing new deposits and cus-
tomers, and trust to receive a continuance of this support in the future. It is in this way that the business of the
bank can be most readily increased and the interests of shareholders advanced.
By Order of the Board,
-H. T. WILSON, Manager.

A LETTER FROM THE FRONT.
Preliminary comment on Khaki, and the
Story of what an English Soldier did in an
Emergency.
“The Khaki,” says an American newspaper
correspondent in South Africa, “is the British
soldier’s only protection. He cannot learn to
advance under cover, and is sure to stand up and
stretch himself against a skyline.”
When a Boer sharpshooter, hidden behind a
boulder, is watching out for any wandering
Tommies that may be in the neighbourhood it
is well for the latter to look as nearly as possible
like the rocks, the soil, and the grass.
Hence the commonsenical Khaki.
The mud turtle and the crocodile understand
the value of the low color-tones, the one animal
resembling a rusty stone and the other a rotting
log. They bid for no experimental rifle practice
from Cook’s tourists “as they sail, as they sail,”
Not they. Like Brer Fox, they lie low, and take
the air in their native khaki.
But prowling burghers are not the only enemies
our army has to fight in South Africa. The
casualty list in the papers is sorry, yet instructive,
reading. Wounds in lamentable plenty, and
diseases of the stomach and bowels; these are the
leading points of the story.
The boys fight like the gallant chaps they are,
and they conquer ; but the price of victory!—
might it not be less ?
Early in May a dead Englishman was found on
the veldt near the Vet River. He lay in the
middle of a large clear space. Horse and rifle were
gone. His water bottle lay beside him, and
balanced on it was a chess pawn. The initials
“ N,Me. W,’’were on his handkerchief. Was it a
shot, or was it disease? Poor fellow ! And as to
those who loved him ?
“I was sent down from the front,” writes a
soldier, “very bad with fever and dysentery.
The doctors-cured these complaints, but my
stomach was in such a state that I could take no
solid food.
“On eating I suffered frightfully with pain in
the pit of the stomach. I told the doctor about
it, and he gave me medicine which did me no
earthly good.
“Instead of getting better I grew worse. I
could scarcely sleep or walk.
“Not knowing what to do or where to go for
help, I told a comrade of my condition—not that
I supposed he could do anything, but because I
wanted sympathy and friendship in my trouble.
“ ‘Why, he cried, ‘your ailment is indigestion
6r dyspepsia; probably with an inflamed
stomach. The thing for that—the very best
medicine in the world—is Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
It’s famous all over the world ; you must have
often heard of it in England.’
“ I told him I had, but had not used it, as I
was always well when at home.
“ ‘You’re bad enough, now,, anyway,’ he said,
‘and ought to begin taking Mother Seigel right
off the reel.’
“I went directly to the stores and got a bottle.
But they charged 3s. 6d. for the same bottle I
have seen sold at home for Is. IJd., and I thought
the price was outrageous, even for South Africa.
“You know what a soldier’s pay is, and that
Tommy Atkins can’t afford to throw money away.
However, I don’t begrudge that three and a half
bob, for the Syrup turned out to be the best
investment I ever made in my life.
“ Thanks to that small bottle of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup, I am again in good form for anything that
comes along, the Boers included. I am going to
the front to-morrow morning, and, with any sort
of good luck, will be with my regiment in a week
or so.
“Why, the Syrup acted so quickly that I was
much better after three doses. I have been in
this country seven months, and am healthier
now than at.any time since we landed at Table
Bay.
“ When I began to break down I was afraid I
should be invalided home, and now I am going
to have my share of the best fun of the campaign.
Once more I blow the bugle of praise for your
grand remedy, and only wish every man in the
aimy had a bottle of Mother Seigel’s Syrup in hi 3
haversack. lam sure that, by keeping the men
well, it would do almost as much to end this war,
and to drive old Kruger into permanent retire-
ment, as 50,000 fresh troops and a dozen shiploads
of lyddite shells.
“ God save the Queen, and reward Mother
Seigel.—Yours, Bds. Wallace Dove, Ist Sherwood
Foresters, Field Force, c/o Shaw Bros., Naauw-
poort, S. Africa. May 15th, 1900.”
“An army marches on its stomach,” said
Frederick the Great, and Mother Seigel’s Syrup
keeps that organ strong and steady.
Old Favorites.
Ten Years Ago.
Dost thou remember the old garden, where
We used to steal,
To build our silly castles in the air,
My pale Lucille ?
I was thy Knight; and thou, my love and Queen,
No shame didst know —
For had we not played—babies on the green—
Ten years ago ?
We part. We meet. Thou statelier grown, and cold,
I gaunt and grey,
For thou art rich, and I—in sorrows old
Since childhood’s day.
“ Lucille ! at last, my love 1 ” —your pale cheek flames.
“ Did you not know
My husband, sir ; we met —when was it, James?”
“ Ten years ago ! ”
Well—mine the fault was if I did not please,
You judged the best ;
You feared for poverty, and longed for ease,
Comfort, and rest.
His horses stepped as high, your diamonds made
As brave a show,
For all he won them in the tallow-trade
Ten years ago.
Yet that white brow, methinks, is less serene
Than in that time,
When bright birds sang, and trees and fields were green
In youth’s fair prime ;
When all the world smiled rosy at our feet
In fancy’s glow—
Ah,me! what wondrous dreamswe dreamed, my sweet,
Ten years ago !
Now you are sadly learned, I am told
Five tongues you speak ;
You sing, compose—what leaf is that you fold ?
Plato in Greek !
I see—you study at all times—you fret
At progress slow ;
—You had not needed Greek, had we but met
Ten years ago 1
Nay, never blush, Lucille. lam not base
To him or you.
From thy soul’s cell no love must his displace
Thy whole life through.
His safeguard and thy solace lie in this—
Is it not so ?
His constant kindness since the bridal kiss
Ten years ago.
We meet. We part. If life’s bright best be lost,
Much still remains ;
Perhaps a higher heaven for him , the cost,
Paid with thy pains.
Good-bye, my dear, and if this tale you tell,
These verses show ;
Say only, “ This man fought a hard fight well,
Ten years ago.”
And ever fights ! For if, as churchmen say,
In skies above
Soul mates with soul, as ray melts into ray,
And Heaven is Love,
He will be there, and —if he still loves thee —
Must never know
That thou on earth hadst e’er a thought for me
Ten years ago.
MARCUS CLARKE.
Album Verses.
What can I write in thee, O dainty book ?
About whose daintiness faint perfume lingers,
Into whose pages dainty ladies look,
And turn thy dainty leaves with daintier fingers ?
Fitter my ruder muse for ruder song;
My scrawling quill to coarser paper matches ;
My voice, in laughter raised too loud and long,
Is hoarse and cracked with shouting tavern-catches !
No melodies have I for lady’s ear,
No roundelays for jocund lads or lasses ;
But only brawlings born of bitter beer,
And chorussed with the clink and clash of glasses.
do tell thy mistress, pretty friend, for me,
I cannot do her ’hest for all her frowning ;
Whilst dust and ink are but polluting thee,
And vile tobacco-smoke thy leaves embrowning.
Thou breathest purity and homely worth—
The simple jest, the light laugh following after,
I will not jar upon thy modest mirth
By harsher jest, or by less gentle laughter.
So some poor tavern-haunter steeped in wine,
With staggering footsteps thro’ the streets returning,
Seeing thro’ gath’ring night a sweet light shine
From household lamp in happy window burning.
May pause an instant in the wind and rain
To gaze on that sweet scene of love and duty,
But turns into the wild wet night again,
Lest his sad presence mar its holy beauty.
MARCUS OLARKE.

A pleasant feature of Nellie Stewart’s return to Mel-
bourne was her rapturous hugging of her old dad,
treasurer Stewart, on the boat.
George Melville died suddenly, week before last, at the
Old Actors’ Homes, Melbourne, and many old friends at-
tended his funeral. An amiable fellow—a very respect-
able actor of the useful type. Geo. Melville is remem-
bered as one of the Auld Licht elders in “ The Little Mini-
ster,” and up to Nov. last, when he was playing with Hol-
loway and Anderson’s co. at Melbourne Royal, he could
always take his own part with credit. His professional
career had_ a tragic ending, for he was struck speechless
by paralysis on the_ Royal stage during a performance.
Melville’s wife, Marion Medway, survives him.
Marshall Hall’s professional visit to Sydney will not
happen until after 20th Dec. in any case. Melbourne
Oonservatorium of Music closes for the summer vacation
Dec. 16th, and then the “ Master ” will he at liberty to
give a series of lectures and concerts to the poor heathen
beyond the Murray.
Almost definitely decided that charming Antoinette
Trebelli will start another Australian concert tour next
April under her new platform name of Antonia Dolores.
This time she is to open in Sydney.
Yet another Boer-war lecturer to the front—this time
in the person of Mr. C. M’lvor, who has returned to
Sydney minus a leg,—broken by the kick of a horse on
board a transport bound for South Africa. When the
sufferer was, later on, landed at Albany, amputation had
become necessary. Mr. M'lvor begins at Burwood,
Sydney, and intends to tour N.S.W.
A significant remark is made by Nellie Stewart in an
Argus interview. When questioned as to the progress of
Amy Castles, Nellie replied : ‘ Madame Marchesi was so
far unable to pronounce judgment upon little Amy’s
future, as her voice had been so badly strained that its
possibilities were not manifest.”
Donald Macdonald was last heard of at Dunedin (M.L.)
—reception by the Journalists’ Institute, money turned
away from his lectures, and all things as they have been
at every point of his tour. The work seems to be telling
on Mac. somewhat. Manager Edwin Geach sends along a
capital photo, of the “ Man from Ladysmith ’’and himself
in an unprofessional group, and the M.f.L. looks even a
little more hungry than sardineic Edwin. But perhaps
the photographer worked lines of suffering into Mac’s
cheek.
Mainly about Wybert Reeve : “ Within a week or two
Australia will lose the handsome old man who has in re-
cent years managed Adelaide Royal. Wybert Reeve has
been a pro. since he was 18. He has written many plays,
and, strangely enough, his first (written when he’was '2O)
was called “ An Australian Hoax.” Reeve battled for 20
years in the provinces before a London chance came, and
one of his first characters was Count Fosco in Wilkie
Collins’ “ Woman in White.” Collins was one of Reeve’s
greatest friends. Reeve came to Australia under engage-
ment to Coppin in 78, and appeared as Fosco, probably'
his only part worth remembering. Subsequently was
lessee of Melb. Opera House and Bijou. Wybert Reeve
is a lovable old warrior, with a kindly heart, and leaves
many friends and not a single enemy behind him in
Adelaide.
“ Cro.” : Mrs. G. B. Lewis as Madame Vinard in
“ Trilby,” gave the best bit of original work discoverable
in the Melb. revival. Am surprised to find quite a
definite strain of humor in the buxom matron, who, when
last seen by me, was posturing as Hamlet on the boards of
Melh. Bijou. Not so very long ago either. Mrs. 0.8.L.’s
Hamlet was an exceedingly matronly young gentleman.
When Plimmer played Ingomar, the best joke in the
performance was the contrast between the elocution of
the cultured Greek maid and the terrible Barbarian. For
instance, after Nance had spoken in her own sledge-
hammer style —“ Two souls with but a stn-gle thought,
two hearts that beat an one,” the gentle Plimmah would
meditatively repeat the lines with the intonation and
general “ intensity ” of a modern drawing-room poet.
Editor Bulletin,—Re Melbourne Opera House
(“ Poverty Point,” 13/10/’00): In justice to ourselves we
will ask you to publish this letter, or the substance
thereof. Our designs for Melbourne Opera House were
1 approved " of by Mr. Rickards, passed by Melb. Board
of Health, and all other local authorities before Mr. Pitt
praotically drew a line. There certainly was a difference
between ourselves and Mr. Pitt, and we eventually went
out of the business. The “ root ” of all the trouble was,
that Mr. Pitt wanted to be “ joint ” architect with us,
while we were only willing that he should be our “ repre-
sentative.” But even as it was, had we altered our de-
signs to suit the ideas of the ground landlords and their
architect, Mr. Pitt, we would have been at work in
Bourke-street to-day. For our own protection, and in
the light of past experience, we attached a condition to
agreeing to the alteration, and, this condition not being
met, the severance resulted. We have been building
theatres for 12 years past, having five now to our credit,
and we are still going on building them. Mr. Rickards
gives us his Brisbane theatre to design, and has for our
work on the Melb. house paid us more money than if we
had carried the work through.- It is only fair to him to
say that he has treated us handsomely, and that there
will not be a law-suit.—Yours, Backhouse and Back-
house.
Billee Barlow’s 14-year-old son is quite the “ mother’s
boy ” —very like her in face, and with the same ingratiat-
ing turn of the tongue.
Musgrove has provided cloak-rooms for the Princess
gallery patrons, and has backed and padded the seats to
propitiate the gods.
They are preparing for pantomimes at three Melb.
theatres. Evidently one cannot have too much of a bad
thing.
George Rignold contemplates a farewell tour of Aus-
tralasia prior to his departure for London. He is now
busily engaged organising a company.
Died the other day, rather suddenly, at
the age of 44, journalist Thomas Veale. Mr.
Veale, who had latterly been conducting Sydney
Licensing Guardian, was at one time on the
Melbourne Age, and also founded in Sydney the
short-lived Tomahawk. He was a man of con-
siderable scholastic attainments, and was a well-
known figure about Sydney.
Reported that Jimmy Governor, one of
the Breelong white-blacks, has been wounded by
a shot fired by one of the hundreds of so-called
“ pursuers,” and the three-months “ chase ” may
end soon._ The method of “chase’’now adopted
is to garrison all the huts and houses in a radius
of 20 miles and wait for the two black-whites to
attack! And they do attack; they came to a
garrisoned hut and shot at a policeman the other
day—hunting the hunters with a vengeance !
The big meeting in Sydney Centennial
Hall to protest against the desecration of the fore-
shores of the Domain and any further inter-
ference with the Gardens was badly gene-
railed. It was a huge meeting, and ready to
make a record demonstration. Had it been led
by men who knew how to use a first-class weapon,
Sydney D.T. would have backed down, and by
next morning the idea of road-making in the
Domain would have been off the programme. Dr.
Cullen, W. Campbell, M.L.C., and Norton,
M.L. A., were cheered again and again ; and when
Norton finished the resolution should have been
put. It would then have been carried unanimously
by 5000 people, who were quite ready to proceed
to the Domain and wreck the hideous crane, tear
up the pegs, and otherwise give practical expres-
sion to their feelings. But the resolution wasn’t
put when Norton sat down amidst thunders of ap-
plause, and one small man after another was
allowed to get up and drivel out vain repetitions,
while the audience tired and filtered away. So it
came about that the resolution was not put till
more than half had drifted out, and a fine oppor-
tunity was lost.
A subscription is being raised to enable
Miss Helen Hart, long known in Australia as a
fearless advocate of, and tireless worker for, the
woman suffrage cause, to proceed to England.
Mr. J. P. Tredgold, Mayor of Leichhardt,
Sydney, will be pleased to receive large or small
sums.
The big foreign steam companies trading
with Australia and on the Australian coast have
advanced their passenger rates by 10 per cent,
owing to the great rise in the price of coal.
Welsh steam coal prior to six months ago was 20s.
in the English open market; now it is from 355.
to 405.; at Colombo three weeks ago Cardiff coal
was £6 10s. per ton !
It is proposed to organise a benefit on
behalf of Sydney journalist John I. Hunt, an
early contributor to The Bulletin and well-
known to-day as a humorous writer in the
Freeman’s Journal (“Flaneur”) and Sunday
Times (“Boondi”). The kind and gentle
“Johnny” has for long years been a desperate
sufferer from an agonising neuritic affection
which has utterly destroyed the sight of one eye
and now bids fair to leave him in total blindness.
But poor “Johnny” Hunt has endured his tor-
tures like a hero, sticking to his work throughout
with a cheerful pluck that has excited the astonish-
ment and admiration of the medical profession.
Now, however, he has at last to go into hospital,
and The Bulletin hopes that the proposed benefit
to the brave and clever little man will be as
successful as the case deserves.


From the letter of a N.S.W. soldier in
S’Africa:
Ever since Cronje’a capture the Boers have been fight-
ing gamely for a lost Cause. They are men—no matter
What anybody says, and if I could get away 1 wouldn’t
fight against them any more. I was prone behind a
big stone the other day, and Could have shot a Boer that
came out into the open, but I hadn’t the heart to do it.
Wonder would he have let me off.”
Judge Murray, in a case where Labor-
Supt. Creer was charged with saying what he
oughtn’t to one of his lambs, spoke the epitaph of
the man who tries to do his duty in N.S. Wales—
Bis Honor, in giving judgment, said that Mr. Oreer had
a difficult and very invidious duty to perform, and he
tried to perform it to the best Of his ability, and, in the
hature of things, became unpopular, because any man in
H.S.W. who tried to do his duty became unpopular. The
more anyone tried to do his duty the more unpopular he
became because he did his duty.
Verdict for plaintiff, with one farthing damages,
and no costs.
A Vic. Parliamentary Committee is now
hitting to inquire into the circumstances attend-
ing the dismissal of a railway porter named. Hall,
for giving information to another commission of
inquiry presided over by Judge Casey. .Hall
Claims that on the formation of this commission
information was sought from all, and it was inti-
mated that communications would be treated as
confidential. Hearing certain rumors he thought
worthy of investigation, Hall communicated them
to the commission who found they were incorrect,
and Judge Casey, he alleges, divulged his name to
the Dept, on the ground that his having given false
information deprived him of the protection of the
commission. If Hall’s assertions re this case
prove to be correct, another committee should be
quickly appointed to consider 'Whether Judge
Casey is a fit person to occupy his billet any
longer. If a man in his position cannot be
trusted to retain a confidence, tlie sooner Hovt.
secures One who can the better. Even If Halls
statements were not correct, he evidently made
them in good faith, and never yet was there a
commission appointed but had tO investigate a
heap Of baseless charges.
A Sydney “push ” synonym for wife is
“incubator.”
Humanity comments trenchantly on the
fact that some of the English newspapers pub-
lished, without a word of reprehension, such state-
ments as that, when the relieving force entered
Kimberley, “fellows went up to smell the Boer
blood on the lances.” Pointed out, also, that in
the Times itself appeared, without unfavorable
comment, an English officer’s letter containing a
reference to the “excellent pig-sticking the
cavalry had enjoyed. A sense of humor, it is
added—if not of self-respect—should have pre-
vented British jubilation about a triumph over a
foe of such inferior strength—attained, too, at a
cost, on the winning side, of at least 40,000 lives
in all, reckoning the casualties of battle and
disease.
AFTER TYPHOID.
WEAK, WEARY AND WORN.
A Complete Resuscitation.
The ease of
MRS. E. S. GLENN.
.{Kepobted fob The Joumcu.)
— &
fa consequence of hearing a few remarks
ftnent the indisposition of a lady residing at
78 Cleveland-street, Sydney, our repre-
sentative called at that address and ex-
pressed a desire to her husband, who
answered the door, to be granted the favor
of an interview with Mrs. Glenn on the
subject. “By all means,” replied Mr. Glenn.
“Just step inside, and Tm sure my wife
will be glad to tell you all about it. The
invitation was accepted, and the erstwhile
patient related her story in the following
manner:— o
\ Some time ago—between two and three
years, it must be—l became conscious of the
fact that an illness of some kind was
creeping on me, almost imperceptibly. It
was quite clear to me that I was becoming
unwell, because of a most unpleasant sense of
general lassitude, a feeling of lack of interest
and absolute indifference to everything that
previously absorbed my attention, a feeling
of unusual languor and depression. I was
always ill at ease, being so restless and dull.
A sensation of drowsiness was continually
upon me, but yet my days and nights were
nearly always sleepless. What little sleep
1 did manage to obtain was not at all refresh-
ing as I would awake just as weary as
before. The work of the day, which hitherto
had been a pleasure to me, gradually became
an aversion. Everything I tried to do
speedily developed itself into a'monotony,
and had the effect of digging out what
little strength remained in my body. I was
altogether exhausted, and the listlessness
and apathy that had seized hold of me was
made more unbearable by the incessant pre-
sence of dull frontal headaches. I became
weaker and weaker through a total loss of
appetite, and lost flesh rapidly. At times
I had a strange ringing sensation in the
ears, and would feel chilly at regular inter-
vals. My state of prostration was now so
complete that the mere action of raising my
head from the pillow produced a feeling of
dizziness and sickness at the stomach. At
other times I lay in a state of utter stupe-
faction, not even being able to engage
myself in thought, and would remain for
long periods gazing into vacancy. I would
arouse myself from this predicament after a
while, but only to realise more clearly the
weak condition I was in.”
You had a doctor’s attention, surely?”
enjoined the reporter.
“Oh, of course ; and my case was pro-
nounced to be one of typhoid fever. Ido
not know whether such a thing is customary
in typhoid fever cases, but I had swellings
of various sizes all over my body. They
caused me no end of anxiety and pain, and I
was afraid of other complications setting in,
although my medical attendant at length
persuaded me there was no reason to be
unnecessarily alarmed. To add to the long
list of other ailments, I must not forget to
tell you that on numerous occasions I was
suddenly made aware of dull, heavy pains in
'the chest, as though it were indigestion ;
but considering that I could not eat I do nob
think it could have been that. Well, thia
state of affairs went on for ten weeks, and
during that time I was just about as bad as
v 'll can imagine a patient could be reduced
1 The doctor’s medicines I drank during
term of sickness would surprise you ;
ou i they evidently did not suit my case.
They made me no stronger, and did nob
remove any of my distressing symptoms.
A.t length I got tired of taking physic, and
iecidea to act upon the advice of a friend,
tvho was continually advocating the ex-
jellenee of Clements Tonic. This gentleman
lequaintance had himself suffered from the
lame 'complaint as my own, and he was so
mthusiastic regarding the amount of benefit
ae had derived from Clements Tonic, and
;he way it restored his lost vitality, that he
convinced me there must be something in its!
preparation which would do me good.
Without any more delay I procured a bottle
and discarded the doctors medicine altr
gether, being quite satisfied that the str J
he had ordered was not doing me an atom ol
good, although his instructions were care-
fully carried out. I had been almost desti-
tute of hope for a thorough recovery from
my long and serious illness, even after the
fever had left me, until the necessary
assistance arrived in the form of Clements
Tonic; and my feelings of gratification can
be more easily imagined than described
when I found the great amount of benefit
which had accrued from the consumption of
the first bottle.”
“ What effect did it have upon you ? ” >
“The most noticeable result of my first
bottle of Clements Tonic was a marked
decrease in the swellings on my body, which
clung to me in spite of my convale--»ouce;
and the agonising pains in my head, jvhich
had been the cause of so much torture and
anguish to me, soon left me altogether, and
I was then in such a happy frame of mind
that my prospects of regaining strength
soon became quite bright. I could also par-
take of the nourishing food which my
enfeebled condition so much required, ana
which for weeks had been returned un-
tasted. And after eating there was an
absence of that dull, heavy feeling on the
stomach which I had previously experienced,
and I knew that my meals were being
properly digested. I continued Clements
Tonic, and I seemed to reap advantage from
each dose. My cheeks began to display a
healthy colour, and each day added more
strength and vigor. By the time I had
arrived at the bottom of the second bottle
the swellings had gone down entirely, and I
was able to go about the house with an
appreciable degree of comfort and energy.
With my daily increasing appetite came an
increase in flesh, and I could now take an
interest in my household arrangements,
having sufficient vitality to do my work
with feelings of mingled interest and
pleasure. When night arrived I could look
forward to have plenty of rest, for I no
longer turned from one side to another in
the vain hope of gaining repose. No ; I could
always feel sure of a good sleep, and would
arise in the morning feeling very much
better for it. ”
“You appear to have made a complete
recovery ? ”
“ Yes; I am thankful to say that
Clements Tonic has restored me to the best
of health, although when I sometimes catch
a chill I get an attack of neuralgia ; but I
always keep to the one remedy, and after
taking two or three doses of Clements Tonic
the neuralgia leaves me.”
“May I make your case known?”
“ Indeed you can, and I trust it may be the
means of alleviating the suffering of other
people.”

By Removing the Impure Matters which Hinder their
Action, KUTNOW’S POWDER Restores Weak and
Congested Kidneys to a Strong, Healthy Condition.
When the stomach, liver, and bowels are deranged
the blood becomes charged with an excess of uric acid
and other waste substances, which the kidneys have to
remove from the system. In this way they act as filters
for eliminating poisonous matters from the blood. Fre-
quently, however, this extra labor causes them to
become weak, disordered, and congested, with the result
that a certain proportion of these substances remains in
the kidneys producing gravel, Bright’s Disease, or other
ailments. Diseases of the kidneys are therefore due to
derangements of the stomach, liver, or bowels, which are
themselves due to carelessness in the matter of food and
drink and unhygienic habits. When the degeneration
of the kidneys has reached a certain point, death must
soon follow ; but, as a matter of fact, it is very seldom
that the kidneys become permanently ruined, as they
are strong enough naturally to last for two hundred
years, and hence it requires a good deal of careless eat-
ing and drinking to injure them beyond repair. Even
when a considerable portion of the kidneys is diseased,
the healthy portion can do satisfactorily the entire work
of the whole.
The proper remedy for kidney diseases is Kutnow’s
Improved Effervescent Carlsbad Powder, which, by
putting the stomach, liver, and bowels into good order,
enables the kidneys to perform their proper functions.
Kutnow’s Powder has such a cleansing, healing, and
strengthening effect on all the digestive and excretory
organs that thousands of sufferers from kidney ailments
whose lives were despaired of have been restored to
complete health by a course of the Powder.
Kutnow’s does not construct new kidneys. It simply
frees them from the impure accumulations which have
clogged them, and it stimulates their action without
irritating them.
If you suffer from gravel, smarting and scalding sen-
sations, excessive uric acid formation, backaches, swell-
ing of the feet, nervousness, cold feet, and other dis-
agreeable accompaniments of kidney and bladder dis-
orders, make free use of Kutnow’s Powder, which can
do no harm, but is certain to do good.
Kutnow’s Powder is a clever combination of the
purifying and curative elements of the most celebrated
mineral-water springs of Europe, whose value in remedy-
ing affections of the kidneys and bladder has -been
recognised for many generations.
“ Kutnow’s ” is palatable and inexpensive, and
entirely free from any drastic or lowering properties.
