Woman Suffrage.
It would indeed be a waste of picturesque matter
if The Bulletin refrained from allowing- “Skotos”
to let off this fine, large picturesque whoop,
despite the fact that the bulk of said whoop will
by no means bear close inspection :
Alleged reform on behalf of woman has drummed about
a third of masculine civilized humanity out of the ranks
of labor. Woman has entered into competition with
man and cut down his living wage, so that he can no
longer indulge in the luxury of a wife. Woman wants to
legislate for the natural legislator. In despair, for having
extinguished the voter, she demands a vote—and, if she
howls long enough and loud enough, she will probably
get it; at least, a section of her will—the faddist section,
the young fool and old maid section, the social-purity and
women’s-temperance-union section, the idle section whose
lack of personal charms debars them from the most en-
thralling of all pursuits—the hunting of big game, of the
biggest of all game, Man.
If all women demanded the franchise, little harm would
be done in giving them the franchise. But the great
majority of women are indifferent, very much more than
that often-talked-of apathetic man who neglects to use
his vote. And, it must be remembered, the most apa-
thetic man does little harm by the non-use of his privi-
lege ; whereas the type of woman who would use it would
do positive and lasting harm ; for the conservatism of
women is the inviolable conservatism of Custom, how-
ever irrational. Democracy will not be served by the
granting of the female franchise. All innovation in legis-
lation is not necessarily democratic. We of late years
have begun to attach a new meaning to the word
“ democracy." We think that it signifies anything
“ novel." The decapitation of octogenarians might be
greeted a democratic measure on grounds just as logical
as womanhood suffrage. Indeed, in a country subject
to clysmic waves of commercial depression, in a country
in which the “ unemployed ” is regarded as a state insti-
tution, not only might legislative sanction to checks to
conception and to the practice of abortion, but even to
actual infanticide, be regarded as democratic measures of
an exceedingly advanced and popular character.
Woman’s subservience to the dictates of ever-changing
fashion will, of course, be advanced by her apologists as
evidence of her progressive outlook. Now, the most
prominent of the women-suffragists are the most dowdy
in dress : their costumes are as antiquated as their social
schemes are crude and impossible. But. to the conten-
tion that woman’s subservience to the dictates of fashion
is evidence of her progressive outlook, may be answered
that she circumscribes her position in the social economy
by “ subservience,” whether to the “ dictates ” of chang-
ing Fashion, age-long Tradition, or ever-present Force.
Her seemingly-rapid adaptation to the whim of the
period does not affect her innermost prejudices. To the
day of her death will she continue to boil potatoes (if she
is not too “ intellectual ” to boil anything) after the
fashion of her grandmother.
On grounds of political expediency, and on grounds of
political expediency alone, can the argument of extend-
ing the franchise to woman be conducted. But woman,
in her advocacy of what shecalls her “ right,” puts under
tribute every conceivable art and science, She appeals
to biology, to history, to authority—and all to her em-
phatic undoing. Having placed herself in an untenable
position, she must be answered according to the logical
conclusions of her self-selected premises.
In the first place woman puts the medico-cwm-historical
argument—viz., that woman’s inferiority to man is cen-
tury-inherited, that it is a matter of social evolution. This
\vould he true if females had no fathers. As a matter of
biological fact the female is more the offspring of the
male than of the female parent. Neither is there neces-
sity to insist on the inferiority of woman. Between her
and man there is a difference of function (Carlyle would
say, a difference of duty) which is purely a matter of
sexual evolution from that remote period when no dif-
ferentiation of sex existed. The stress of environment
and the needs of a scheme of procreation evolved sex,
with the resultant of fruitfulness. The logical extension
of the so-called “rights" of woman would end in the
suppression of the sexual instinct and consequent sterility.
In short, the insistence upon the Saraii-G RAND-advocated
“right” of exercising the functions of maternity is
merely a roundabout method of feminine suicide. Here,
in Australia, we are attaining this delectable condition.
For instance, every N.S. Wales statistical report shows a
lower percentage of births over deaths, notwithstanding
the fact that over 50 per cent, of the total number of first
births are illegitimate or of ante-nuptial conception. Mar-
riage also is growing more distasteful, or more impossible.
Two or more clergymen were, the other day, in a weekly
paper, commenting on the fact that such marriages as did
take place were solemnised between young girls and
elderly men. These marriages between Spring and Mid-
winter were becoming more common. The pietistic inno-
cents complimented the young - womanhood of N.S.
Wales. They were becoming sensible. Pshaw! Young
men cannot afford to marry. They are less able to afford
marriage with every day that passes over their heads.
Girls, therefore, have to barter their bodies with middle-
age ; or, at least, such middle-age as female competition
has permitted to secure a competency. It is an absolute
axiom of political economy : “ That in countries in which
the females, as well as the males, of a family are
compelled to follow gainful pursuits, the earnings of that
family are not higher than in other countries in which no
such necessity exists. All things being equal, the rate of
wages for the man will be the lowest in those industries
in which his wife and children compete for employment.”
Mill, in the 14th chapter of the second book of his
“ Principle of Political Economy,” puts the question in a
nut-shell:—
“ Those trades are generally the worst paid in which the
wife and children of the artizan aid in the work. The in-
come which the habits of the class demand, and down to
which they are almost sure to multiply, is made up, in
these trades, by the earnings of the whole family, while
in others the same income must be obtained by the labor
of the man alone.”
The modern girl, who has ousted the male from em-
ployment, who has usurped his calling, and cut down his
wages, still clings to the tradition of her sex, “ The man
pays ! ” She writes ineffably silly letters to such silly
papers as publish them “ On the Meanness of Young
Men ” —the young men whose businesses in life her com-
petition has ruined, but upon whose purses she still en-
deavors to sponge. She is able to compete against man,
however, only by the splendid chivalry of man which,
like the scent of roses, still clings to the shattered jar.
Thus N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan :
“ Although the wants of woman of the same social
status, for amusements, travelling, and the like, may be
equal to those of the average man, it must be recollected
that they are ordinarily supplied by the male bread-
winner (in relation to the female) —father, friend, brother,
fianc6, and on this account constitute an item in the
normal expenses of the man instead of in those of the
woman.”
_ And the Distaff will continue to beg alms of the Sword
till the crack of doom. Woman can understand subser-
viency to man, superiority to man; but equality with man
—never. She will not forego one miserable privilege of
courtesy which chivalrous tradition has given to her
weakness ; but she wants all the privileges also deducible
from an argument based on her abstract “ rights.”
Woman demands a vote ! Now the extension of the
franchise to females means allowing her a voice in the
determination of great public expenditure—and she does
not know where to bestow her own purse. She carries it
in her hand for thieves to snatch at, or in a ridiculously-
placed pocket which even she herself cannot exploit—
though the light-fingered can.
Why should woman have separate representation ? The
majority of the members of every parliament in the
civilised world are married men. It is to their interest
and welfare to safeguard the best interests and welfare
of all women. No Act affecting anything human can
exclude women. The writer ventures to assert that the
broad and liberal divorce laws of most civilised countries
would never have been carried by a legislature controlled
by the female vote. Womanhood suffrage is a step
towards the accomplishment of absolute sexual license.
August Berel, woman’s great champion, makes no
no mystery of this “ ideal ” :
“ In the choice of love she is free just as man is free.
She wooes and is wooed, and has no other inducement to
bind herself than her own free will.”
In fact, this alleged reform is always patted on the
back by young bachelors who have anything to gain from
its accomplishment without the shouldering of any
responsibility, and by old men who have nothing to gain
save a little notoriety to serve as a torch to light them to
the grave, and only the dead shucks of past responsibility.
Woman’s appeal to biology is ridiculous in its shallow
absurdity. She assumes a female culture, undisturbed
in its protoplasmic succession, since the first germ of life
fermented in a fecundating sun-ray. Now, the pre-
dominant female gives to the world men. In Sparta,
woman was almost supreme —but not quite. Gorgo, the
wife of Leonidas, was addressed by a woman of another
country in these words : “You of Lacedaemon are the
only women in the world that rule the men.” And Gorgo
replied, “ We are the only women that bring forth men.”
The predominant male gives to the world women. The
girl is the biggest sharer in the father’s attributes, as the
boy is in those of the mother ; and thus Nature keeps her
balance of sex. If there is a difference between the sexes
it is plainly a difference of Nature’s making.
Some of the controversialists go to the biological
museum, and come thence laden with a store of facts con-
cerning some very low forms of aphidce and other insect
pests. They appeal to the republic of bees—among other
things. They should remember that female sex can be
produced in bees, or in frogs, and in a number of reptilia
and insectivora, by a simple means—feeding. This is a
reversal application of the gibe at man, “Feed the brute!”
Bees, moreover, are sexless —save the drones, who are all
murdered by the eunuchs of the palace after one of their
number has served his purpose, and the queen—the
queen-bee wears her coronet only on condition that she
lays eggs.
“ There is no sex in Intellect! ” Another absurdity.
Women themselves speak of one of their number, say a
George Eliot, as having a masculine mind. Very true,
but masculine mind as it was, in undoubted female body,
it fell far short of the greatest masculine minds in male
bodies. There is so much sex in intellect that it may be
boldly asserted that never was there a woman whose
alleged womanly function in art, in in learning,
or even in cookery, could not be, has not been, more
successfully discharged by man. Woman cannot argue
logically. She says, “ What! give that illiterate laborer
a vote, and deny it to Mary Somerville 1 ” Eheu 1 Are
there not some illiterate draggle-tails of her own sex
wherewith to compare the illiterate laborer, and who is
Mary Somerville when Lord Kelvin is mentioned ? Then
they complain, “ We never have a chance.” Sappho had
her chance, and took it, in days when the intellectual (us-
abilities of women were genuine grievances. In woman-
oppressed China the whole of armed Europe has been
held at bay by one pig-headed woman. Great women are
the exceptions ! And they are not so great as their male
compeers. Where are the female Homers, Dantes,
Shakespeares, Goethes? A scroll of famous names
would vainly challenge female corellants ! In all the arts
woman occupies second place, save in one alone—
the art of acting, which, as a sex, she uses
both as sword and buckler. All this is said sans
disrespect. It is a matter of fact, not a matter of
opinion. Now and then, woman has essayed an expres-
sion of her intellectual greatness in something she has
labelled “ emancipated” literature. A few years ago some
women who aped masculinity, and a few men with
feminine intellects, deluged the bookstalls and the stage
with plague-saturated filth. Even women scouted it—
and, be it understood, writer is referring now, not to
women as a sex, but to the section who aspire to what
they fairly call “ predominancy ” over mere males.
Be it noted, the advanced woman generally paints her
own sex as angels ; the opposite, as devils. There is to
be no truce, no quarter, no prisoners are to be taken.
They call a meeting, and a “ Man ” presides over it. They
write books, and sign them with the names of men.
George Eliot, a woman of masculine intellect, could, how-
ever, not deceive everyone. There was a flaw in her
armour of proof, which so practised a reader of the human
heart as Charles Dickens discovered at once.
A house that is divided against itself cannot last, and
the home is the political unit. But nobody need worry !
The home will continue to be the political unit. But the
women who live outside “ homes” are deeply to be com-
miserated, should women gain their point. There are
wives enough, and to spare, who are built on the broad,
natural plan, and who will follow man’s lead in war as in
love. Every married man who is married to such a wife
will have hia voting power doubled; and, if he is a
wise man, with grown-up daughters sensibly trained,
trebled, quadrupled, what not ! And the Labor
Party may be trusted, and the Labor Party’s
wife may be trusted to fight with the Female Franchise
as a falchion to the bitter death. But the trouble
looms in the future. Marriage is decreasing at such an
ominous speed, the birth-rate of the Australian race
is diminishing so fast, that verily the sexes threaten to
fall into two hostile camps, with opposite interests, and
conflicting aspirations. War may, indeed, be a blessing
in disguise. If men are wanted in sufficient numbers to
fight, they can well afford to hand over all the arts of
peace to their womankind, including the art of govern-
ment ; for woman’s self-interest would impel her to take
the strictest care of her defender.
It is emphatically inexpedient to confer on woman the
franchise. Honorary members of all societies or associa-
tions are not allowed to vote. Woman is the honorary
member of the community. She is not permitted a seat
in the legislature, therefore she should be debarred from
voting to send someone else there —a traitor, moreover, a
suborned member of the enemy sex.
The chief medical officer of the Indian
Army advocates anti-typhoid (enteric) inocula-
tion. Asserts that “inoculation insures an im-
munity equal to or greater than that which
accrues to a person who undergoes and recovers
from an attack of the disease.” The operation is
said to cause no risk to health. Experiments were
made and 2000 men treated successfully—accord-
ing to above authority. Prof. Haffkine also advo-
cates very vigorously anti-cholera inoculation and
gives a long array of striking figures in support of
his theory.
A trooper, writing from S’Africa: “I
have seen a lot of the Australian Bushmen over
here. They are champions to fight. They are
called Australian Butchers ! ” And Lord Roberts
is reported at second-hand to have said that the
Australians were “ too cruel.” Well, a recently-
printed letter in Sydney Daily Telegraph
(written to the swaddy’s mother) means either
callous brutality or crass youthful thoughtless-
ness :
Burning farms is rather an exciting pastime. You ride
up and tell the people to remove all their things, as you
propose burning their residence. The women and chil-
dren all start howling at once. There is no time to lose,
however; all hands start carting out the things and a fire
is soon going. It is necessary to keep away from the
burning place, as the roof and ceilings are all full of cart-
ridges, which explode as soon as the fire gets to them,
and if you are not careful the women are not above
taking a rifle and having a shot in the general scramble.
“Pastime,” indeed!
Cabled that the Russian liquor traffic has
been nationalised. Russia is far ahead of Australia
in this matter. State liquor control has been in
force for years over a large area of the Empire,
and apparently the experiment has succeeded so
well as to encourage the Government to
apply it all round. Liquor is made in private
distilleries under direct control and supervision of
Govt., which also fixes the price, basing its esti-
mate on cost of material and of production. No
spirit is allowed on the market till properly
matured and rectified, and it is sold in sealed
bottles bearing labels setting forth price, strength,
and quantity. When State control was first
inaugurated the Little Father started to reduce
drunkenness in no half-hearted fashion. In St.
Petersburg, out of 937 taverns 759 were closed,
and 400 out of G5O hotels, all without compensa-
tion, while in smaller towns the closure was
applied even more drastically. As a consequence,
drunkenness has immensely decreased. Also,
when the Russ does get drunk, he does so on pure
spirit.

The Kidneys are the most important organs of
the body because they filter the blood .
The statement given below was made by a resi-
dent of Leichhardt, this city. If the same state-
ment had been made by some person in another
town or country it might, of course, be true, but
we would never know, because we couldn't prove
it. But we can prove this statement, for the lady
who gives it lives right here in Sydney.
Mrs. Pat. Duffy, 43 Marion-street, Leichhardt,
this city, says:—“Twelve months ago I took
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills for pain in the
back, which I knew to arise from the kidneys.
The doctor said that I could expect it owing
to the change of life. I had been taking
various remedies (both internal and exter-
nal) without getting any benefit. Only a few doses
of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills released me
from all my pain, however.
“My husband had been suffering from kidney
troubles, and, seeing how splendidly they acted in
my case, he took the same remedy. He works
for the council, at asphalting, and is out in all
kinds of weather. The pains in his back were
extremely bad. He had been unable to get more
than a passing relief out of anything he had pre-
viously tried, but Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills
cured him. Since being cured he was out work-
ing in the rain, and he caught a cold in his kid-
neys, and so he took more of the pills. They are
first-rate kidney pills, and he says that nothing
ever did him as much good as Doan’s Pills. I
procured these pills at E. Newman’s pharmacy, in
King-street.”
This is only one instance of what Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills are doing in Sydney. There
are hundreds of similar cases equally as strong
and equally as convincing, and they are all Sydney
people. For instance : Mrs. J. M‘Adam, 40 Ken-
sington-street, Chippendale, Sydney; Mr. W.
Hibberd, St. Paul-street, Randwick, Sydney;
Mr. W. Lane, 102 George-street, Erslcineville,
Sydney; Mr. J. Cameron, 297 Balmain-road>
Leichhardt, Sydney; Mr. Jas. Rees, 6 East-
street, Surry-hills, Sydney ; Mrs. Spear, 32 Good-
sell street, St. Peters, Sydney;' and scores of
others.
As we hear of no other medicines curing Sydney
people like this, why are Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills so successful here ?. We will tell you
why.
Doctors used to think that only urinary troubles
were to be traced to the kidneys.
Modem science has proved, however, that
really all constitutional diseases have their be-
ginnings in disorders of these important organs.
And this is common sense, for the kidneys filter
and purify the blood. When they do not the
whole body suffers.
When the kidneys are ill, impurities permeate
the blood, muscles, bones and nerves, like foul
vapours from a marsh. They saturate every pore.
There is a dull pain in the back, rheumatism,
dizziness, headaches, nervousness, heart trouble,
pains in the limbs, bloating, sallow complexion,
circles under the eyes, bladder irritation, sup-
pression of the urine, worn out, tired feeling, lack,
of ambition.
Our readers will be interested to see the picture
of James Doan, the discoverer of this famous
medicine, which we publish at the head of this
article. He is the noted chemist of Kingsville,
Ontario, and his discovery, spoken of above, has
made his name known all over the world.
Remember it was Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills which Mrs. Duffy used. See that you get
the same. You may always know the genuine,
because the wrapper bears the full name, Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills, in bold type, and has the
signature of James Doan. You need never be
imposed upon if you will bear these two things in
mind.

The Ballad of John Bigloy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Bigley, of Giles, Smith and Co.,
Worked from dull morn till day was dim,
And hoped that God would soon put trust
In one who trusted Him.
A wife and child were Bigley’s care;
“ One-ten ” a week was Bigley’s screw;
The foreman was a princely man
And gorged on sovereigns two.
A godly man was Bigley known
As ever drew teetotal breath ;
Though stood he next for Johnson’s place
He wished not for his death,
But merely felt a gladness thrill
His pulse when Johnson’s step moved slow,
Or when the dancing sunbeams showed
Hair nearer to the snow.
One-ten a week was Bigley’s screw,
And one-third more would give the wife
A rush of welcome mountain air,
And with it years of life;
And thus, the destination Death,
There chanced a race between these two—
John Bigley’s feeble wife and him
Who owned the princely screw.
The man who staked his all on one
Walked ’neath the cliff’s all-towering height,
Close where the darkening billows rolled,
Foaming to angry white.
He felt the storm upon his cheek,
And heard unawed its rising roar,
When far apart the foreman moved,
Pacing like him the shore.
What brought him there that dreary day?
What mind-distraction need he seek ?
Perchance griefs visit even those
Who earn two pounds a week 1
The foreman paused, when Bigley’s eyes,
Bent idly on the cliff above,
Saw beetling o’er the waiting man
A trembling boulder move,
Pocked in the rising tempest’s breath.
Then Bigley thought to speak, when dim —
For once linked with the powers of hell—
His wife’s face rose to him,
Sad-eyed and sunken-cheeked ; not such
As years agone he fondly kissed.
Then Bigley murmured o'er a text
From some evangelist
And sent abroad a straining cry.
(The rising storm the warning drowned]);
Then ran with sudden flying feet
That spurned the uneven ground.
And knew ’twas but a moment’s race
Between him and the next sea-breath.
Which with the quivering rock must bring
To him beneath it death.
With all his body’s strength he tried
That man to reach, but vainly tried.
The moment came . . the storm wailed loud .
“Thank God !” the husband cried.
There is a wife of happy eyes
And cheeks sun-browned in mountain air
Who murmurs of a Providence,
And One who answers prayer.
There is a man of kindly heart
And godly life as ever trod
This earth, who nightly pardon asks
Of God—for thanking God !
N.S.W.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
Our New Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.—LEAVING SHINGLE HUT.
A Change had come to Shined© Hut. An air of
prosperity was about it. Broad-backed, up-
standing draught-horses, fat and fresh, fed about
the place; the paddock was stocked with sleek,
well-bred cows and spring heifers, and four
and five-year old bullocks fit for a show; the
reaper-and-binder in the shed was all our own;
two ploughs were going, and —ye money-lenders !
—the mortgage was paid right off.
For six successive years our wheat crop had
been a big success. No matter what Dad did lie
couldn’t go wrong. Whenever he was compelled
to sow late there was sure to be too much rain and',
early crops would run rank, or take the rust or the
smut or something, whileourswould come on nicely
and be a success; or else no rain at all would
fall—somehow it would wait for Dad—and when
Anderson’s and Johnson’s and all the wheat about ;
was parched and perished ours was a picture good
to see. And Dad earned and enjoyed a reputation
for long-headedness, for persistence and practical
farming; people praised him and pointed to him
as a pattern for their sons to follow—an example
of what could be accomplished on the land by
industry and a bit of brains.
Yet Dad wasn’t satisfied. He talked of selling
out—of taking up a thousand acres somewhere
and expanding operations. But mother opposed
it; she thought we were doing well enough.
Shingle Hut was good enough for her ; she had
worked hard and spent the best of her days in it,
scraping and struggling, and all she asked for now
was to live the rest of her life there —to die peace-
fully and be buried near the house.
The rest of us agreed with Dad. We wanted a.
change; what the result might be we didn’t con-
sider—we only wished to shift. Ripping up the
old house -rounding up the stock—camping under-
a dray a night or two on the way to the Promised
Land—this gave food for delightful speculation..
We longed for it all to come about in reality.
* *
“See anything of him?” Dad asked, as Dave
rode into the yard and dismounted, after searching,
for a lost horse one day.
“ No,” Dave said, and leaned on the fence, and
nibbled the end of a straw. Dad leaned on the’
other side and reflected.
A short distance off a new building was going
up. Donald MTntosh, a broad-shouldered Scotch-
man with a passion for politics, and oneM‘Donald,
who didn’t believe in governments at all and
confined his studies solely to the weather and
fjumpkins, were building a humpy with a shing-
ing-hammer. Donald MTntosh was on the
rafters, arguing wildly and shaking the hammer
menacingly at McDonald, who was on the ground.
Dad and Dave looked up.
“Come doon, ye ! -—!” shouted'
M‘Donald, and MTntosh sprang from the rafters
and pursued him round and round the humpy.
‘‘ A fight / ” Dave said, excitedly; “ come on / ”
and ran a few paces. But at the same moment
Joe rushed on the scene out of breath.
“ I fuf—fuf—found ’im, Dad ! ” he said, ex-
citedly.
“ When ! ” Dad asked, eagerly.
“ Bub -bub —bet y’ can’t gug—guess ?”
“ Where th’ devil is he, boy ? ”
(“Look at MTntosh ! —after him across the
ploughed ground ! ” enthusiastically from Dave.)
“Down th’ w—w — well.”
“ Wat?" Dad hissed, showing his teeth and
punching the wind with his fist.
“ Is he dead ? ” he added.
“ Don’t know th — that, Dad ; but he s—s—
st —inks / ”
Dad groaned, and walked inside and out again,
then round the yard.
“Yoke up Gypsy and Tiger,” he said sternly to
Dave, “and bring them down.” Then he went
off to the well by himself.
Dad peered into the well a moment, and drew
back and pulled a very ugly face.
Dave arrived with the horses. He went to the
well to look down, too, but ran away and spat.
Joe held the horses and chuckled.
Dave thought of the wind. It was blowing
towards him. He made a wide circuit and ap-
proached the well on the other side. But when
he leaned forward to look down, the wind changed
and he ran away again. But Dad was deter-
mined. He advanced with a frown and a heavy
rope ; fastened one end of the rope to a sapling
close by, and hurled the rest of it into the well—
then he, too, retreated.
“Now,” he said to Dave, “go down and fasten
it on ’im ! ”
“ Me 1 ” Dave said, backing further away.
Joe chuckled again.
“ Well, y’ don’t expect me to go down, do y’ ? ”
Dad snorted.
Dave grinned a sickly grin.
“It won’t kill y’, will it ?” Then, after a pause,
“ Are y’ going t’ do it—or not ? ” There were
shell and shrapnel in Dad’s eye, and he looked
ugly.
“W-w -wet y’r nose, Dave,” Joe said, ad-
visedly.
Dave hesitated, then reluctantly descended.
He disappeared along the rope and was below
some time. He came to the surface again and
gasped and staggered and threw himself on the
frass and seemed ill. Change of air didn’t do
)ave much good.
Dad fastened the horses to the rope and
told them to “get up.” The chains jerked and
tightened. Gypsy and Tiger hung in their collars
and strained, and tore the ground with their toes.
Dad shouted and waved a big stick over them.
Captain’s form gradually rose till his head was in
sight and his nose caught against the sleepers that
lined the mouth of the well; then he stopped.
“ Gypsy I . . . Tiger ! ” Dad roared, and rattled
the stick encouragingly on their hides. Gypsy
and Tiger began to tire and eased off.
“Look out!” Dave cried, “don’t let them
back/” Dad seized their heads and held on.
But the dead horse gradually descended again and
was slowly dragging the live ones and Dad after
it. Dad struck Gypsy on the nose with his fist to
make her “stand up.” Gypsy reared and fell
across the well and kicked desperately. Then
Tiger tried to turn round in his chains and lost
his footing and lay on his back, his tail hanging
down the well. Dad was horror-stricken. He
threw away his hat and ran in several directions
in search of something. He found a sapling,
lifted it, threw it down again and ran back to the
horses and held his hands above his head like a
preacher.
Sal came to tell Dad he was wanted at the
house, but he wouldn’t hear her.
“ Curse it —can’t y’ do something /” he cried to
Dave.
Then Gypsy made a big effort to rise and fell
■down the well and dragged Tiger’s harness with
her. Tiger jumped up and made-off. Dad stared
aghast.
“W-w-wot did y’ hit ’er F?” Joe asked, re-
proachfully.
“ Yah/” Dad bellowed, and sprang at Joe.
•Joe didn’t look behind till he reached the house. _
But Burton happened to come along with his
Bullock-team and rescued Gypsy and dragged
'Captain’s corpse from the well. Then Dad went
for a drink of water in the gully and sat down
under a bush, and Sal came and spoke to him
again, and when he was calm he went to the
house.
* * * *
The man from town who had offered us £4OO
for the selection was at the house waiting. They
went inside.
“ Well,” said the visitor, “ have you considered
imy offer?”
“Yes,” Dad answered, “I’ll take it!” —and
Bhingle Hut was sold !
Mother clutched her knees with both hands and
istared hard and silently at the fire-place till her
•eyes filled with tears.
Sal ran out to Dave and Joe, and the three of
them dilated on the turn things had taken.
Mother came out to them.
“ It’s sold? ” Sal said.
“Yes!” mother replied slowly, “ it’s— s-sold ! ”
And again the tears came, and she sat on a sleeper
beside the barn, and hiding her face in her apron,
•cried hard.
Sal hung her head and thought; but Dave went
to mother and sat beside her and tried to explain
the advantages of selling-out and beginning a-
fresh, and the man left the house and walked to
his horse and shook hands again with Dad and
went away.
Then Dad paced up and down, up and down,
and round about by himself for a long, long time.
* * * *
A cold, dull day. Heavy, black clouds hung
low and darkened the earth. At intervals a few
•drops of rain fell—a deluge threatened. No gentle
winds blew ; no birds whistled among the boughs.
Dave passing slowly out at the slip-rails with a
dray-load of furniture and farm-implements; Joe
sitting astride Dad’s old saddle-mare, in charge
•of the cows in the lane; Dad loading a second
dray ; Sal putting a horse in the spring-cart; the
rest of us gathering nick-nacks and things about
the place. We were leaving—leaving Shingle
Hut, the old house we. had known so long—the
■old home where Kate was married—where Bill
and Tom and Bert were born —the home where
merriment so often mocked misfortune, and light
hearts and hope shut out the gloom of reverses
and mellowed the harshness of adversity.
And Anderson and Mrs. Anderson came to see
us off ; kind-hearted people were the Andersons !
And Judy Jubb came all the way from Prosperity
Peak to kiss Sal.
“Good-bye, then, and God bless y' /” Mrs. An-
derson said, her large eyes swimming in tears.
Mother held out her hand, but broke down, and
was helped into the spring-cart.
“ ’N’-I hope y’ won’t regret it! ” Anderson said,
as he shook Dad’s hand. Then with a last look
around—a look of lingering affection—we bade
farewell to Shingle Hut and started for “Our
New Selection.”
